And the author of this book, upon completing for you, my children, this book which is a part of medical knowledge in its entirety and reaching the utmost clarity and explanation within it, saw fit to complete it for you with this chapter which is a part of practical work by hand.
For practical work by hand is refined in our country and in our time completely absent, to the point that its science is almost studied and its trace is cut off. And what remains of it are slight illustrations in the books of the early scholars, which have been corrupted by hands and plagued by errors and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its benefits distant.
So I saw fit to revive it and compose this chapter in the manner of explanation, clarification, and abbreviation, and to provide illustrations of cautery blades and all other surgical instruments, as it is an enhancement of clarity and a necessity for what is required.
The reason there is no skilled craftsman in our time is because the practice of medicine is long, and its practitioner should first train in the science of anatomy, which Galen described. Until one becomes acquainted with the benefits of the organs, their forms, their temperaments, their connections and disconnections, the knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles, their number, their origins, and the positions of arteries, veins, and their origins.
For this reason, Hippocrates said that there are many physicians in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of the hand. We have mentioned a part of this in the introduction of this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have mentioned regarding anatomy is likely to make a mistake that could kill people, as I have witnessed many who have presumed in this science and claimed it without knowledge or understanding.
That is because I saw an ignorant doctor who incised a goiter in a woman's neck, thus cutting some of the neck arteries, causing the woman to bleed until she fell dead before him.
And I saw another doctor who attempted to remove a stone from an elderly man, and since the stone was large, he took a risk and removed it along with a piece of the bladder. The man died around three days later, and I was called to remove it, and I saw from the stone's hardness and the patient's condition what led me to that decision.
And I saw another doctor who was hired by some of the commanders in our city to practice medicine.
A young black boy under his care had a fracture in his leg near the ankle with a wound, so the doctor, out of ignorance, quickly bound the fracture tightly over the wound with bandages and splints without leaving room for the wound to breathe.
Then he let him follow his desires, leaving him for days and ordering him not to loosen the bindings until his leg and foot swelled, and he was on the verge of death.
I was called to him, so I quickly loosened the bindings, and he gained relief and was alleviated from his pains, but the corruption had already taken hold in the limb, and I could not prevent it.
The corruption continued to spread in the limb until he perished.
And I saw another doctor who cut a cancerous tumor, which ulcerated after days until the sufferer's condition greatly worsened. This is because if the cancer is purely of a black mixture, it should not be treated with surgery at all.
Unless it is in a limb that could potentially be amputated entirely.
And for this, my son, you should know that work with the hand is divided into two parts: work that accompanies safety and work that brings damage in most cases.
And I have warned in every place that comes from this book about the work that involves risk and fear; you should be wary of it and reject it.
So that the ignorant may not find a way to criticize or slander, take for yourselves firmness and caution, and for your patients, gentleness and thoroughness.
And use the best method leading to safety and a praiseworthy outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure diseases.
And purify yourselves from what you fear may bring doubt into your religion and worldly affairs, for it is more lasting for your honor and exalted in this world and the next for your station.
For Galen said in one of his advices: Do not treat bad diseases and thus be called bad doctors.
And I have divided this article into three chapters.
The first chapter.
On cauterization with fire and cauterization with sharp medication, organized and categorized from head to foot, including images of the tools and cauterizing blades, and everything needed for hand work.
And the second chapter.
On incisions, cautery, bloodletting, and surgeries, including the extraction of arrows and similar procedures, all are arranged and ordered with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third chapter.
On setting fractures, dislocations, treating rheumatism, and similar conditions, arranged and ordered from head to foot with illustrations of the instruments.
The first chapter.
On cauterization.
And before we mention working with it, it is necessary to mention the manner of its benefits and harms and in what temperament it is used.
I say that the discussion on the manner of the benefit of cauterization and its harms is a long discourse, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and many wise people have spoken about it.
They differed on this, and I have condensed their discussion to avoid lengthiness.
I say that cauterization generally benefits all bad temperaments, whether with matter or without matter, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without matter and the dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, they differed on it. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, while others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for a disease caused by heat and dryness because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to cure a hot and dry disease with a hot and dry treatment. Those who argue the opposite say that cauterization with fire may be beneficial for a hot and dry disease that occurs in people's bodies. For when you add the body of the human and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you affect the body of the human with cold.
And I say by his saying because experience has revealed that to me many times, but it should not be imagined that this matter is for anyone except those who have been trained and practiced in the art of cauterization to a great extent. And he observed the differences in people's temperaments and the state of diseases in themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and the duration of time.
And as for the other temperaments, there is no need for you to fear them, especially the cold and moist diseases, as all physicians agree on them and do not differ about the benefit of cauterization in them.
And know, O my sons, that one of the secrets of treatment is cauterization by fire and its superiority over cauterization with burning medicine because fire is a single essence whose effect does not extend beyond the organ which was cauterized and does not harm another connected organ except with minimal damage.
And cauterization with burning medicine may extend its effect to other organs and may cause a disease in the organ that is difficult to treat and may even kill. But fire, due to its nobility and the generosity of its essence, does not do this unless it is excessive.
And this has become clear to us through experience due to the length of service, attention to the craft, and understanding the realities of the matter.
And for this reason, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse, but if it were not inappropriate for my book, I would present to you a mysterious secret about fire and its manner of action on bodies and its removal of diseases with philosophical and demonstrative speech that penetrates your understanding.
And know, O sons, that they have differed regarding the time in which cauterization is suitable, and they have made the best time the season of spring.
And I say that cauterization may be suitable at all times, because the harm caused by the time is overshadowed by the benefit brought about by cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for severe, necessary pains that are dangerous and cannot tolerate delay, for what is feared from it is a calamity that is greater than the slight harm caused by the time.
And do not let what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine come to your minds, O sonsâ€”that cauterization which cures a certain disease will never return, and they consider it perfect.
But it is not as they imagine, for cauterization is like medicine that alters the temperament and dries the humidities which are the cause of pains.
However, cauterization is preferred over medicine for the speed of its success, the strength of its effect, and the severity of its power.
And the disease may return at some time depending on the temperament of the patient, the establishment and strength of his disease, and what accumulates in his body from excesses and neglecting himself in acquiring them from foods and other such causes.
Except that the disease in which cauterization is used is a mild disease and in an organ with little excess and humidity. Such as extracting the tooth from pain and the like, for it may be that such pain will not return, and this is the least.
And as for the common people's saying that cauterization is the last of medicine, it is a correct statement not according to what they intend, because they believe that no treatment with medication or otherwise avails after cauterization, but the matter is contrary to that.
The meaning of cauterization being the last of medicine is that when we use various treatments for a disease and those medicines are ineffective, then we use cauterization as the final option, and it succeeds. Thus, the saying "cauterization is the last of medicine" comes from this context. Not in the sense that the common people and many ignorant doctors have understood it.
The early scholars mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron. They said this because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence. They also said that the site of cauterization does not suppurate, but this is not always the case.
For I have tested this and found that it only occurs in some bodies and not others. Cauterization with gold is indeed better and more preferable than with iron, as they said. However, when you heat the cautery in the fire, it is not clear when it has reached the desired heat due to the redness of the gold.
Because it quickly cools down, and if you increase the heat, it melts in the fire and becomes liquid, causing the craftsman to encounter difficulties. Therefore, cauterization with iron has become faster and more accurate for practical use, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections organized from head to foot to make it easier for the student to find what he desires, God willing.
The first chapter.
Cauterization of the head is done once.
This cauterization is beneficial for overcoming the excess moisture and cold in the brain, which are causes of headache, frequent discharges from the head to the area of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, toothache, and throat pain. In general, for every disease that occurs due to cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, stroke, and other similar diseases.
The procedure for this cauterization is to first order the patient to purge with a mild, brain-cleansing medicine for three or four nights, according to what is required by the strength of the patient, their age, and their constitution.
Then, you order him to shave his head with a razor. Then, sit him in front of you, cross-legged, with his hands on his chest.
Then, place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes. Where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then, heat the olive-shaped cautery, which looks like this.
If the bone becomes exposed to the size of a clove head or the size of a coriander seed, lift your hand. Otherwise, reapply with the same cautery iron or another if it has cooled. Until you see what I have mentioned of the bone, then take some salt and dissolve it in water and soak cotton in it and place it on the area and leave it for three days.
Then apply cotton soaked in fat on it and leave it until the inflammation subsides, then treat it with the four-part ointment until it heals, if God wills.
And they have said that the wound is better and more beneficial the longer it remains open to drain pus.
And some have mentioned that the skin should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery iron should be held until part of the thickness of the bone is burned. Then what has been burned from the bone should be removed and then treated.
And others have said that it is necessary to heavily cauterize until it strongly affects the bone, causing pieces like the size of a carat or a small coin to fall from the bone. They claimed that it releases vapors from the head in that area and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then it should be treated until it heals. I do not see these types of cauterization at all except in some people and as a risky method, and leaving them is better for me. If safety is ensured, then the head weakens whenever its natural connection is disrupted, as we have observed with other organs.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and better for me, and I use it. Follow it and you will be safe, if God wills.
Chapter Two.
Surgery of the head also.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head region and persists for a long time with the patient having used compresses, decoctions, fumigations, ointments, and dressings, especially if cauterization with the single cautery we described has been applied and none of these treatments have been beneficial, then examine. If the patient's head is inherently strong in structure and not weak, and he feels a severe coldness, cauterize with another cautery slightly above the previous one. Then cauterize each temple of his head with a cautery until the thickness of the skin is reduced and the amount of bone exposed as we described. Cauterize with a cautery at the back of his head in the area known as the axe, but be gentle and do not expose the bone, for the patient will feel severe pain from this, more than from all other head cauterizations. I will mention this cautery in its proper place.
The cautery used for the temples and the back of the head should be milder than the cautery used for the center of the head. This is its description.
Chapter Three.
Surgery for non-chronic migraine.
If pain accompanied by headache occurs on one side of the head and extends to the eye, make the patient vomit with medicines that cleanse the head and use all the treatments I mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If this is not effective, then cauterization should be applied in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with burning medicine, it is to take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides. Then, incise the site of pain on the cheek with a broad lancet until there is a space under the skin large enough to hold the clove.
It is inserted under the skin until it disappears, then tightly bandage it with good binding and leave it for about fifteen hours. Then release it and remove the garlic, leave the wound for two or three days, then proceed.
Place cotton soaked in fat until the area pusces, then treat it with ointment until it heals, if God wills. If you prefer, you can do this with some of the burning medicines that I provided in the eighteenth article on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is done in this manner: heat the cautery iron which has this shape:.
It is called the nail-shaped cautery because its head resembles a nail, with some roughening, and in the middle, there is a small protrusion.
Then place it on the site of pain and hold your hand as you rotate the iron slightly, and the amount by which you burn through the skin should be about half its thickness. Lift your hand to avoid burning the artery below, which could cause bleeding. Then soak some cotton in salt water and place it on the area and leave it for three days. Then soak the cotton in fat, then treat it with the ointment until it heals, if God wills.
And if you wish, cauterize this sinus with the blade end of the cautery tool, and be careful not to cut the artery in this non-chronic sinus especially.
Chapter Four.
On Cauterizing the Chronic Sinus.
If you treat the sinus with the aforementioned treatments and what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and you see in the condition what cannot be managed by the initial cauterization with medication or fire as we described. You should heat the blade cautery until it becomes white, then apply it to the site of pain in a line about half a finger long or so. Keep your hand steady once and press until you cut the artery and reach near the bone.
However, you should be careful not to affect the jaw that moves during chewing, as burning the muscle or nerve that moves it can cause spasms.
Be cautious and wary of the bleeding from the artery you have cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for those who do not know what they are doing and have not tried a proven method, it is better to refrain from the procedure.
The management of incidental bleeding from the artery will be discussed in its place in the book, if God wills. If you see in the condition what cannot be managed by this cauterization.
I saw the body of the patient able to bear cauterization in the middle of the head as we described and treated the wound until it heals, if God wills.
And if you wish, use the cautery we mentioned in the chapter on arterial forceps with the two-bladed iron, for it is a better and more effective cautery than this one.
Chapter Five.
On cauterizing the pains of the ears.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other treatments we mentioned in the classification, but the pain does not subside, heat the cautery known as the point, which has this shape.
Then apply it around the entire ear after heating it, as you circle around, or around both ears if the pain is in both. And keep the cautery a little away from the base of the ear after marking the area with ink. The cautery should be about ten points in each ear or so. Then treat the areas until they heal, if God wills.
Chapter Six.
On cauterizing the loins.
The loins that are treated with cautery are of the type caused by phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
Cauterization should be avoided for the type caused by dryness and nerve spasms.
If you have treated this type of loins with compresses, fumigations, and gargles but your treatment has not been effective, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauteries. One is at the base of the ear, the second slightly below his temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cautery on the opposite side of the affected area because the relaxation occurs in the side that appears healthy.
The image of the cautery is to cauterize it slightly below the upper part of the ear under the temple and another one at the temple, and its length should be equal to the length of the thumb.
Lower your cautery until it burns to about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cauterizing tool, which is a type of scalpel that has been previously described, but it is slightly finer as you can see. The knife should be slightly thicker, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Seven.
On the cautery of chronic stroke.
If the stroke persists and the treatments we mentioned do not work, and there is no fever in the patient, cauterize it with four cauteries. Place one cautery on each temple of his head, one in the middle of the head as mentioned, and a cautery at the back of the head as previously described.
The description of the cautery is as previously stated. It may also be cauterized at the mouth of the stomach, which can be more effective, then treat it as previously described.
Chapter Eight.
In order to forget.
That which is caused by phlegm should first be treated by giving the patient large decoctions and purified seeds for the brain.
Then, his entire head should be shaved, and a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, should be applied to the back of his head several times. This is a form of cauterization.
Do this in the same manner as I described there, for he will be healed by it. If not, cauterize him three times at the back of his head, arranged from the top of the head to below the neck.
Make the interval between each cauterization the width of a finger. Then treat the cauterization as previously described. If you wish to increase the treatment and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle area again.
If you wish to increase the treatment, cauterize him on the temples, then treat him until he is healed. The cautery should be olive-shaped as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On the cauterization for hemiplegia and the relaxation of the entire body.
It is necessary to proceed with purifying the head with decoctions as we have mentioned. Then, shave the patient's head and cauterize it with one cautery in the center of the head, one on each temple, and one at the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If you need more than that for a condition of body laxity and the patient can tolerate it, and the disease is strong and firmly established, then apply four cauterizations on the vertebrae of his back. And continue with the cauterization until it burns most of the skin, then raise your hand. Then treat it as previously mentioned until it heals, if God wills, and the cautery iron should be olive-shaped.
The Tenth Chapter.
On the cauterization of epilepsy.
Only the epileptic who has epilepsy due to phlegm should be cauterized. It is appropriate to first cleanse his brain with large electuaries and all other treatments that we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and can tolerate taking medicines.
If it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then use gargles and cleansing mouthwashes to purify the brain for many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then shave his head, then cauterize one spot in the center of the head as previously described, and another cauterization at the back. And on each temple of his head, apply a cauterization. If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then apply the cauterizations mentioned for paralysis and body laxity on the vertebrae of the neck and back. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped as previously described.
If the patient is a child, then make the cautery iron gentle in this form.
Chapter Eleven.
On curing melancholy.
If the cause of melancholy is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, apply the cauterizations we mentioned in the treatment of paralysis.
If the cause of melancholy is an excess inclined towards blackness and the body of the patient is moist, then give him what purifies his brain as mentioned previously in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a firm cake from linen like a circle, then place it in the center of his head while the patient sits cross-legged holding it from each side. Then take one pound of old sheep tallow.
Then heat it over the fire to a moderate temperature, about what a finger can tolerate when inserted into it. Then pour it in the center of his head within the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this once a week with the patient along with other proper treatments until he recovers, if God wills.
And if you wish, apply cauterization with small drops without holding the cautery iron tightly, but rather by smelling it, for this type of cauterization moderately moistens the brain.
Then apply cotton soaked in sheep tallow or chicken fat to him.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing water descending into the eye.
If it becomes clear to you that water is descending into the eye based on the signs mentioned in the classification, then promptly give the patient what purifies his head, protect him from all moistures, and sweat him in a bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and make a cauterization in the middle of the head, then make two cauterizations on the temples if the water starts to flow from both eyes or from one side if it is from one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries under the skin, and let the cauterizations be long in the width of the temples, and beware of blood gushing out.
If you see any of it, cut it immediately with whatever treatment is possible for you, and we will come to the wisdom in letting the arteries bleed and cutting them while being cautious of bleeding.
And it may be cauterized at the back of the head under the two bones with two significant cautery points.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the cauterization of chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and they originate from the veins and arteries that are on the surface of the head outside the skull, and you are certain that this is due to excessive cold and thick phlegmatic humor, then cauterize with the cautery I described precisely at the beginning of the water flowing. A cauterization in the middle of the head, two cauterizations on the temples, and two cauterizations at the back of the head under the two bones. If more is needed, cauterize with a small cautery on each side at the end of the eyebrow near the corner of the eye.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the cauterization for foul-smelling nose.
If you treated it with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment was not effective, then quickly apply the Cochinchina to the patient for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize it moderately with an olive-shaped cautery iron. Then cauterize twice above the eyebrows slightly under the hair with a nail-shaped cautery iron, and be careful not to cut the artery, and this is the image of the nail-shaped iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing the relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid sags due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
If you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows twice on each side and keep away from the temples, and the length of each cautery should be equal to the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cautery with your hand, but to the extent that a third of the skin is burned, and the shape of the cautery iron should be as described.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If the eyelashes turn inward and irritate the eye, then the cautery for this has two types. Either cauterize with fire or cauterize with a burning medicine. For cauterizing with fire, you should instruct the patient to stop plucking his eyelashes if he does so, until they grow long and even.
For if you graft them when they grow, then bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow. Once they have grown and are stable, place the head of the patient in your lap, then mark on the eyelid with ink in the shape of an as leaf.
And the beginning of the mark should be near the eyelashes, then place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of the buzzard, then heat an iron, this is its shape.
Then iron over the shape you marked little by little in many times until the surface of the skin, which is like the shape of an as leaf, is all burnt, especially its outer part. The sign of your work being correct is seeing that the eyelid has tightened and the hair has risen from the eye's flesh.
Then lift your hand at that time and leave it for three days, then apply cotton with fat until the scab falls off, then treat it with ointment until it heals if God wills. If any hair returns after some time and the eyelid relaxes.
Repeat the cauterization on that area as you did initially. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not affect the eye with hair. As for cauterization with a burning medicine, instruct the patient to let the eyelashes grow long and even.
Then make a shape of an as leaf from paper, then take from the known soap and unslaked lime, about one dirham of each. Crush them both very well together, and do this quickly so that they do not dry out. Then spread it on the paper you made like a leaf and place it on one or both eyelids, and put cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and have the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on the medicine and hold it slightly, moving it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medicine because it will feel like fire to him. As long as he feels the sting, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. Once the stinging subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And observe; if you see the eyelid has risen as it would when cauterized with fire or cut, and if not, then reapply the medicine to the area where it did not affect and did not turn black until your work is complete and the eye is cauterized. Then apply cotton with fat over it until the inflammation from the burning subsides.
Then treat it with the palm ointment or another until it heals. You should be extremely careful during the procedure to ensure that no part of the medicine falls into the eye, for if the eyelid becomes loose after a few days, there is a fear of hair falling into the eye.
Reapply the medicine to the area where the eyelid has become loose, as you did initially, then treat it until it heals. This is a picture of the leaf-shaped paper.
Know that people's eyes may differ in size, so adjust your cauterization accordingly. The right method is not hidden from those who are skilled in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On treating the hernia that appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the hernia with what we mentioned in the classifications of diseases and your treatment is not effective, it should be cauterized according to this method.
Instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, and have a servant hold his head between your hands so that he does not move or shake his head.
Then, place cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a mouse on his eyes, then heat the cautery which looks like this.
It should be hollow like the shape of an eagle feather on one end where the cautery is, and if you want, the other end can be open, or it can be sealed like a needle, but this hollow one is better for your work, if God wills.
Then, squeeze the hernia if it is open and remove the pus and dry it, or if it is not open, then open it and extract its purulent matter. Then place the heated cautery, which is very hot, on it, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
Move your hand slightly away from the eye toward the nose during cauterization so that your hand does not err or startle the patient, causing the cautery to fall into the eyelid and damage it. If you reach the bone on your first attempt at cauterization, otherwise repeat the cautery if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton with oil and treat it with a drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply an intense corrosive medicine until the bone is revealed and scrape it as will be described in its section, if God wills.
Another method for cauterizing the polyp mentioned by some early scholars is to direct yourself to the location of the polyp, cut it open, then place a thin tube into the incision. This is its shape.
Pour into it the amount of a drachm of molten lead, and hold the tube firmly with your hand tightly without moving. The patient must not move at all so that the molten lead does not flow into his eye. It is advisable to place cotton soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
For the lead burns the site of the polyp and cures it wonderfully. If the polyp is cured by what we have mentioned of cauterization and treatment, then there is no need for further action. Otherwise, it is necessary to use nasal puncture and return the polyp to the nasal passage as will be detailed in the specific section on this topic.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing the cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, called a fissure, especially in children's lips, as it frequently happens. If you treat this fissure with the methods mentioned in the division and the treatment is not effective, then heat a small knife cauterizer shaped like this.
Its interior should be as thin as a knife. Then place it, while hot, quickly into the fissure until the cauterization reaches the depth of the fissure. Then treat it with keroty until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Nineteen.
On treating the abscess occurring in the mouth.
If a tumor appears at the root of the gums or in the palate or at the roots of the molars, then it becomes hard and bursts, and the pus flow causes an abscess. Then, if treatment does not succeed, you should heat an iron to fit into the abscess.
Then, insert it while hot into the abscess hole and hold your hand until the iron reaches the depth of it. Do this once or twice, then treat it with the treatments we have mentioned until it heals, if God wills.
If the material is severed and it heals, well and good. Otherwise, it is necessary to examine the area and remove the diseased bone as will be detailed in its chapter, if God wills.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating loose teeth and gums.
If the gums become loose due to moisture and the teeth move, and treatment with medications is ineffective, place the patient's head on your lap. Then heat the iron, whose image will be shown after this, and place the tube on the tooth.
Insert the heated iron quickly into it and hold your hand for a moment until the patient feels the heat has reached the root of the tooth. Then raise your hand. Repeat the application of the iron as many times as needed, then have the patient rinse their mouth with salt water.
Hold it for a while and then remove it. The loose teeth will become stable, the relaxed gums will tighten, and the harmful moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On the Treatment of Toothache.
If the toothache is due to cold or there is a worm in it and treatment with medicines is ineffective, then cauterization should be done in two ways: either by cauterization with fat or by cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with fat, it is to take buffalo fat and boil it in an iron spoon or a shell. Then take some cotton, wrap it around the tip of a probe, dip it in the boiled fat, and place it on the painful tooth quickly. Hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth. If you wish, you can dip a wick or cotton in cold fat and place it on the painful tooth, then put a heated iron above it until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, it is to take a copper or iron tube that has some thickness so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery tool, whose image is provided, and place it directly on the tooth. Hold it until the cautery tool cools. Repeat this several times.
The pain will disappear either that very day or the next day. After the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good fat, hold it for an hour, and then spit it out. This is the image of the cautery tool.
Cauterize with whichever end you prefer and to the extent possible. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On castrating pigs.
If the pigs suffer from phlegm and cold moistures and do not mature with medicines, and you desire quick maturity, heat the hollow iron, which is depicted here as perforated at both ends.
So that the smoke comes out from the other end when cauterizing, place it heated on the same swelling once and twice if needed until you reach the depth of the swelling. If the swelling is small, make the iron according to the size of the swelling.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in fat until what was burned by the fire is gone, then treat it with ointment and poultices until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If moistures prevail over the windpipe, especially if this is accompanied by a cold temperament, it is advisable to first purge the patient with laxative medicines, then cauterize him in the hollow of the neck at the base of the pharynx in the lower area.
Beware that the cauterization does not reach the pharynx and do not burn more than half of the skin, then cauterize another spot at the joint of the neck at the last prominent vertebra, and the iron should be awl-shaped as previously described.
Then treat it with what has been mentioned previously until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On treating lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold moistures and there is no fever or consumption in the patient, and the disease is chronic, then apply two poultices above the collarbones in the low, soft, empty areas, and another poultice in the center of the chest between the breasts.
The poultice should be nail-shaped as previously described. If you prefer, your treatment may be by dripping with an iron that is called the dropper, whose image has been previously shown in treating ear pain, and the number of drips should be around thirty or so.
Then treat it with what has been mentioned until it heals. You may also make a three-pronged iron for cautery in this shape, as it allows you to perform the cautery quickly since you can apply three cauterizations at once with it, and here is the shape of the iron.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus dislocates due to slippery moistures or does not stay in place when reduced, making it a habitual dislocation that occurs with the slightest movement as we have observed, it is necessary to first reduce the joint. Then the patient should lie on their back or on the correct side. Then raise the skin inside the armpit upward with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inward.
Then heat the iron with two handles which looks like this.
Then iron the skin until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the ironing will be four irons. It can also be ironed with an iron that has three prongs, in which case the shape of the ironing will be six irons, and the prongs should be according to the thickness of the material.
This is the image of the iron with three prongs.
And one can be added to this number, making the irons eight. Then, balls are applied with finely ground salt over the ironing, and the patient must rest without moving the limb for a while until it strengthens. If the dislocation is upwards, which rarely happens.
So iron above the shoulder one strong nail-shaped iron or many dotted irons, for the joint will become firm, the moisture will go away, and the patient will heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On ironing the stomach.
If cold and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, causing it to deviate from its natural temperament, and if various treatments have not been effective, then the patient should lie on his back, extend his legs and arms, and be ironed with three irons. One iron under the sternum, the size of a finger, with a nail-shaped iron, and two irons below on both sides of the single iron, so that the shape of the irons forms a triangle, spaced apart so they do not come together if there is pus discharge. The depth of the ironing should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and this is the shape of the irons.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you want, make one large indentation in the center of the stomach as follows.
And the iron used to make this single indentation should be in the shape of a circle as follows.
And the stomach may be cauterized by spots for those who are apprehensive of this cautery. This is done by marking points on the stomach with ink to the extent you desire, then cauterizing it with a spotted iron, then treating it with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the cautery of cold liver.
If pain in the liver is due to cold and moisture or to a hot vapor such that it has departed from its natural temperament excessively, and if the patient has been treated with what we mentioned in the division but that was not effective. It is appropriate that the patient lie on his back, and three cautery marks should be marked with ink on the liver, below the ribs where the arm of a person ends, in this shape and exactly this amount.
There should be the thickness of a finger between each cautery mark. The cautery should be applied along the length of the body in a straight line, and do not press your hand too firmly on the cautery iron.
The amount of skin burned should be half its thickness, no more. The patient should stand on his feet; if he cannot stand, he should lie down with his legs stretched and arms raised. This is the shape of the cautery iron.
And you can cauterize these burns with a knife cautery if you have gentleness and skill in the craft and take care not to overdo the cauterization so as not to burn all the skin and reach the intestines, for the skin there is thin, so know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cauterizing the swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver and you want to determine if that swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its membrane, know that if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will feel a heavy pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the membrane of the liver, there will be a sharp pain along with the pain. You may find that its treatment has exhausted the physicians. It should be that the patient lies on his back, and then you mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire, which is the cautery that resembles a mill, and this is its shape. Cauterize it once with it until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the membrane, until all the pus is drained out, then treat it as you would an abscess until it heals.
This type of cauterization should only be performed by someone who has long experience in the practice of medicine and has dealt with these diseases through experience many times. Only then should one proceed with such a procedure, but leaving it alone is better in my opinion.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On extracting the cataract.
The earlier ones mentioned extracting the cold cataract with the root of the zeraunad in this manner: take one long dry root of the zeraunad as long as you can find, and it should be as thick as a finger. Then dip it in oil and ignite it in fire. Then cauterize it with one cauterization between the connection of the collarbone and the neck, two small cauterizations below the arteries slightly inclined towards the side under the beard, and two large cauterizations above the breasts between the third and fourth ribs.
Two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backwards. Another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. Three cauterizations from behind: one between the shoulders, and two beside the spine below the cauterization that is between the palms.
You should not deepen the cauterization but it should be on the surface of the skin slightly. Some of the earlier ones mentioned that some people used to employ an iron rod similar to a needle, heat it, and insert it between the ribs until it reaches the tumor itself.
The treatment is carried out as mentioned for the liver tumor. In this cautery of the peritoneum, there is a great risk; either the patient may die immediately, or an incurable fistula may develop in the area.
Chapter Thirty.
On the cauterization of the spleen.
If you treat the spleen disease with the treatments we mentioned in the division and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization can be done in three ways, all of which are correct. One of them is to cauterize it with three or four cauterizations arranged along the length of the spleen.
In the form of liver cauterizations which are presented in their shape, and there should be between each cauterization the thickness of a finger or slightly more, and the quality of the cautery is what we mentioned for liver cauterization, and do not deepen your hand with the cautery, and the patient's image is lying on his back.
And the other side is in the elbow, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends, and lifting the skin should be across the body so that the cauterizations fall along the length of the body.
Then insert the two probes very carefully until they penetrate the skin from the other side, then remove the cautery so that there are four cauterizations. If you wish to use the other cautery with three probes.
Then treat the cauterized area after allowing it to discharge pus for many days, as this is more effective than all the previous treatments mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the cauterization for dropsy.
Cauterization is beneficial specifically for dropsy of the zacc type. If you have treated the person with various treatments as mentioned in the classification and your treatment has not been effective, then you should cauterize with four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, one on the spleen, two behind his back between the ribs, one opposite his chest, and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be close to the thickness of the skin. Then leave the cauterized area open to discharge pus for a long time.
Do not let the patient leave treatment after cauterization with what is appropriate, as it combines both meanings and hastens recovery. The shape of the cautery iron used for the back should be nail-shaped as previously described, and that which is used for the back should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On Cauterizing the Feet and Legs.
If they swell in the dropsy and are filled with yellow water, it is appropriate to cauterize on the top of the foot between the little toe and the ring toe.
Hold the cautery iron with your hand and do not bend it. Then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for it will drain the yellow water. The cautery iron should be shaped like this.
Then cauterize both legs, twice on each leg. The cauterization should be done with the blade end of the cautery iron and along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another lower towards the middle of the leg. On each thigh, cauterize twice as well.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time so that the water drains from it, then treat it with all the methods we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On Cauterizing Diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is due to cold and moisture, which weakens the holding and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and if it has not been cured by various treatments, and you see that the patient can tolerate cauterization and is strong enough, then cauterize him with a large cautery on his stomach. As mentioned previously regarding ironing the stomach with a circular iron and four ironings around the navel, gently use the fine nail-shaped iron and apply one ironing on the cotton above the rectum, large or two.
If you see the moistures are abundant and the patient can tolerate that, then apply one ironing on the pubic area, one on each flank, and you may add two small ironings on the stomach near the large one. Indeed, this is a successful treatment whose benefit does not fail, if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On ironing hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic hemorrhoids, whether many or one, and they are composed of thick cold mixtures or corrupt moistures, and if the treatment mentioned in the division has not been effective, then apply three ironings to the patient on the lower part of the lumbar spine slightly below the waist in a triangular pattern.
Apply one ironing below the navel with a width of about two fingers. If you determine that his stomach has cooled and his food is not digesting, and you see his face swollen, then apply a large ironing on his stomach as previously mentioned and one on his liver. Apply another one on his spleen with a nail-shaped iron. Leave the ironing open for some time, then continue treating him until he recovers, if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On ironing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the iron which resembles a mill then insert it hot into the middle of the cut wart and push your hand until the iron reaches the source from which the blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one individually as described, then treat them with appropriate ointments until they heal. Cauterizing them again on cotton can be more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On cauterizing the anal fistula and its surrounding areas.
If the patient does not respond to incision and the procedure we described in its place and is unwilling to do so, it may heal by cauterization. If one develops a fistula and pus and corrupt humors continuously flow from it, the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then determine its depth with the probe. Heat the iron which resembles a mill, then insert it hot into the fistula straight to the depth where the probe entered. Cauterize it repeatedly until all the corrupt substances are burned, once or twice or three times as needed.
Be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a major vein. If the fistula leads to the bladder or the intestines, be cautious.
Be cautious of all these areas. You should only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area and you determine that it is not perforating. Then treat the area until it heals. If the area closes up, the discharge stops, and remains so for a period of time, know that it has healed completely.
And if the materials do not cease from it, know that it is penetrating or there is rotten bone in its depth or something like what will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On the cauterization of the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidney due to cold or dense wind and the patient's strength decreases because of it, then it should be cauterized on both sides directly on each kidney with a nail cauterizer as previously mentioned. Sometimes it may be necessary to apply a third cauterization on the same spot, so three cauterizations in a row would be more beneficial for treatment.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and moisture until the patient cannot hold urine, then cauterize it with one cauterization below the navel on the bladder where pubic hair begins, and another cauterization to the right of the navel and another to its left.
The cauterization on each side should be to the extent of a thumb joint. Cauterize it with one cauterization below the back or two if necessary, and the cauterizer should be nail-shaped as previously mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On the cauterization of the uterus.
If cold and moisture cause material to form in the uterus, preventing conception and spoiling its functioning, or if pain occurs with its menstruation, it should be cauterized with three cauterizations around the navel as mentioned in the cauterization of the bladder.
