Chapter Forty.
On how to dislocate the hip.
Incorrect moistures may settle in the hip joint, causing it to dislocate. Its sign is that one leg appears longer than the other when measured against each other, and you will find a gap where the dislocation occurred. It should be treated by searing the affected area around the hip joint itself in a semi-circular manner.
After you mark with ink around the joint, as it circles, so that the center of the joint falls in the middle of the circle. The sealer should be as previously described for sealing the stomach. If this sealer is not available to you, then use three sealings with a large olive-shaped sealer.
Make the sealing deep according to the thickness of the entire skin, then treat it until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Forty-One.
On sealing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint and the cause is cold and moisture, and if the treatment mentioned in the division has not been effective and the condition persists for a long time, it should be treated by lightening the body with strong mixtures such as stinking seeds or manna seeds, and the like.
Then seal it, and the sealing can be done in two ways: either by sealing with burning medications or by sealing with fire. Sealing with fire can be done in many ways, one of which is to seal three triangular sealings directly on the hip joint itself, as follows.
And it acquires a suitable depth, and there should be between each cauterization and another the thickness of a finger. The cautery should be olive-shaped. A cautery may also be applied to the middle on the hipbone itself, making four cauterizations. If you wish, apply the circle previously mentioned in the cauterization for dislocation of the hip with one cautery.
To have the circle surround all of the hip and burn through all thicknesses of the skin, this is the description of the cautery for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, apply two cauterizations on the thigh at the place where the patient indicates the pain is felt, and one cautery above the ankle by four fingers towards the outer side slightly. The cautery should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cautery should be only as thick as the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, apply cautery at the places he points out with a point cautery three or four times or more if needed. If he indicates the pain is below the knee towards the leg, apply one knife-shaped cautery there.
Be cautious in all your cautery applications to not burn a nerve or a major artery, which could cause a severe or permanent disability to the patient.
I have seen cases where cautery above the ankle was excessive, leading to gangrene in the leg that spread to the foot, causing it to ulcerate and resulting in the complete destruction of the limb, followed by diarrhea and death. If the pain is on both sides, apply cautery to both in the same manner described.
Some scholars among the wise have mentioned a certain cauterization of the hip, characterized as follows: a vessel is made from iron, its diameter being half a finger-breadth, and at its mouth it should be as thick as a date pit or slightly less. Inside this vessel there are another two vessels.
And there should be a distance equal to the width of the thumb joint between each vessel, and these vessels are open on both sides, with their height being about one or two joints. A handle made of iron, securely fastened to the vessels, is also attached, and this is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it turns red and spits sparks. Then it is placed on the affected hip, with the patient lying on their healthy side, and it cauterizes it with three circular cautery applications at once. Then it is left for three days, and it is dressed with lard, leaving the wound open for some days.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals. The author of this book says that this type of cautery is rarely used due to its severity and the fear invoked by its appearance, and few can endure it. However, it is an effective cautery for those who can withstand it and apply it correctly.
As for cautery with burning substances, it involves making two vessels like rings into which the tip of the penis can fit, made of bronze or iron.
There should be a height of about two joints or so around them, and the distance between them should be equal to the thickness of a finger. They should be open at both the bottom and the top, and they should be secured to each other in this manner.
Then place it on the true hip with the patient lying on his sound side and secure your hand firmly, then pour between the two circles of sharp water that is warmed by fire slightly and keep it for an hour's duration and the patient must endure its stinging and burning for he will feel a sting like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then raise your hand with the tool and wash all the sharp water off the hip with fresh water, then leave it for three days and apply fat to it until the blackness of the burn goes away and leave it for some days until pus flows from it, for this is more beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a poultice for him that holds water according to what you made for the hip alike. This is the description of sharp water and I have confirmed it in the article on the preparation of medicines.
Take an equal part of soda ash and unslaked lime, crush them, and place them in a new pot that has one small hole at the bottom, just large enough for the liquid to pass through. Place another glass-bottomed pot underneath the first pot.
Then pour fresh water over the soda ash and lime to cover them by a finger's depth after you have squeezed them firmly with your hand, and leave the pot until the sharp water drips into the bottom glass-bottomed pot. Then collect all of that water and pour it over another batch of lime and soda ash as well.
It is then extremely sharp and is used for many medical procedures and for cauterizing various body parts, as it acts like fire itself. Some medicines that are also used to cauterize the hip include colocynth, baladonna honey, lime with soap, all pounded together.
Galen reported a story about an ancient man's treatment for hip pain and sciatica, greatly exaggerating its importance, claiming that it does not require any other treatment and that it cures in one attempt.
To the extent that he might enter the bath carried and come out healed. This is done by taking green mercury, and if green is not available, then fresh dry mercury should be taken, grind it well with some fat, and apply it to the hip where the pain is, or on the leg, or on the thigh.
It is tightened and left for about three hours or until the patient feels that the burning has subsided. Then, he enters the bath; when his body sweats, he should enter the pool. The pain will disappear and he will be healed by the will of God. If he is not healed, repeat the dressing again after ten days.
For it will be healed, if God wills. Know that one should not use cauterization with these medications unless the body has been purged. Dioscorides mentioned that goat dandruff, if used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial for it.
The cauterization should be done in this manner: take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep area between the thumb and the wrist, which is closer to the wrist. Then, take dry goat dandruff.
Ignite it with fire until it becomes a ember, then place it on the wool and leave it until it extinguishes. Then take another one and continue doing this until the sensation reaches the middle of the arm to the hip, and the pain subsides by the will of God. This type of cauterization is called dander cauterization.
Chapter Forty-Two.
On treating back pain.
Pain in the back may result from various causes, such as a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like. It is caused by the accumulation of cold and moist matter, and cauterization is only applied to this type.
When the pain is due to the accumulation of cold matter, after the patient has vomited with the help of aromatic seeds or similar substances, cauterization should be performed on the back where the pain is located, making three rows across the affected area. The location should first be outlined with ink, and in each row, there should be five burns or more, depending on what you see fit based on the patient's tolerance and strength.
Cauterization should be done with a point cauterizer. If you prefer, you can perform three or four burns with a medium-sized nail cauterizer in this manner.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On cauterizing the initial stage of hunchback.
This condition often affects small children. A sign of its initial onset in a child is difficulty breathing when standing and moving, and you may find a protruding vertebra at the end of their spine that stands out from the other vertebrae.
If you see this and want to prevent it, cauterize it with a circular cauterizer in this manner. Apply the cauterization equally on both sides of the vertebra. If you prefer, cauterize around the vertebra in two or three rows with a point cauterizer, making the points close to each other. Then treat the area until it heals as previously mentioned.
And beware of using cauterization on the hump that results from nerve spasm.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If the joint pains are due to cold humidities that affect any part of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, it is customary for physicians to call this gout, in particular.
If cold gout is treated with the various treatments we mentioned in the classification and the pains do not subside, then cauterization can alleviate them. This involves cauterizing around the joints of the legs after purging, performing many cauteries. The cautery should be olive-shaped and medium-sized, as follows.
And if you need to apply cauterization to the foot, do so with a point cautery. If the pains ascend to the knees or other joints, which often happens, cauterize each knee with three or four cauteries on each side using the same olive-shaped cautery.
If more cauterization is needed, do so, but do not penetrate deeply with the cautery; it should only be as deep as the thickness of the skin. If the pains ascend to the hips or back, apply the cauterization as we have described in its proper section.
If the pains are only in the hands, apply point cauterization around the wrists in two rows. If the pain remains in the fingers, apply a point cautery to each joint and on the palm. If the pain ascends to the elbows or shoulders after several days.
Treat both from all sides and do not neglect proper care and taking medicines, for if he improves his diet and expels phlegm, then he will recover with this cauterization, if God wills.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin and part of the intestine and spermatic cord descends into the testicle, and this is newly occurred and recent, it is advisable to order the patient to abstain from food for a day and to use laxatives that will expel stool from his body.
Then he should lie down in front of you on his back, and you order him to hold himself until the spermatic cord or intestine protrudes, then you push it back with your finger. Then mark with ink under the hernia on the pubic bone a sign resembling a semicircle, its ends directed upwards towards the body.
Then heat an iron that looks like this.
Heat it until it turns white and stops sparking. Then push the intestine or spermatic cord back into his body. A servant should place his hand on the area to prevent the intestine from protruding. The patient's legs are spread apart, with a pillow placed under him, and another servant ties his legs.
Another person should hold his arms down by standing on his chest. Then place the iron directly on the marked spot, press with the iron vertically and firmly, and hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat once more if it did not reach the bone the first time.
You must protect your effort from the protrusion of the intestine while cauterizing so as not to burn it, which could result in either the patient's death or a great disability. Know that if you do not reach the bone with the cauterization, your work will not succeed. The cautery for children should be mild according to their size and for adults according to their size.
Then treat the cauterized area after three days with lard until the dryness of the fire disappears, then treat it with other ointments until it heals. Let the patient lie on their back for forty days until the wound is completely sealed.
You should make his food during the treatment period what soothes his stomach so that the intestine does not protrude when he strains or defecates. Then, if he wishes to get up after forty days, use a firm bandage and keep it on for another forty days. He should reduce fatigue, overeating and drinking, and loud shouting.
For if this procedure is followed, he will be completely healed, God willing. I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its own section, God willing. As for the hernia that occurs in other parts of the abdomen and is new, if you want it not to worsen, cauterize the hernia with a circular cautery according to its size. Let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds, then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not worsen, God willing. Some early practitioners have suggested cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, make a line across at the top of the hernia and another line below it. Place your hand in the middle with a single nail-shaped cautery. The first cauterization is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Six.
On cauterizing the uterus.
If pain occurs in some organs due to a fall or blow and the pain persists for a long time and treatment with medicines is ineffective, it should be cauterized with a cautery of points. The cauterization of each organ should be according to its size, strength, and weakness.
If the pain persists and affects the uterus, it should heal from the first cauterization. If not, repeat the cauterization because these pains usually move from one organ to a nearby one; therefore, you should follow them with cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter Forty-Seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they can greatly benefit from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that results from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
When you want to cauterize it, look if the leprosy is in its early stages and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, but it has not subsided and has not been halted, and you fear that corruption may take over the entire constitution of the patient, then cauterize him on the head with five cautery sessions. The first one in the middle of the head, the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one at the back on the bump of the occiput. Insert your hand slightly with the cautery so that it affects the bone a little.
And remove its scales to facilitate the breath of thick vapors from it and cauterize it like the spleen as previously mentioned. And if leprosy has persisted in the patient and clearly appeared, then it should be cauterized with these types of cauterization that we mentioned for the head. Cauterization at the tip of the nose, two cauterizations on the cheeks, two on the vertebrae of the neck, six on the vertebrae of the back, one large one on the buttocks at the base of the tail, another above it on the same plate, and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee, two on the shoulders, two on the elbows, two on the ribs of the chest, and cauterize each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet with a cautery, and on each heel of his feet and the wrists of his hands.
And be careful of the nerve at the back of the heels so as not to burn them. It may also be cauterized on the pubic bone, another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver. Know that the more you increase the cautery, the more beneficial and effective it is.
Know that the patient does not feel pain from cauterization as much as a healthy person would because his body has become numb. The cauterizing instruments should be of various sizes, large and small, according to the parts and joints, as previously mentioned in the descriptions of the tools.
Then treat the cauterized area with crushed cabbage mixed with honey and other treatments until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Forty-Eight.
In cauterizing numbness, when a body part becomes numb and is treated with medicines, ointments, and dressings but does not heal, then cauterize the numb body part itself according to whether the part is large or small, and let your cauterization be somewhat deeper in the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals. Cauterization may also be applied to some forms of numbness affecting the hand and foot due to problems in the vertebrae where the nerves that move that body part exit, thus eliminating the numbness.
None but those who are well-versed in the anatomy of body parts and the exits of the nerves that move the body should undertake this.
Chapter Forty-Nine.
On cauterizing leprosy.
If leprosy persists and no medical treatment is effective, then apply a cauterization that is slightly deep according to the thickness of the skin until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with ground lentils mixed with rose oil, mint leaves, and pigeon blood or thrush blood, using an equal part of each. Mix everything together, apply it to a cloth, and keep it on the area until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On cauterizing cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to halt its progression, cauterize around it in a circular motion with a circular cautery. Some wise men have mentioned that a severe cauterization should be applied to its center, but I do not agree with this because I expect it could lead to ulceration, which I have observed on several occasions.
The correct thing is to cauterize around it in a circle as we said or with many cautery points.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On cauterizing the hernia.
If a person develops a hernia that delays in maturing, either due to the active humor causing it, or because of the age of the patient if he is old and has little blood, or due to time, and you wish to hasten the maturity of the hernia, cauterize around it with many small cautery points drop by drop.
Then leave it, for it will mature faster. If you want to cauterize it further, heat the cautery iron which looks like this.
Place it in the center of the hernia until it penetrates the skin, and let the cautery be on the lower part to facilitate the flow of pus. Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On cauterizing the cancer.
Cancer is a corruption that spreads in the organ, consuming it like fire consumes dry wood. If you see cancer in a place that can be cauterized with fire, heat many nail-shaped cautery irons, small and large, according to what is suitable for the location where the cancer is.
Then cauterize it from all sides until all the corruption is completely removed and nothing remains. Then leave it for three days, and apply powdered sulfur with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scabs and corruption are completely removed.
Then treat it with poultices that promote flesh growth. If you see after three days that the flesh grows properly without corruption, otherwise repeat cauterization on what remains of the corrupted areas. The ulcer can also be treated with sharp medicine.
For it is more quickly effective than fire, and I have mentioned its treatment with sharp medicine in the classification, so take it from there whenever you need it.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the cauterization of reversed and non-reversed nails.
This condition often occurs on the soles of the feet, and it is a rough thing that irritates the person. Cauterization for this has two forms: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with sharp water.
As for cauterization with fire, it involves heating a hollow iron tool similar to an eagle's feather. The tool should be made of iron and sized appropriately to surround the nail on all sides, with a thin edge. Then place the heated tool over the nail and rotate it around the nail until the tool reaches the depth of the nail.
Leave it for three days until it begins to pus, then bandage it with crushed wild barley mixed with salt. Leave the dressing on overnight; it will remove it from its roots. Then treat the wound site with a poultice that promotes flesh growth until it heals. If the nails are not reversed.
This condition often occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and legs. You should take a tube made of copper or iron or shaped like an eagle's feather, place it over the nail or wart, then pour a small amount of sharp water into the tube.
Hold your hand as you turn it with a slight twitch of your hand so that the edge of the tube affects the base of the nail and the sharp water finds its way to penetrate to the base of the nail and the patient endures the sting of the sharp water for a while.
Then leave it, for the nail will be pulled out by its root. Do this one by one until you have treated all of them in the body. Then treat the areas after they are pulled out with ointments that promote flesh growth.
Chapter Fifty-Four.
On cauterizing the tonsils.
If one of the tonsils is affected by a cold in the nerve or by a quarter fever or something else, it should be cauterized four or five times between the vertebrae with one cautery per vertebra and one cautery on his chest. And on his stomach, apply one cautery with the olive-shaped iron, for the tonsil will subside and hasten the maturation of the cold disease.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
On cauterizing abscesses that occur in the body.
Sometimes, ugly abscesses may appear in the body due to thick, cold, and corrupt materials. When they first appear, each pustule should be gently cauterized on its head using a cedar stick with one end ignited by fire, or the long root of zeraunjan, or a lentil-shaped cautery iron.
And the blisters should be cauterized at the beginning of their eruption in this manner, so they do not increase and the excess causing them dissipates, and the patient recovers from it, except that this should be after the patient has been purged with a venesection.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
On cauterizing bleeding occurring from cutting an artery.
Bleeding of blood often occurs from a severed artery in an external wound or when slicing a tumor or cauterizing an organ, and so on, making it difficult to cut it.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger at the mouth of the artery and press firmly so that the blood is confined under your finger and nothing comes out. Then put several small and large olive cauterizers in the fire and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the place where the artery was severed. Place the cauterizer directly on the vein after quickly removing your finger and hold it until the bleeding stops.
If the blood gushes out when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery and extinguishes the cauterizer, quickly take another cauterizer from those in the prepared fire. Continue doing this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve, which could cause another disability in the patient. Know that if blood is bleeding from an artery, it cannot be cut, especially if the artery is large, except by one of four methods: either by cauterization as we said, or by amputation if it has not already separated. For if it is amputated, its ends will contract and the blood will be cut off. Either it should be tightly sutured with threads, or medicines that stop the bleeding should be applied to it and secured firmly with bandages.
And as for those who attempt to cut it with a ligature or by tightening with rags or placing cauterizing substances and the like, it will not be severed at all except in rare cases. If any of this occurs to someone and there is no doctor or medicine available, they should immediately place their index finger on the mouth of the wound itself as we have described.
He should tighten it greatly until the blood is constricted and flows out from above the wound and on the artery. The finger should not be removed, always keeping it cold with very cold water until the blood freezes, thickens, and stops. During this time, you should assess what it needs in terms of cauterization or medication.
The Second Chapter.
On Incisions, Punctures, Lancing, Surgery, and the Like.
Khalef said, "We have mentioned in the first chapter every disease that is suitable for treatment with cauterization by fire and burning medicines, its symptoms, causes, instruments, and forms of cauters. I have organized this into sections from head to foot.
And I follow the same path in this chapter to make it easy for the student to understand what is required.
Before I begin, you should know, my children, that this chapter contains more risks than what is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And for that reason, it is necessary to have the strongest warning, because the work in this chapter often involves bleeding which sustains life when a vein is opened or an incision is made on a swelling, or lancing an abscess, or surgical treatment, or extraction of an arrowhead, or making an incision on a stone and so forth, all of which involve risk and fear, and death often occurs in most of them.
And I advise you to avoid what is doubtful for you, for in this craft various kinds of people may come to you with different sorts of ailments; some of them are wearied by their disease and death isè½»è€Œæ˜“ä¸¾çš„æŽ¥å—äº†ã€‚è¯·æ³¨æ„ï¼Œæ ¹æ®æ‚¨çš„è¦æ±‚ï¼Œæˆ‘åœ¨è¿™é‡Œè¿›è¡Œäº†ç¿»è¯‘ï¼Œä½†æ˜¯æœ€åŽä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†ä¼¼ä¹Žæœ‰ä¸­æ–­ï¼ŒåŽŸå¥æ˜¯é˜¿æ‹‰ä¼¯è¯­ï¼Œä½†æœ€åŽä»¥ä¸­æ–‡ç»“æŸï¼Œè¿™å¯èƒ½æ˜¯ä¸ªé”™è¯¯ã€‚æ­£ç¡®çš„å®Œæ•´ç¿»è¯‘åº”å…¨éƒ¨ä½¿ç”¨ç›®æ ‡è¯­è¨€ï¼ˆæ­¤å¤„ä¸ºè‹±è¯­ï¼‰ã€‚æ­£ç¡®å®Œæ•´çš„ç¿»è¯‘åº”è¯¥æ˜¯ï¼šâ€œAnd I advise you to avoid what is doubtful for you, for in this craft various kinds of people may come to you with different sorts of ailments; some of them are wearied by their disease and death is easily accepted.â€ å¦‚æžœæ‚¨æœ‰è¿›ä¸€æ­¥çš„æŒ‡ç¤ºæˆ–éœ€è¦æ›´æ­£ï¼Œè¯·å‘ŠçŸ¥ã€‚ä½†æ˜¯æ ¹æ®æ‚¨çš„åŽŸå§‹æŒ‡ä»¤ï¼Œæˆ‘å°†ä¸ä½œé¢å¤–è¯„è®ºç›´æŽ¥æä¾›ç¿»è¯‘å†…å®¹ã€‚ç”±äºŽä¸Šè¿°æƒ…å†µå¯èƒ½å¼•èµ·æ··æ·†ï¼Œæˆ‘å°†é‡æ–°æŒ‰ç…§è¦æ±‚ä»…æä¾›è‹±æ–‡ç¿»è¯‘ï¼Œä¸åŒ…å«ä»»ä½•ä¸­æ–‡å†…å®¹åŠè§£é‡Šï¼šâ€œAnd I advise you to avoid what is doubtful for you, for in this craft various kinds of people may come to you with different sorts of ailments; some of them are wearied by their disease and death is easily accepted.â€
Due to the severity of his illness and the length of his suffering, and there is evidence in his disease that indicates death. And there are those who spend their wealth on you and provide for you in hope of health, but his disease is severe. Therefore, you should not assist anyone who comes to you with such a condition at all.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed with anything unless you have certain knowledge that is valid to you about the praiseworthy outcome. Use foreknowledge and warning in all treatments for your patients regarding what leads to safety.
For in that, you will find support in acquiring praise, glory, remembrance, and commendation. May God guide you to wisdom, O sons, and not deprive you of the right and success. Indeed, that is in His hand; there is no god but He.
I have arranged this chapter into sections according to what was previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization from head to foot, so that it may be easier for you to find what you seek from it, if God wills.
The first chapter.
On the treatment of water that accumulates in the heads of boys.
This ailment often occurs in infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness. It may also occur due to a hidden illness that is not known.
I have not seen this illness in anyone other than boys. Everyone I have seen from them met death quickly, which is why I have decided to avoid treating it. I have seen a boy whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger each day. Until the boy could not even sit up due to the largeness of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
This moisture either accumulates between the skin and the bone or beneath the bone on the pericranium. The treatment for this is as follows: if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, it should be treated by making a single incision across the middle of the head, with the length of the incision being about two inches so that the moisture can flow out. This is the form of the scalpel.
If the moisture is more and the swelling is larger, make two intersecting incisions in this form:.
If the moisture is beneath the bone, its sign is that you will see the sutures of the head open on all sides, and the water will sink when you press it with your hand; this should not be hidden from you. You should make three incisions in the middle of the head in this form:.
After the incision, all moisture is drained, then the incisions are sutured with bandages and stitches. Then it is treated from above with drink and oil until the fifth day. Afterward, the bindings are removed and the wound is treated with lint and ointments. Do not leave the head tightly bound and feed the patient a dry diet low in moisture.
Until the organ strengthens and heals, another description of the incision is to look where the bone of the swelling and accumulation of water appears because it may be more at the back of the head or in the front or on the right or on the left.
Aim the incision at where the swelling and accumulation of water appear, make the incision as much as you can, but be cautious not to cut an artery, which could cause bleeding leading to the patient's death from that bleeding, even with the expulsion of moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cut the two arteries behind the ears, which are known as the carotid arteries.
When one is afflicted with severe attacks to the eyes or chest, and this persists without medication proving effective, the most effective treatment is to cut these two arteries.
If you intend to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a coarse cloth so that the artery becomes visible. Then secure the patientâ€™s neck with his shirt. Look for where the pulse beats; their positions are the two lower areas behind the ears, which are rarely hidden except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, and cut them with a sharp lancet to the bone. This should be done across the head. If you wish, insert the lancet below the artery and lift it upwards while cutting. The length of the cut should be about two closed fingers.
For if the sweat duct is severed, blood will flow out in a pulsating manner, spurting forward continuously. If the artery does not appear perceptible, it is necessary to estimate from the ear about the width of three fingers, then mark with ink and cut until the bone, and what should be done is.
Six ounces of blood should be let as a moderate amount, but more or less may be let according to what appears to you regarding the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries. Then look at the wound, if there is any pus left on the bone, cut it off.
To prevent a hot swelling, then secure the wound with a wick of linen cloth, then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the ligation of the arteries in the cheeks.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks caused by sharp humidities and heat in the cheek muscles, or severe chronic headaches and the like, and treatment with various medical treatments has not been effective, we have tried in these diseases to ligate or cauterize the arteries in the cheeks as described.
The method of ligation involves shaving the patient's hair in the cheeks and locating the visible artery in the cheek, as it can be identified by its pulse. It is rarely hidden except in rare individuals.
Or in extreme cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with a fold of his garment, then rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel which is depicted here. Then gently peel the skin with it until you reach the artery, then insert a probe and pull it up until you extract it from the skin and free it from the adhesions beneath it on all sides. If the artery is thin, then bend it with the tip of the probe. Then cut a portion from it according to how far apart its ends are and that it constricts without causing bleeding. For if it is not amputated or severed, the blood will not flow at all. Then let about six to three ounces of blood.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong double thread. The thread should be either from abriysim or from the sinews of wood so that rot does not set in quickly before the wound heals and causes bleeding.
Then cut what remains between the ties. Do this at that time or another later. If you wish, cauterize it to the bone with a surgical cautery until its ends are severed; this will serve the same purpose or even better as we have said.
Except if the patient has a fever or an overheated temperament, for cauterization is what helps in consuming moistures and thus is more beneficial. After excising the arteries, the area should be packed with old cotton, and tight bandages should be applied. After removal, it should be treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth and by bandaging until it heals, if God wills.
If bleeding occurs from the artery during your work, immediately cut it either by cauterization or fill the area with pitch and hold tightly until the bleeding stops. If you do not have any of these, place your finger until the blood clots.
The area should be washed with very cold water until the intensity subsides and it is tightened as necessary. And what is lighter and easier than cauterizing the artery is to sear the vein with this two-bladed iron after marking the areas with ink.
Heat it to a very high temperature until it reaches the bone and sears the vein in two places so that there is distance between its ends, for they will not unite with this searing at all. This is the image of the iron.
The two blades should be sharp, resembling small points, but they must be much less sharp than a knife because if they are as sharp as a knife, they cool down quickly and do not sever the flesh swiftly. If there is some thickness to them, they retain the heat of the fire better.
And cut the flesh quickly. This method is better than all others, being lighter and easier. The space between the two blades should be about the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of persistent hot tearing to the eyes.
If the tears are persistent and their flow is from the veins on the outer part of the skull, and if no treatment with medical drugs has been beneficial, and you see that the patient's face has reddened. He feels a sensation in his forehead like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are weak and moist, with his eyelids eroded and his eye sockets damaged by the intensity of the tears. Therefore, the initial treatment requires this method, which is called treatment by the sword, involving ordering the patient to shave the hair on his forehead.
Then make three parallel incisions on the forehead along its length; each incision should be about two fingers in length. The first incision is parallel to the length of the nose at the center of the forehead, the second slightly away from the movement of the muscle that is in the temple, and the third from the other side.
Be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws. The distance between each incision should be about three fingers held together. Have pieces of prepared sponge or many dry cloths with you to soak up the blood.
Then insert the sharp two-edged instrument, which is depicted as follows. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, peel off all the skin between the two incisions along with the fascia on the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove this instrument. Also insert another instrument into the first incision, which is called a skewer that is sharp on one side and smooth and not sharp on the other side. This is its depiction:.
Make its sharp side face upward toward the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side face the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, cutting all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the outer surface of the skin.
Then do the same from the middle incision to the other one. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, squeeze the areas to remove clotted blood. Then place a wick of cotton in each incision and put on it a dressing moistened with a decoction and oil or vinegar and oil.
So that a hot swelling does not occur, on the third day untie the bandage and use plenty of wet-to-dry dressing with lukewarm water, then treat it with a poultice of basilicon with rose oil and all other treatments used for wounds until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from inside the head.
If one experiences frequent, sharp, hot discharges and you see the eyes as a result being weakened, smaller, with their sight diminished, and the eyelids ulcerated with the eyelashes falling out. There will be a sharp, painful headache and continuous sneezing. From these symptoms, you can determine that those materials and discharges come from deep, numerous veins. The best treatment for this is as follows: order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the center of the forehead.
There will be a sharp, painful headache and continuous sneezing. From these symptoms, you can determine that those materials and discharges come from deep, numerous veins. The best treatment for this is as follows: order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the center of the forehead. Or raise it slightly with an instrument and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, making the incision until you reach the bone. Keep your hand away from the temples' moving muscles used during chewing. Once the bone is exposed, soak up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or a linen thread, then secure it from above with bandages. Soak the bandages with a decoction and oil so that no hot swelling occurs. When you remove them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should scrape the bone until flesh begins to grow.
Then treat it with the drying regimen that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colocynth, preparing an ointment from them, and using it to promote flesh growth in similar surgeries.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear is of four types: either a metallic stone or something like a stone such as iron and glass, or a plant seed like chickpeas and kernels, and the like, or a liquid such as water, vinegar, and the like.
Or an animal; if a stone or something like a stone that does not grow in the ear falls in, then face the sun with the ear. If you see the stone, drip some purple dye or turpentine oil on it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with cinder. And plug the nostrils when sneezing comes, after placing a band of cloth or wool around the ear and lifting the ear upwards, as this treatment often causes it to come out. If it does not come out, then try to remove it with a gentle probe, which is depicted in this manner.
If it comes out with the probe, and if not, try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved blind hook. If it does not come out that way, then make a tube of copper and insert one end of the tube into the ear hole.
And seal what is around the tube with softened wax to prevent air from taking any route other than the tube, then draw it out forcefully with your breath; this often causes it to come out. If it does not come out by the methods described, then take a little of the incense of Anbat or the bird-luring resin and place it at the end of the probe.
After you wrap it with tight cotton, then gently insert it into the ear hole after drying the ear from moisture, if it does not come out with all that we described, then proceed to cut before a hot swelling or stiffness occurs.
The method of cutting is to bleed the patient first in the cupping and then let them release blood according to their strength. Then sit the patient in front of you, turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear where the lobe is, at its lower part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you have. Then quickly sew the incision and treat it until it heals.
And if what has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, try to remove it as we described. If it does not come out, then take a thin, delicate lancet in this form.
Try to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear with it. You should only do this if you are certain that the seed has been moistened by the steam in the ear until it becomes small pieces, then remove them with a blind forceps or a delicate probe or by suction.
As we mentioned, it becomes easy to remove them. As for water that enters the ear, the patient should first try sneezing with cumin while filling their ear with some old cotton while lying down on the side with the affected ear. If it comes out this way.
Otherwise, take many small smooth flat stones and warm them slightly over fire. The patient should insert one into the ear canal, lie down with that side elevated, and strike the stone in the ear with another stone.
He should continue doing this with stone by stone until all the water comes out. The water may also be removed by taking papyrus or feathers, inserting one end into the ear, and holding the other end to fire until most of it burns, then repeat with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out, or draw it out with a tube as described previously for stones. As for removing an animal that has entered, if it is small-bodied like a flea or similar, treat it as mentioned in the section on incision.
If its body is large and visible to the touch, try to remove it with hooks and forceps; this is easier than removing all other things that pierce the ear. As for removing worms generated in the ear, if treating them as mentioned in the section on incision in the chapter on fumigations does not work.
You should look into the ear in the sunlight. If any part of the worm is visible, remove it with a hook or delicate forceps. If nothing is visible, take a tube, which looks like this.
Narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, insert the thin end of the tube into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly. Do this repeatedly until all the worm comes out. If it does not come out, seal around the tube with wax as mentioned for stones.
If it does not come out as mentioned, then use the fumigants that I have described and which the ancients have experimented with to kill the worms. You will find this in the article on fumigants. Your pouring of ointments and medicines into the ear is done with this instrument, and here is its shape:.
As you see, it is made from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole and wide at the top. If you want, the cannon inside the tube can be made of solid copper. If you prefer, take a cord and tightly wrap cotton around one end.
Then pour the ointment or juice or any of these medicines into the tube while it is in the ear. Then insert the cord with the cotton on top and squeeze it moderately with your hand so that the ointment is forced into the auditory canal and is felt.
Place it in the affected ear, and let what you pour into the ear be warmed slightly over fire. Be careful that the thing which drips into it is neither too cold nor too hot, as the ear cannot tolerate such extremes.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of obstructive ear conditions.
Some children may be born with their ear canals unperforated.
Some people may also experience blockage in the ear canal due to an injury or the growth of flesh inside it. This blockage may be deep within the ear canal and not visible to the eye.
It may also protrude slightly outside the hole and be visible to the eye. What occurs deep in the ear and is not visible is usually difficult to treat, while what is visible should be treated by exposing the affected ear to the sun. And look at it, and if you see the obstruction apparent, then open it with a delicate scalpel which should be of this form:.
Its end should have a slight width, limited at the edge, and the rest of the scalpel should be smooth on both sides so as not to harm the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate forceps and cut it gently until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep in the ear, take a smooth, delicate probe and warm it slightly over fire. Then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction and the patient feels heaviness in hearing, remove it gently.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum. Then insert a piece of cotton into the ear, sized according to the width of the hole, and wrap it in Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are assured that the wound has healed or take a piece of cotton, moisten it with water, and sprinkle powdered zØ§Ø¬ on it.
Use it. If you see that the ear has become swollen with a hot swelling, you should remove the cotton and replace it with another piece of cotton wrapped in kerotie made with rose oil until the hot swelling subsides, then continue to treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear. Use the rest of the treatment that stops bleeding as mentioned in the section on bloodletting.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry, and they should be grasped with forceps or a pincers and cut out with a scalpel from their roots, then apply powdered zinc to the area.
If blood flows from it, cauterize it with a gentle lens-shaped cautery. Cauterization is better after cutting. They often return if anything of their roots remains. If you cauterize with fire, it burns those roots and they do not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of transient cold in the eyelids.
Often, something that resembles cold in its intensity and hardness appears on the eyelids.
For this reason, it is called cold, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment for this is to examine it; if the cold protrudes on the outer side of the eyelid and can move in all directions, its treatment is easy, so make a transverse incision on it.
Then peel it off gently from all sides until it is removed, then hang it with the pincers and cut it. If you cannot cut it without penetrating the eyelid, that will not harm the patient at all. If the incision is large, sew it up and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no harm, as the ointment will heal and seal it. If the cold leans towards the inner surface of the eyelid, flip the eyelid and hang the cold with pincers without needing to make an incision, then trim it from all sides.
For if the eyelid is perforated by cutting, that does no harm; then wash the eye after removing the bandage with salt water and treat the area with what causes it to heal until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of the boil that appears in the eyelid.
A boil is a swelling that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid, and it most often affects children. It burdens their eyes, causing them to have colds at night, and they are unable to look at sunlight. Because the tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore, you will find them always leaning on their faces and sleeping that way, with their eyelids beneath the eyebrows being moist, swollen, and the boil clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you can feel the boil between them. The procedure involves the patient placing their head in your lap.
Then take a piece of linen, prepare it into a wick, and make a circle from that wick according to the size needed to surround the boil on all sides. Then place it over the boil and press with your fingers from all sides to gather the boil in the center of the circle.
Then make an incision with the lancet in the middle of that moisture across the width, and the cut should not be larger than the one made in the abscess. As for the depth, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the boil.
In most cases, the boil will protrude from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat. Pull it out with a cloth wrapped between your fingers, turning your hand right and left until it is completely removed. Be careful not to enlarge the incision so that the lancet does not reach the eye and harm it.
If the boil does not appear at the beginning of the incision, you should increase the incision slightly gently until the boil appears, then pull it out as we said, then dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area and bandage it with a dressing. Some people crush salt.
He places it in the depth of the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treats it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with soothing bandages until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On Types of Eyeshadow.
If excess eyelashes grow outside the natural course beneath the natural eyelashes and persist, they can harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, drooping of the eyelids, whiteness, and thickening until this becomes a cause for eye impairment.
Eyeshadow application can be done in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or by using a sharp medicine as mentioned previously in the chapter on cauterization, or by cutting andç¼åˆï¼Œæˆ–ç”¨èŠ¦è‹‡ï¼Œæ­£å¦‚æˆ‘å°†è¦æè¿°çš„ã€‚
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then flip the eyelid with your left hand. If it flips, well and good; otherwise, insert a needle with a thread from below the eyelid and bring the needle with the thread through from above, near the hair itself, and pull the thread up with the eyelid.
You flip it with a spatula, then incise inside the eyelid without the excess hair using a sharp scalpel from the larger opening to the smaller opening. Then insert the thread and place a small dressing of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of an oak leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to lift the eyelid, for people may differ in this, with some needing a suitable amount to be cut from the eyelid according to how it has sagged.
Some may need to cut less, all of this according to how the eyelid has sagged. Then, incise with the scalpel along the two lines that have been taught, starting from the larger opening to the smaller one, and each incision should be close to the natural hairline by about the thickness of a needle.
Then insert the forceps into one of the skin corners, then peel it all off. Then bring the two lips together with a needle and fine wool thread for suturing, wipe the blood, and attach any remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substances.
If you wish to do that, otherwise it does not matter. Then leave the sutures and threads for about three or four days, then treat it. If you prefer, you can leave the wound without suturing and treat it with things that dry and constrict, as the eyelid will rise when the wound is sealed and united, but suturing is better.
This method of eyelid retraction was mentioned by the predecessors, except that it can be burdensome for the patient. It is a good technique and has no risk. Another method of eyelid retraction is to shape the eyelid like an oak leaf, as we have described. Then lift the eyelid with three forceps, which can be spaced apart or grouped in this manner:.
Then cut the remaining part of the eyelid with a small scissors in this manner, cutting moderately:.
If you cannot hold the forceps steady and they do not suit you, take a needle with thread and insert it in the center of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger opening and a third thread near the smaller opening, then gather the threads evenly between your fingers and lift them with an even lift.
Lift all the marked skin as we described, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling appears when you cut or incise, relieve that swelling with kerotie and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also turn inside out, and in such a case, you should use what we have mentioned of cutting, suturing, and cauterization with fire and sharp medicine. Cauterization with fire or sharp medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, though cutting and suturing are more skillful in both cases.
Cauterization with reed is done in this manner: you turn the eyelid inside out and make a cut on the inside as described. Then, you make two thin reeds or sticks, their length equal to that of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you notch the ends of both on both sides where the threads will hold. Then, gently bring them together over what remains of the eyelid and tightly secure the reeds on both sides. Leave it for days, as the tightened skin will die, turn black, and rot.
Until it falls off by itself. If it takes longer, use a pair of tweezers to remove it, then treat it until it heals. Once it has healed, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes will not turn inward. This is the shape of the two reeds, made in this form and with the same length and width:
Chapter Twelve.
On removing ingrown eyelashes with a needle.
If there is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle and insert a thread of smooth fine silk into it, then gather its ends and tie them in a very gentle knot. The length of the thread should be about a span.
Then, attach another thin thread shorter than the first and tie its ends if you wish. The patient should place his head in your lap and be positioned near sunlight to clearly see the work as it is delicate. Then, insert the needle with the loop into the root of the excess hair.
Pierce the eyelid quickly with it until it emerges above the natural hair, then pull the thread over the loop and draw the hair into the loop if there is one or two or three hairs, not more.
Then pull with your hand until the hair comes out with the loop from the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the loop and the hair does not come out, pull the loop downward with the thread you attached to it until the loop exits from the upper hole.
Then put the hair back in and pull both. Do this until it rises to the upper hole and you can see it clearly. At that point, remove the loop and thread, secure the eye, and leave it secured for two or three days until the hair stays in place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a long hair from natural hair so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer over time, then lift it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the treatment of sties that occur in the upper eyelid.
Which is affected by this sty is called a harelip, and this sty is either natural or incidental. The incidental one occurs from the healing of a wound, tear, burn, or the like.
The method of treatment is to incise that healing tissue and separate its edges, placing a wick of linen between them, and bind it until it heals. It is not appropriate to use things that dry and constrict in its treatment.
If you do that, the sty will return worse than before. Instead, use things that relax, such as fenugreek and alum in water boiled with figs and flaxseed, and a cerate of diazoeleum dissolved with some oils, apply it to the wick and treat it thus.
The principle of its treatment is to strive by every means that it does not heal in the same condition as before. This action we mentioned is part of correcting the sty, not restoring the place entirely to how it was originally.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of sties that occur in the lower eyelid.
This sty that occurs from below is what is truly called a sty, and it can be natural or incidental. The incidental one results from a wound, tear, burn, or the like.
The method of treatment is to take a needle with a doubled thread, pierce the flesh with it, and pass it from the left side to the right side until the thread is at both ends of the flesh. Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, otherwise take a needle and place it at the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it, and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid so that the ends of the two incisions are from the corners of the cut you made until they meet to form a corner. Until when they come together, its shape becomes similar to this shape:.
It is the Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is lower than what adjoins the eyelid, then join the separated parts with two stitches using wool thread, then treat it with the relaxants and lint we mentioned until it heals.
If the lesion occurred due to an incision, suturing, or cauterization, it should be given a slight incision without hair from the eyelashes as previously mentioned, then separate the two lips with lint as we have described.
In summary, the treatment of a lesion whether it is from above or below should be carried out according to what appears suitable for the form of the lesion, as it may greatly differ in appearance; the skilled practitioner devises the method in whatever way is possible for him.
