Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may affect many people, where the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing it from moving easily and hindering its natural functions; therefore, it is necessary to insert a thin strip under the eyelid.
And raise it upwards or stretch it with a forceps, then cut the adhesion with a delicate scalpel that is not as sharp as regular scalpels but slightly blunt so as not to distress the patient during the procedure, which could cause the scalpel to slip and harm the eye with its sharpness. Your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed. Then, pour salty water into the eye to wash it or drip a solution of zanjabeel dissolved in water into it. Finally, separate the eyelid from the eye with a cotton swab.
Place wool moistened with egg white over the eye, and after the third day, use soaked compresses until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the removal of the ingrown nail and excess flesh around it.
The ingrown nail can be of two types: either neural, which resembles a hard, thin membrane, or non-neural, which resembles a white, solid moisture that breaks when touched by iron or attempted to be removed with forceps.
And no forceps were used in it, and both types begin from the larger orifice until they are inserted little by little until they cover the eye and prevent light and movement of the eye, and the manner of cutting them is that the patient puts his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes and you lift the eyelid with your hand, then grasp the ingrown nail with slightly curved forceps and pull it upwards, then take a needle, insert a hair from horse or cow hair into it along with a strong thread, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce it in the center of the ingrown nail.
And you thread it with the needle and tie the nail with the thread, pull it upwards, and scrape the side of the nail that is next to the quick with the hair as if you are spreading it out with the hair to the end, then cut the remaining part at the base of the orifice with a sharp scalpel or small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the orifice.
So that cutting it does not cause a continuous flow of tears. The ingrown nail can be pulled out with forceps alone or with thread as we said, then scrape the smooth-edged scalpel on the side which looks like this:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the corneal membrane so as not to cause a tear in it which could damage the quick. Then after cutting, put some powdered salt or zingiber officinale into the eye and leave it for another day, then treat it with what is appropriate until it heals.
If the ingrown nail is not painful and you cannot insert a needle into it or secure it with forceps, this type of ingrown nail is often treated with medication until it heals as described in the section. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a delicate, smooth, weak-edged scalpel in this manner:. Shave the nail from above gently with it. If you see that it becomes loose and crumbles due to the shaving, drip a little of zanjari shayaf or red shayaf or powdered salt into the eye and bandage the eye for another day.
Then, repeat the procedure until it is all gone. However, if a hot swelling obstructs the eye, leave it and treat the hot swelling until it heals, then resume the shaving procedure until it is healed. As for the flesh tumor of the meibomian gland.
If the tumor greatly harms the eye, attach that tumor with a hook and cut some of it, but do not overcut to avoid causing tearing. Then drip red shayaf or zanjari shayaf into the meibomian glands until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the sutures and the excess flesh that grows in the eye.
Red flesh may grow in the eyes of some people, accumulating until it covers the pupil or approaches it or overflows onto the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids turn outward, resembling the rose of the jelenar.
The procedure is to have the patient place their head in your lap, then open their eyes, and use appropriate forceps to remove all of that red flesh.
Or grasp it with a forceps or pincers, then cut the flesh bit by bit until all of it is removed by cutting. Be careful not to harm the eye while working. You can cut it either with the scalpel we described for cutting nails or with a small scissors. As with which the bones are set, as its figure will appear in the chapter that follows this chapter.
When the cutting is complete and all inflammation has subsided, fill the eye with finely ground salt or drip into it red vitriol and other similar caustic medicines. Apply cotton smeared with egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent hot swelling.
If any inflammation remains and the blood overpowers you and you fear hot swelling, then leave the eye alone and treat it with what calms the swelling, then return to the procedure until it heals. Do the same for any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this kind.
However, you should avoid treating any eye that is weak by nature or by condition with iron procedures. Do not involve yourself in any of these actions until you see that the organ can withstand such treatment.
Chapter Eighteen.
On removing foreign objects from the eye.
Foreign blood vessels form on the eye, impeding vision and weakening the eye over time. You should first examine whether the eye with these vessels is strong and free of other diseases besides the presence of these vessels.
Then proceed with the extraction, which involves having the patient place their head in your lap. Then grasp those vessels with a pair of tweezers, either one or two, depending on your skill. The tweezers should have delicate curves, like this:
or they could be double tweezers in one body, like this:
Then, with a delicate scissors, pluck those veins gently and wipe the blood from time to time until you see that those veins have gone from the eye and the bleeding has stopped; be careful not to harm the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done in the morning facing the sun.
Be very careful in your work so as not to cut anything other than those veins; when you are finished, drip into the eye green or red chiretta to dissolve with its sharpness what remains of the specks, for if you cannot pluck all of them at that time, bandage the eye with something that soothes the pain.
Leave it for days until its pain subsides and you are sure that the hot swelling is gone, then repeat the procedure on her in exactly the same manner until it heals. This is the image of the scissors alleviating it from pain.
Leave it [and the patient's head should be in the student's lap, and another should dry the tears and examine the vision at the front and back of the eye and you]. The proper way to perform the procedure is to open the eye with fixed or triangular speculum held by a student.
The patient's head should be in the student's lap, and another should dry the tears and examine the vision at the front and back of the eye. You may choose to start plucking from the top of the eye or from the bottom, but it is preferable to dry two areas at the upper side, one at the end of the iris and the other at the beginning.
Hold it between the middle finger and the index finger, then attach specula from below opposite them. Then hold it between the little finger and the ring finger and lift your hand with a light pull until you see all the specks have risen. Cut from the smaller side if you use scissors, or if you prefer, use a delicate lancet.
And you insert the needle with silk, and all the sutures are tightened until they rise above the wound, and do not cut off the ends of the thread, for tears and blood will loosen them.
Then cut with the scissors what extends beyond the crown from above and below until nothing protrudes except in the cornea. If you can lift it from the cornea, peel it with the needle, which is better. It may heal in some people.
And when you cut it, the area will swell for days, and its pain will subside, and the heat of the swelling will diminish, then repeat the procedure on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On restoring the septum to the nose.
Physicians call the septum a nasal polyp. If you treat it with cauterization or with sharp, burning medication as previously described and it does not heal, there is no alternative but to incise the swelling when it ripens and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed. If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument that looks like this:
It is called the rough head, made from Indian iron, with its head rounded like a button, engraved with file or rasp patterns finely. Place it on the corrupted part of the bone and turn it between your fingers while slightly pressing to ensure that the corruption is scraped off.
Do this several times, then treat the area with drying and constricting medications. If the area closes up and flesh grows without the flow of pus continuing for forty days, and no recurrence or inflammation occurs.
And know that if it has healed, then there is no solution except to return the naá¹£Å«r to the nostrilâ€™s opening in this manner: that you expose the bone again with iron or a sharp medicine. Once the bone is exposed, take it out in this form.
The tip of the iron should be triangular and its end conical, drawn to the tip as you see, then place it on the bone itself, and this should be close to the throat but keep your hand a little away from the eye, then rotate your hand with the instrument until the bone is penetrated and the patient feels it.
That is when he finds air coming out of it if he holds his hand over his nose. At that point, treat the area with astringent and drying medicines as I have informed you. Once the flesh has grown and the place has become firm, then the material which was protruding outward will return to the nose, making this easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On correcting eye protrusion.
If the eye protrudes entirely and there is no harm or deficiency in the sight, it should be that you ease the patient, then place them in a basin, then put a leech on the back without restriction, and apply suction gently.
Then place on the eye a poultice made from acacia, storax, frankincense, and zanzibar. Then bind the eye over the dressing with many bandages. The method of the bandages is to take soft cloths, fold each cloth into four folds, and make them plentiful.
Then place them one on top of the other to the extent needed for the eye, then bind them tightly with a strong cloth. Repeat the cupping by suction without restriction, then remove the cup and leave the eye bound for a day and night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened well. Then remove the bandage after a day and night as we said. If you see that the eye has returned to normal, otherwise repeat the dressing, padding, binding, and cupping until it returns.
The Twenty-First Chapter.
On the Removal of the Polyp.
If a hernia appears in the grape-like layer, then it swells and protrudes outside the eyelids resembling a polyp, thereby disfiguring the person's appearance, and you wish to remove it, then the procedure for it is as I describe.
It involves inserting a needle into the base of the polyp from bottom to top, then inserting another needle with a doubled thread on the side of the opening and passing it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then cut the part where the doubled thread bends, tie it around the base of the polyp with the needle, and tighten it well. Then remove the needle and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye. Leave it until the threads fall off with the polyp. Then treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals. The polyp may be tightened from all sides with a circle of cloth until the eye becomes more protruding. Then, use a thin scalpel to reach the depth of the eye so that the egg-like moisture flows out and the eye descends and withers in place. Then bind it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of scabies.
This disease which is called scabies is a discharge that gathers in the eye, resembling flowing water but there is none. The method of treatment is to have the patient sit on a chair upright, then take his head in your hands from both sides.
Move it until you see the discharge move to the bottom of the eye with your eye, then it stabilizes and does not leave, and the light is released, and the patient sees things as he used to see them. If it does not go down, we know that it is water. If the discharge cannot be made to go down as mentioned.
Otherwise, have the patient sit in front of you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision above the corneal layer at the point where the conjunctiva meets the cornea in the limbus until the material is released.
Once it is released, drip warm water mixed with honey or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled into the eye. Then continue the treatment until recovery.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which is suitable for extraction with clear and explained words, so refer to that for accuracy. At this point, you should have the patient sit in front of you, squatting opposite a light source near the sun.
You bind his healthy eye and tighten it well, then lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye that has the cataract is the left one or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the probe near the cornea with the thickness of a needle in the white part of the eye from the side of the smaller aperture, then push the probe forcefully while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates into the white part of the eye and you feel the probe reach an empty space.
It is necessary that the depth to which the probe goes should be equal to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the iris, for you will see the copper through the clarity of the cornea. Then direct the probe upwards to the place where the cataract is.
Then press it down repeatedly; if the cataract descends immediately, the patient will see what his sight opens up to him immediately, with the probe still in his eye. Then let it rest for a while; if the cataract rises again, lower it once more without removing the probe.
When it has settled and does not rise, gently remove the probe while slightly twisting it with your hand little by little. Then dissolve some clear Andranian salt in water and wash the eye with it from the inside. Then apply wool or moistened lint over the entire outer part of the eye.
With rose oil and egg white, you bind it along with the healthy eye. As for us and our time, we apply ground cumin mixed with egg white on it. If the probe does not enter the eye due to its hardness, because there are those among people whose eyes are very hard indeed. It is necessary to take the scalpel which is called the brid which looks like this:
You pierce only the sutures with it and do not deepen the piercing, but rather create a slight opening for the probe, then insert the probe as mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare a secure bed for the patient to lie on his back in a dark room.
He should avoid all movements and coughing, and his food should be what alleviates his condition. He must not turn his head to the right or left at all, and the bandage remains in place until the third day. Then, you remove it in that dark room and test his sight.
Show him things, then reapply the bandage until the seventh day. You should not do this during treatment or after cauterization at your time, but you should avoid it before because water rises quickly with intense looking.
If a hot swelling occurs, you should remove the bandage before the seventh day and treat it with what calms the swelling until it subsides. At that point, release his sight and place a veil on his face through which he can gradually accustom his sight while he remains in that dark room for several days.
He leaves the house gradually and manages his affairs. Know that in cauterization, the learner cannot do without observation multiple times before proceeding with the work. I have heard from some Iraqis that they mentioned making a penetrating probe in Iraq with which water is absorbed.
I have not seen anyone in our country make that, nor have I read about it in the books of the early scholars. It is possible that this is a recent development. Here are images of various types of probes for those who are ignorant of them:.
It is made of copper particularly and its tip should be of this same thinness, sharply pointed and triangular.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of flesh grown in the nose.
Flesh of various kinds may grow in the nose; some resemble many-legged scorpions, others are hard and cancerous with a dark color, and some are soft and not dark in color.
As for the soft flesh that is not harmful or cancerous, it is advisable to have the patient sit before you facing the sun, open his nostril, and insert the forceps into that flesh, then pull it outwards, and cut off what has been grasped with a delicate sharp scalpel from one side.
To ensure that all the flesh has been removed, if any part remains that you cannot cut, scrape it off with a delicate instrument gently until nothing remains. If bleeding persists or inflammation occurs.
Treat it as appropriate or if it is a harmful inflammation, act quickly to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is removed. Then, after cutting, insert vinegar and water or a liquid into the nose. If the nose opens and moisture flows down to the throat.
Know that it has healed if the moisture flows as it should. If the moisture does not flow as it should, know that there is growing flesh inside on top of the loose bones which the instrument could not reach to cut. In this case, you should take a thread of coarse linen.
And make many knots in it, and place between each knot the length of a finger or less. The patient then inserts one end of the thread into his nose with a needle or as best he can after making something like a button, and pulls it until it reaches the nasal passage.
And it comes out at his throat. This action is often done by children in school, and it is easy for whoever wants to do it. Then gather the two ends of the thread, one end that came out of the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the thread with knots to cut the flesh. Do this until you know that the flesh has been severed by the knots on the thread. Then remove the thread and, after cleaning the blood in the nose, insert a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh. Then, the last thing to be placed in the nose is a lead tube for several days until it heals. If drying treatment is needed, use that method.
And this is an image of the instrument used to drip oils and medicines into the nose:.
He places a small lamp made of silver or brass, open and lubricated, with its flow also lubricated.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed and lubricate the flat applicator.
It has a handle at the end as you see, to hold when heating oil or any other liquids or flowing substances.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On warts growing at the end of the nose.
Warts often grow at the end of the nose, increasing in size over time until their appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it should be cut off at its first appearance and completely removed. Then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine that can replace cauterization to the area. If it is not cut off until it grows large, examine it; if it is hard, solid, of a dark color, and less sensitive.
Do not treat it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor; I have often seen that cutting this tumor results in great harm to the patient. If the tumor feels soft upon touch and is not dark in color, and you find that it can be entirely cut out. Purge the patient, and cut it without hesitation. Treat the area with substances that dry and constrict until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On sewing the nose, lip, and ear. If the connection is severed due to a wound or the like, know that when there is a separation in the connection of any of these cartilages, it rarely responds well to treatment except in some people.
Therefore, when such a thing is presented to you, if the wound is fresh with blood, bring the edges of the wound together by suturing and treat it until it heals. If the separation has caused the edges to part and each side has become healthy, then you should incise each side to its thickness until it bleeds.
Then the lips are sewn together and tightened, and powdered sulfur and frankincense are sprinkled on it. A plaster of unguent or another adhesive ointment is placed over it, and it is left tight for two or three days, then it is released.
The medicine is changed until the stitches fall off by themselves, then it is treated with ointment until it heals. The method of sewing is to bring together what has been separated either with needles as described in stitching the abdomen or with thread as was explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing knots that appear in the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling a cherry kernel in size, while others are smaller or larger. It is advisable to turn the lip, incise each knot, and attach it with a forceps.
It is cut from all sides, then the area is filled with powdered zinc after cutting until the bleeding stops. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with a constricting agent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On Removing Excess Tissue from the Gums.
Often, excess tissue grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis. It is advisable to grasp it with forceps or hold it with a probe and cut it at its base. Allow the blood to flow out, then apply powdered zinc to the area.
Or one of the astringent drying agents. If that flesh returns after treatment, it often returns; cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On Scaling Teeth with Iron.
Hard, ugly scales may form on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums. These can turn black, yellow, or green until they cause damage to the gums and make the teeth unsightly.
It is advisable that the patient sit before you with their head in your lap. You should clean the molar and tooth where the scales and sand-like particles appear until nothing remains. Do the same for black, green, yellow, and other discolorations until it is clean.
If what is inside is removed with the first scaling, otherwise repeat the scaling on another day, a second and third time until you reach your goal. Know that the molar requires various forms and many shapes of scalers according to what is suitable for your work.
For the scaler used to clean the molar from the inside is different from the one used for the outside, and the one used between the molars has another form. Here are several forms of scalers that you should have prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On Extracting Teeth.
It is advisable to treat the molar for pain with every method and hesitate to extract it, for there is no replacement for it once extracted, as it is a noble essence. Even if extraction becomes inevitable.
It is necessary if the patient decides to extract it that you ensure until you find the aching tooth sound. For often the pain deceives the patient, and he thinks it is in the healthy tooth, so he extracts it, but the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted. We have seen barbers do this many times.
When you have accurately identified the painful tooth, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong instrument until you separate the gum from all sides, then move it with your fingers or with delicate forceps gradually little by little until you loosen it.
Then take hold of it firmly with the larger forceps, and have the patient's head between your knees so that he is immobilized. Then pull the tooth straight out to avoid breaking it. If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, insert it gently under the tooth from all sides, and rock it as you did at first.
If the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill that hole with a piece of cloth and secure it well with a thin strip of linen to prevent it from crumbling when you grip it with the forceps. You should also carefully incise around the gum on all sides.
Be careful with your force so as not to break it, leaving a part behind, which would cause the patient greater harm than his initial pain. Beware of acting like ignorant barbers who recklessly extract teeth without using the methods we have described.
For often they inflict great disasters on people, the least of which is that a tooth may break and some or all of its roots remain. Or it may pull out some of the jawbones as we have witnessed many times. Then, after its extraction, he rinses with a drink or with vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site, which often happens, then grind some zadj and apply it to the site.
Otherwise, use it if the zadj does not help you. The image of the delicate forceps used to move the tooth first has long ends and a short handle, thick so that it does not bend when you grip the tooth with it. This is the image of the larger forceps:
It should be as you see, with thick handles so that when you grip it, it does not yield or bend. The ends should be short, made of Indian iron or well-tempered steel with tips that have teeth which fit into each other, allowing for a strong and tight grip.
The ends may also be made like a file, which makes them strong and precise in grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On Extracting Tooth Roots and Removing Broken Jawbones.
If after extracting a tooth, a broken root remains, it is advisable to place cotton with fat on the site for one or two days until the area relaxes, then you can insert the wax or forceps into it. which have ends resembling the mouth of a bird called the blerga. This is the image of the forceps:
It has been made like a scraper from the inside or like a catheter, and if these forceps do not work for extraction, you should dig at the root and remove all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert an instrument that resembles a small lever, which looks like this:
Short at the end, slightly thick, and not brittle so that it does not break. If the root comes out with this, otherwise, use these other instruments whose shapes are as follows, the first is triangular at the end and somewhat thick:

Triangular at the curved end, somewhat thick to avoid breaking, and not brittle. Know that tooth instruments are numerous, as are other tools; they are almost innumerable. The skilled and experienced craftsman may invent his own tools according to what his work and the diseases themselves suggest.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not describe instruments due to the variety of their types. If a bone in the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth breaks or becomes rotten, examine it in its place with one of these suitable instruments or forceps mentioned for extracting roots.
And we use this instrument, which looks like this:
It should have some slight thickness to hold the bone so it does not slip until the bone comes out, and we set the area with appropriate medications. If there is a little corruption in the bone, clean it of its corruption and blackness until it is purified, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the Eruption of Teeth in Unusual Places.
If the teeth grow outside their natural path, they spoil the appearance, especially if this happens in women and slaves. It should be examined; if a tooth has grown behind another tooth.
And if it cannot be erupted or repressed, extract it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, which has this shape:.
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron. Your cutting should be done over many days due to the hardness of the tooth, so as not to disturb the other teeth. If it protrudes firmly due to its coldness, cool it with a file that looks like this.
It should be entirely made of Indian steel, with a very fine edge like the file used for making needles. Cool the tooth gradually over many days with gentle pressure so as not to disturb the tooth and cause it to fall out. Finally, smooth it and clean it with some scrapers.
If a tooth is broken and hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed until the roughness of the break is smoothed out and does not hurt the tongue or hinder speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On securing loose teeth with silver threads or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything edible for fear that they will fall out, and treatment with constricting medicines has not been effective.
The solution is to secure them with a gold or silver thread, and gold is better because silver can corrode and decay after a few days, whereas gold remains unchanged forever and does not suffer from such issues. The thread should be of moderate thickness, suitable enough to fit between the teeth.
The method of securing is to take the thread and insert its bend between two healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until the weaving reaches another healthy tooth on the other side.
Then, return the weaving to the side where you started and pull gently and wisely so that they do not move at all, ensuring that the thread is tight at the roots of the teeth so it does not come loose. Then, cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, bring them together, twist them tightly, and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth.
So that the tongue is not irritated, then leave it tied this way as long as needed. If it loosens or breaks, secure it again with another thread, and thus enjoy it for a lifetime. This is how the teeth are arranged and how they should be secured between two healthy teeth and two loose teeth as you see.
And it may happen that one or two molars return to their place after falling and interlock as we have described and remain. This is only done by a skillful craftsman, who may carve bone from the bones of an ox and make it in the shape of a molar and place it in the spot where the molar has gone. And it is tightened as we have said, so it remains and one benefits from it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament that appears under the tongue and hinders speech.
This ligament that appears under the tongue may either be natural, with which a person is born, or it may be accidental from a wound that has healed.
The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth, placing their head in your lap, lifting the tongue, then cutting the transverse nerve ligament so that the tongue is released from its restraint. If there is some stiffness or knotting and this is due to a healed wound, insert a forceps, make a transverse incision until the ligament is severed and the knot is loosened.
Be careful not to make the incision deep into the flesh so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding. After the cutting, rinse with rose water or vinegar and cold water. Then place a lint wick of linen under the tongue that the patient holds each night.
To prevent it from healing again, if bleeding occurs, apply powdered zajah to the area. If the bleeding persists, cauterize the area with a lentil-shaped cautery suitable for this purpose. Then treat it with all treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On extracting the toad developed under the tongue.
A swelling that resembles a small toad may occur under the tongue, preventing it from its natural function, and it might grow until it fills the mouth. The procedure is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and examine the swelling.
If you find it dark or black and hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not intervene with it for it is cancer. If it leans towards whiteness and has moisture, then pierce it with a probe and cut it with a fine lancet and extract it from all sides.
If the blood overpowers you while working, apply powdered zaj to it until the bleeding stops. Then continue your work until you remove it completely, and then rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the treatment of tonsil swelling.
And what grows in the throat from other swellings may present glands inside the throat that resemble those appearing externally, called tonsils. If treating them with what I mentioned in the division does not heal them,
then examine it. If the swelling is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not treat it with instruments. If it is red in color and its base is thick, do not treat it with instruments either, fearing bleeding. Instead, leave it until it matures; either apply a poultice to it or wait for it to rupture on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut. The procedure is that you look before the operation to see if its hot swelling has completely subsided or decreased somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun and place his head in your lap, open his mouth, and take an assistant who will hold down his tongue downwards with an instrument that looks like this:.
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue with it and locate the swelling, once it comes into your sight, take a forceps, pierce it into the tonsil, and pull it out as much as possible without pulling any membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument that looks like this: it resembles scissors, but its ends are bent so that the tip of each is very sharp against the other, made from Indian iron or Damascus steel:.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a lancet that looks like this:.
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. After cutting, the patient should gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or oak leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then treat him until he heals. Sometimes other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; these should be cut in the same manner as described for cutting the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that had grown inside her throat, extending towards the larynx, somewhat insensitive, nearly blocking the throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and it had prevented her from eating and drinking water.
And she was on the verge of death had she remained like that for a day or two. The tumor had sprouted two branches that had come out through her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserting a clamp into one of them and pulled, extracting a piece successfully.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril hole, and then did the same for what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pushed her tongue aside, inserted the clamp into the tumor itself, and cut some of it, with only a little blood flowing out.
The woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water, then took some food. We continued to cut the tumor repeatedly for a long time, with the tumor regenerating what was cut off, so that this went on for a long period between us.
I resorted to cauterizing the tumor inside the throat, which stopped its growth. Then I left that place and did not know what happened to her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On Cutting the Tumor of the Tonsil.
What is called a "bunch" when a phlegm descends to the tonsil and swells, if it is white and elongated, it is called a pillar; if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called a bunch. If it is treated with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment does not work.
I saw the hot swelling had subsided from it and it was thin, so it should be cut. And what of it was gathered round and had no length, and was blood-colored or bluish or black or without feeling, then it should be avoided from being cut.
In it, you must operate on the patient; therefore, if you see it as described, white and long, sit the patient facing the sun, press his tongue with the instrument previously described, then insert the probe into the growth.
Pull it down and cut it with one of the two instruments I mentioned for cutting the tonsils. It is advisable to only cut what has exceeded the natural limit without excess, because if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
After the cutting, use what we described for cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is reluctant to have it cut, you should employ a method to cauterize it without fear or hesitation. The method of cauterization involves using a sharp remedy, placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then press his tongue with the instrument we mentioned. Take some sharp water as described in the cauterization section and mix it with unslaked lime, not too thick nor too thin. Fill the groove of this instrument, and here is its shape:
One end of it, where you put the medicine, should have a groove like that of a spoon. Place the instrument with the medicine directly on the uvula itself while the patient lies on their side so that saliva flows out of his mouth from inside his throat, so that nothing goes down into his throat and causes him harm.
Then hold the medicine in your hand and squeeze it on the tooth for about half an hour until you see that it has turned black and the pain of the medicine's bite has subsided, and if you want, take some cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, and soak the cotton in the medicine.
Insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton sticks to the tooth. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired effect of cauterizing the gum. Then leave it, as it will wilt and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, do so.
After cauterization, wipe around the gum with cotton soaked in fat and dry the area around it of the medicine. Then rinse with cold water and treat externally with poultices and internally with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and lessens fear.
Teeth can also be treated with a milder method than cauterization and cutting; they can be treated with fumigation as follows: take frankincense, zofa, thyme, sudab, shih, chamomile, qiscam, and similar herbs. Gather all of them or some of them in a pot and submerge them in vinegar.
Boil the pot, which is sealed with clay, and make a hole in the center of the lid. Attach a hollow instrument to this hole, which can be made of silver or copper.
Insert the end with the funnel into the patient's mouth so that the vapor rises to the tooth through the tube until the gum swells well. Then repeat this several times until it wilts. Do not perform this treatment at the first onset of swelling.
For it often increases the swelling, and it should only be done when the hot swelling has subsided. If this instrument is not available, take a reed and attach a chicken eggshell to one end so that the mouth of the patient does not burn.
For the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is good for treatment with safety.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On the removal of thorns and other objects that pierce the throat.
Bone or fishbone or other objects often pierce the throat; you should remove what is visible to the eye after pressing the tongue with an instrument towards the sun.
So that you may see what is in the throat; for what does not appear and hides in the throat, it is advisable to induce vomiting before the food is digested in the stomach, as the piercing object might come out with the vomit, or the patient can swallow a piece of lettuce or the root of celery.
Or swallow a bite of dry bread, or take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a string, then swallow it. When it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly. Do this several times.
For the thorn or bone often sticks to it and comes out. If it does not come out as described, then use a lead instrument of this form:.
It should be slightly thicker than a probe and have a bent tip. The patient should gently insert it into their throat with their head raised upwards, being careful not to touch the larynx to avoid causing a cough, using it to push out the bone or thorn, or the doctor can insert it with their hand.
And introducing the patient to it is better for his knowledge of the location of the embedded object and push downwards or pull his hand up with the tool, all of that according to what is suitable until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On Removing Thorns Embedded in the Throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on treatment with medicines and it does not work, then examine the patient's throat when the sun is up after you press his tongue with the tool that was described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a fine, firm tweezers. If you cannot do so, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert a heated iron into the tube.
Do this several times, and the patient should abstain from water for the entire day. Then, he should take a vessel filled with cold water, open his mouth in it, rinse with it without swallowing a drop, and move the water around occasionally with his hand.
The leech will fall off when it senses the water. If it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and alum using the tool described for inhalations. Do this several times, and it will fall off.
The method for fumigation is to take a pot with hot charcoal, and the pot should be covered with a lid that has a hole in the middle. Attach one end of the tool to this hole, then add the fumigant. The patient places his mouth on the other end of the tube and seals it so that no smoke escapes until he feels that the fumigation has reached his throat.
For the leech falls off at its place, if it does not fall off, then the incense is repeated several times and the patient endures thirst and eats salty and garlic food without drinking water, so it must come out by this management. And this is a figure of an instrument that attracts the leech from the throat when it is seen.
It resembles forceps as you see, except that it has this curved part that enters the throat and its ends resemble a bird's mouth with the roughness of a file; once it grips something, it does not let go.
And another instrument used to press the tongue, it is a wide iron ring as wide as the mouth above the tongue. It enters the mouth standing on its side so that the mouth remains open against the patient's will and they cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth to this same measurement.
Chapter Forty.
In it, there is discussion about treating tumors and incising them.
Tumors have many diverse types, which will be mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in their treatment and incision in two aspects; one of them is the type of tumor itself and what moisture it contains.
The second aspect is due to the body parts where they occur, because a tumor occurring in the head is different from a tumor occurring in the buttocks, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy area is different from a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own specific treatment.
There are tumors that should not be lanced until the pus within them has ripened and is complete. There are also those that should be lanced when they are nearly ripe but have not completely matured, such as tumors that are close to joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and persists until the surrounding area putrefies, it may corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing the limb to become paralyzed. Or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
For if you delay lancing it until it ripens, you will harm the vital organ. Or if it is near the rectum, lance it when it is partially ripe but not fully matured, especially those tumors near the rectum, so that the pus does not spread and penetrate inside the rectum, turning into an abscess.
And it becomes a condition that does not heal. You should know the time to lance tumors that have fully ripened, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever disappears, the redness decreases, and the swelling of the head of the tumor hardens, along with other signs.
You should place the incision at the lowest part of the tumor if possible, so that it is easier for the pus to drain downward, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor. The incision should be made longitudinally along the body if the tumors are on parts like the hands and legs.
And in areas of muscles, tendons, nerves, and blood vessels, generally in all flat areas without bends. As for those that bend, the incision should follow the contour of that area. If the tumor is in fleshy areas, it is best to avoid making an incision.
Until it is fully mature as we have said completely, for if you cut it before that, the pus will continue to flow from it for a long time and be very harmful and dirty, and its lips and bottom might become hard, and some swellings may spread across the body when necessary or according to what the limb needs.
You should use a single incision for small swellings and a wide incision or multiple slashes according to the size of the large swelling. There may be some swellings that require the skin to be punctured and cut if it has become like a rag.
It has happened to some extent that it has died, similar to what occurs in many abscesses and hidings. Some are split into a three-cornered shape, and some are cut in the shape of a plant leaf, like the Arab swelling.
Some require a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar types of incisions. For those without a head, such as flat smooth swellings, only a light incision should be made. If the swelling is large and has lasted for a long time after being incised, do not rush to extract all the pus at once.
Rather, extract some of it, then allow the swelling to constrict until another day, then extract some more pus. Do this gradually several times until all of it is extracted.
This is especially true if the patient is weak, a pregnant woman, a small child, or an elderly person, as the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden release of pus, which may cause the patient to die without you realizing it. Be greatly cautious regarding this matter.
After you have incised these tumors, it is necessary to clean the wound and examine it. If the tumorâ€™s cut is small or if the incision is simple, then use linen or old cotton for bandaging. If the tumor is large and there are many incisions, [continue with appropriate treatment].
You should insert a wick into each incision until they all reach each other. If some of the skin has been cut away from the tumor or if it has been hollowed out, you should fill it with old cotton or the fuzz of linen without moisture and secure it for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a piece of linen in them and apply it to the bleeding area several times.
If the bleeding continues, you should use treatments and remedies which we have described in many places in this book and from the division. If you are in winter time and the tumor site has many nerves, you should soak the bandages with a hot drink and oil.
Place them on the area. If you are in summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply bandages soaked in cold water and oil or a cold drink and oil. On the third day, as we said, you should remove the dressing and clean the tumor.
Use similar treatments for its healing until it is cured. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the details, I have described each tumor and the way to treat it in separate, summarized sections.
Chapter Forty-One.
On incising tumors that appear on the scalp.
Tumors of small size appear on the scalp, and they are among the types of swellings contained in membranes that have sacs like a chicken's egg. Their types are numerous; some are fatty, and others contain moisture similar to mud.
Some contain moisture similar to pus and broth, and so on. Some are hard and solid. All of them can be safely incised and removed unless an artery is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to probe them first with the instrument that will be shown after this, which is called a lancet, to determine their contents. If they contain moisture, then incise them longitudinally with a simple cut in this manner:.
Begin the incision from line ~B~ to line ~C~. Once the moisture has drained, peel and remove the sac that contained the moisture entirely, leaving nothing behind, for it often recurs if any part remains.
Then, dip some cotton in the ointment if available, or in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it for another day as it will dissolve any remaining parts of the sac. Then, replace the cotton with the ointment again, and a third time if necessary.
Continue until you are certain that no part of the sac remains. At that point, treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains fatty matter, incise it in this manner:.
Make a firm incision and place the forceps on both sides of the wound and peel it from all directions and strive to remove the peritoneum which contains it, if an artery obstructs you then do what was described for you thereafter, and similarly deal with the tumor if it is hard in the incision.
The treatment is the same as I mentioned, and incising the hardened tumor is easier because it has less blood and moisture. Some of these tumors in the head in some people have no moisture at all; this is because I have incised a tumor that was.
I incised a tumor on an old woman's head and found the tumor to be like solid, rough, white stone; no one could break it, and if thrown, it would injure. None of the other tumors that occur in the head are like this, such as the tumors that appear on children's heads.
At the base of the ears, make a shallow incision all around and ensure its inside is directed downwards to facilitate the flow of the material downward. Then treat it with appropriate treatment.
The Forty-Second Chapter.
On Incising Boils That Often Appear in the Neck: These boils often appear in the neck, under the armpits, and on the shoulders. They can also appear throughout the body. Those that appear in the neck may be one or many, and some may develop from others.
Each boil is enclosed within its own peritoneal sac, similar to how they occur in tumors and head swellings as described. There are many types of these boils; some are hardened, some contain moisture, and others are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
What I have seen of it is in good condition upon touch, its surface is close to the color of the skin, it moves in every direction, and it was not attached to the neck's nerve nor to the thyroid nor to an artery, and it was not sunken. It is necessary to make a simple incision from top to bottom of the body in this form, from line ~G~ to line ~B~:.
And peel it from all sides and stretch the edges of the skin with forceps or with two forceps or with many forceps if you need that, as we said in head tumors, and pull it out little by little, and make sure not to cut a vein or nerve, and let the scalpel not be very sharp.
So that your hand does not excessively cut or disturb the patient so that you cut what you do not need to cut. If you cut a vein or an artery and it hinders you from working, then place in the wound powdered zaj or some hemostatic agents that stop the blood.
And suture the wound and leave it until the intensity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to show signs of putrefaction, for the blood will stop at that time, then return to your work until you finish. Then examine with your index finger if any more small ones remain, cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the growth, you should not cut it from its root but should tie it with a doubled tight thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without causing harm. Then fill the wound with old cotton that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment and then treat it.
And if the entire boar's [tooth or part] is cut off, it is necessary to bring together the lips of the wound and suture it immediately after you are certain that no fragment remains. If you see that a fragment of the boar's bone remains, [you should address it].
It is necessary to aim with the incision for its root and base, and use suturing as we mentioned. For those wounds from boar bites that contain moisture, lightly bandage them where you see maturation occurring, and make the bandage toward the lower side as we said.
Then after the bandage, use the Egyptian ointment and similar to consume any remaining corruption until you see the wound is clean, then treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Three.
On incising the throat for a swelling that occurs inside the neck.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it, and this is the pattern of their speech. As for those with goiter, they should avoid incising the throat as they would not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and lungs are weakened, as for those who have a hot swelling in the mouth or throat or tonsils, if there is no issue with the trachea, it is necessary to use incision of the throat to avoid the damage that results from suffocation.
If we intend to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the trachea with a small transverse cut between two rings, similar to the incision in the peritoneum and not in the cartilage, and this location is appropriate for the incision.
For it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the surgeon is timid, he should stretch the skin of the throat with a speculum then cut the skin until he reaches the windpipe, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he cuts the peritoneum that we described, and determines the incision of the windpipe by the phlegm that comes out with what is extracted, and from the cessation of sound. The wound is left open for a time, and when the period of fear of suffocation has passed, the edges of the wound are sutured together from the skin.
And outline it alone without the cartilage, then use medicines that promote flesh growth until it heals. The author of this book says, the explanation of this statement we have quoted is that if they see the patient's throat has been blocked by one of these tumors they mentioned.
When the patient is near death and his breath is about to cease, they proceed to incise the larynx so that the patient may breathe through the wound, thus saving him from death. The wound is left open until the course of the disease is completed, which usually lasts for three days or so.
Then they ordered the wound to be sutured and treated until it healed. What I witnessed myself was a servant who took a knife and directed it at her throat, cutting part of the windpipe. I was called to treat her and found she was gasping like a slaughtered animal, so I exposed the wound.
I found that the blood coming out of the wound was minimal, so I was certain that no artery or vein had been cut. The air was escaping from the wound, so I immediately sutured the wound and treated it until it healed. The servant experienced nothing further except a hoarseness in the voice, nothing more.
And after days, it returned to its best condition, so from this, we say that splitting the larynx has no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On incising the tumor that appears in the throat from the outside, which is called pharyngeal polyp.
This tumor, which is called pharyngeal polyp, is a large tumor of the body's color and it is common in women. It comes in two types.
Either it is natural or incidental. As for the natural one, there is no remedy for it. As for the incidental one, it comes in two types: one similar to fatty tumors, and the other similar to the tumor that arises from the entanglement of arteries, and incising it is dangerous. You should not treat it with iron at all, except for the small ones. If you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty tumor and is not attached to any veins, then incise it as you would a fatty tumor. Extract it along with the cyst if it is enclosed in a cyst, otherwise remove all of it, then treat the area as appropriate with the necessary treatment.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On incising various types of tumors.
Tumors have many types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as it can be confusing. I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and alarming pains until the inflammation subsides. The infection completes, then the fever and severity subside, and the wound has no heat, no fever, and no pains. It is enclosed in a peritoneal sac which is its special container, and it takes on the color of the body. Its beginning is like a chickpea and becomes like a fig, larger or smaller.
It comes in two types, either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are diverse as mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you proceed to treat the wound, you should first probe and examine it with an instrument called the medsa, whose image will be shown in the section that follows this one.
Measure the size of the swelling, then withdraw the medsa and look at what comes out in its wake. If moisture of any color flows out, make a slight incision as I mentioned for other swellings. If no moisture comes out after the medsa, know that it is fatty.
Make a firm incision on it in this manner as I have informed you, and suspend it with forceps. Gently peel the skin from all sides and be cautious with the sac; if possible, try to remove it intact along with the swelling.
If the sac is ruptured during the procedure and you cannot remove it intact, then it often happens that way. Remove it piece by piece until nothing remains of it, for if any part of it remains, whether little or much, the swelling will return most of the time.
If it overpowers you and a small part remains, then widen the incision at the end with some sharp corrosive agents, suture the wound tightly, and apply something to reduce the hot swelling on top of it. Treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the swelling is large, sew its edges and treat it with what promotes healing.
If you encounter a vein, whether it is pulsating or not, and blood flow occurs, immediately apply ground zadj to the area and leave it tied for two or three days until the wound becomes putrefied and the boiling of the blood subsides. Then return to cutting what remains of the ligament.

Chapter Forty-Nine.

Chapter Fifty-One.
On Removing Warts That Appear on the Belly.
Warts, called fungal warts due to their resemblance to mushrooms, often appear on people's bellies and other parts of their bodies. Their base is thin and their head is thick, sometimes with swollen edges. They can be small or grow extremely large.
I have seen a man with two warts on his belly that resembled mushrooms the most. They had white, thin bases and swollen edges that were cracked, with moisture constantly seeping from them. I cut them off, and one weighed about eighteen ounces while the other weighed around six ounces.
In cutting them, you should examine if the affected area is moist and if the color of the wart is white, moist, and has a thin base. Cut it with a wide lancet, and have the cautery ready in the fire, as cutting often causes a great deal of bleeding.
If the bleeding is profuse, immediately cauterize it. If you see that the patient is timid and frightened by cutting with iron, take a strand of tightly wound lead wire, tie it around the wart with this description, and leave it for two days. Then increase the tightness of the lead wire, continuing to tighten it regularly.
Whenever it affects the tumor until it separates and falls by itself without effort, if the tumor has a thick base then there is some fear and risk in cutting it, especially what is in the abdomen; however, it may be possible to cut half of it or part of it.

Chapter Fifty-Two.
On the Treatment of Umbilical Hernia.
Umbilical hernia can result from various causes: either a split in the abdominal fascia through which the intestine or bowel protrudes, as seen in other hernias; or blood emerging from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or air that becomes trapped within it.
If it results from a split in the abdominal fascia and the protrusion of the intestine, then the color of the swelling will be similar to the color of the body, it will be soft without pain, and its appearance will differ with various positions. If it results from the protrusion of the bowel, then its appearance will vary even more as described. When you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns. It may also be accompanied by rumbling, and it often enlarges when entering a bath or experiencing severe fatigue. If it results from moisture, then it will be soft.
It does not disappear when you press it with your hand and does not increase or decrease. If it results from blood, then in addition to these signs, the swelling will appear blackish. If it results from newly grown flesh, the swelling will be hard and firm, remaining constant in size.
If it is due to wind, his touch will be soft. In this case, you should look; if the navel protrusion is due to blood from arteries or veins or wind, you should refrain from treating it, for there is fear and risk in that, as I informed you in the chapter where I mentioned the swellings that occur due to arteries or veins.
If the navel protrusion is due to the intestine or hernia, you should order the patient to hold himself and stand upright extended, then mark with ink around the entire navel. If you find that it has been taken by the band, loosen the tourniquet and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find a hernia, stretch it with forceps.
And cut the excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, tie it well and return to your work. Take two needles, insert a strong thread into each, and insert the two needles into the incision you made around the swelling, passing through them, then tighten the swelling at four points on the needles.

Or if there is moisture, the swelling should be lanced as we said and the blood or moisture found there should be drained, then treat it with what causes the area to heal.

Chapter Sixty.

Some are smooth like acorns and rounded, making their exit easy. As for those with angles and edges, increase the incision slightly; if they still do not come out this way, it is necessary to maneuver them either by grasping them with a tightly held instrument whose tip is like an abrasive.
