All these instruments, the more their types and the better they are prepared by the maker, the faster he can work and the higher his skill is regarded by people. Do not disdain any instrument from being prepared, as there will inevitably be a need for it.
Chapter Seventy-Eight.
On the Extraction of the Afterbirth.
If the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, it is necessary to instruct the patient to hold herself and then tickle her nose with cinder, and cover your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out by this method, otherwise take a quantity.
Make a hole in the lid and place the herbs that open the womb, such as fennel, thyme, aniseed, camomile, hyssop, silicium, and centaury. Use all of these medicines or some of them, soak them in water, then heat it over fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole of the pot lid and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, then tickle as we said, for the afterbirth will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in bear grease or lion's saliva.
Then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it. If she finds it, she should grasp it and pull it out slowly. If it is attached deeply in the womb, insert the hand as described until you find the afterbirth, then pull it out little by little gently.
So that the womb does not fall out due to strong pulling, it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then increase the amount of pulling, for it will respond at that time and become free from attachment. If the mouth of the womb is closed, we have described the treatment by tickling and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, do not be forceful in attempting to pull it again. Instead, tie what has come out of it to the mouth of the womb and to the woman's thigh, then apply the quadruple ointment, for it will cause it to detach after a few days.
And it dissolves and exits except that if it putrefies, then a bad smell rises from it to the stomach and head, which hurts the patient, so it is necessary to use appropriate fat.
Therefore, some of the early ones have tried the smoke of pitch and dried figs, a picture of the machine with which the woman is fumigated when menstruation and the afterbirth are retained and similar conditions.
It is made from lead resembling a funnel or it is made from copper, the thin end is placed in the front and the wide end over fire, and the incense prepared on coal is held until that incense is gone and then more is repeated.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the treatment of unperforated piles.
Many boys are born with their anus unperforated, blocked by a thin membrane. The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger, or if not, use a sharp instrument to cut it, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she places wool soaked in the drink and oil on it, then treats it with ointments until it heals. If you fear it may close up, place a lead tube in the hole for many days and remove it when the child needs to defecate.
The anus may also become blocked due to wound healing or a swelling, so that healed area should be incised and then treated with the treatments mentioned, including the use of lead.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of abscesses that occur below.
Abscesses that occur below are a complication and thickening that happens near the anus from the outside or in the space on one of the sides, and the abscess may be one or more.
If that complication persists for a long time, it may rupture and discharge a white watery fluid or thin pus. Some of these abscesses may penetrate to the rectum and intestines, while others do not penetrate. The penetrating ones can be identified by the stool and gas that are discharged when the patient defecates.
Worms may also emerge from them, and some of these abscesses may penetrate to the bladder and urethra if they are in the space. Some may penetrate to the hip joint and tailbone. The penetrating abscess that reaches the anus can be distinguished from the non-penetrating ones.
Insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe made of copper or iron into the abscess if there is no bend in it. If there is a bend, then insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it.
Until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and no stool, gas, or worms emerge from the opening as we mentioned, then know that it is not penetrating. Proceed promptly to treatment.
If the abscess penetrates to the bladder or the urinary tract, the sign is the discharge of urine from it and the failure of the area to heal with medication. If it penetrates to the hip joint or tailbone, the indication is that the probe reaches those areas.
If there is no access to the bone and the pain is located around the area, and pus constantly comes out of it, and treatment is ineffective, and it does not heal with ointment, these penetrating abscesses do not heal at all.
Treating them is futile hardship for those physicians who recklessly attempt it. As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, there is hope for their cure with the iron as I will describe and what I have tested, which is to lay the patient down in front of you on his back.
Lift his legs up and let his thighs slope towards his abdomen. Then, insert a copper or lead probe into the abscess if there is an angle to determine where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the rectum, you should insert your middle finger into the rectum.
If you feel the probe passing through on its own, uncovered, without sensing any intervening membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then be certain that it is penetrating. Do not trouble yourself further, for it will not heal as we have said.
It has been said that it may heal in some people, though rarely. Among the treatments that may be beneficial is to heat a thin iron on according to the size of the abscess as previously mentioned, and insert it hot into the abscess until it reaches near the rectum, then repeat this two or three times.
Until you know that all the excess flesh that has formed, resembling a bird's quill tube, has been burned off. Then treat it with a linen soaked in fat until the burned flesh comes out. Then treat it with healing ointments, and if it heals.
And if it does not heal with any other treatment, and if you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the socket, and there is a thick veil of flesh or membrane between them, and you see the fistula extending below the skin surface, then incise the skin from the beginning of the fistula. And you pass through the incision with the probe while it is in the fistula until you reach where the end of the probe is, and the probe is removed and falls out, then clean those encrusted flesh that resemble a tube of bird's feathers and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the blood overpowers you and hinders you from performing your task of cutting that flesh, the best thing to do and the quickest benefit is to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine, for cauterization with fire combines two good conditions: burning off that excess flesh.
And stop the bleeding and dry the moistures, then treat it with linen soaked in fat or sulfur powdered with oil until pus forms and the burned flesh comes out with the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that promote the growth of strong flesh.
These are the medicines that constrict and dry. For if it heals, it is cured, and there will be no recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the socket and is away from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the socket and explore it.
If you feel the probe and there is a veil of membrane or flesh between you and it, and it is close to passing through, then the treatment should be one of hope and optimism. This means using one of three methods: either cauterize it as we said.
And either you cut it until you reach its bottom so that you can introduce the thread and treatment closely, and do not extend the cutting so as not to sever the muscle surrounding the anus, causing the patient to involuntarily release feces. Then treat him with what we mentioned; perhaps he will be cured as we said.
And either you penetrate that membrane and pierce it after cutting it close to the anus as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp instrument, then clean all the clotted flesh in the incision as much as you can by searching.
Then sew the entire wound including the incision and the upper opening until it is sealed, leaving the fistula open inside the anus, which is less burdensome for the patient. The fistula may perforate in this manner.
This is when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is on one side of the anus toward the body surface with the skin and the anal ring. At that point, take a probe with a perforated tip like a cobbler's needle in this manner:.
And insert a twisted thread made of five threads or so, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches the bottom. If it penetrates the anal canal from inside and is close by.
Then extract the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and pulling out the end of the thread. Gather both ends together and tighten them, leaving it for a day or two. Every time the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads is severed and falls off. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the fistula's mouth is not penetrable, make it penetrable as much as you can, except if it is deep, for then you must not do so to avoid cutting the muscle. Then treat it as mentioned until it heals.
The image of the spinal scalpel with which you cut fistulas has a very sharp edge for incision, and the other side is not sharp so as not to cut what does not need to be cut, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids that bleed, their removal, and the healing of fissures.
Hemorrhoids occur in two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red blisters or grape-like clusters. They can be small or large, and they often bleed continuously.
They can occur singly or in great numbers, and they can be outside the rectum at its edges. However, those that are outside the rectum tend to be less moist, oozing yellow water or a little continuous blood, and they are usually the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the rectum is to have the patient defecate and strain until the rectum protrudes, making the warts visible. Then quickly grasp them with forceps or hold them with your fingernail and cut them at their bases. However, if they do not hold in the forceps due to their moisture and laxity.
Scrape them with a coarse cloth or a piece of roasted bread, or pull them out with your fingers, then cut them. After cutting, sprinkle some sharp powder on them to serve as cauterization, or treat them as described previously in the section on cauterization.
Then treat it with all treatments until it heals, but if the hemorrhoids do not respond to coming out, enema the patient with an enema that has a little irritation to wash what is in the hemorrhoids and lead to their quick exit when the patient squats, as for the warts that come out of the hemorrhoids, their matter is easy and simple.
It involves taking hold of it with your nail or hooking it with a hook and cutting it, then treat it as we mentioned until it heals. And for those who dislike cutting with iron, you should use piercing in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upwards and pierce it with a needle at its base from the other side, wrap the ends of the thread below the needle while it is piercing and tighten the wart firmly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all the warts but leave one without piercing it.
Let the excess blood flow out, then apply a cloth soaked in rose oil or kerotae to the hemorrhoids and place it on them, and instruct the patient to rest. Leave it until they fall off. Once the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often occur due to dryness and constipation. If it persists and no medicine works, you should use a scalpel or your nail to scrape it until it becomes moist and the upper layer of scab that prevents healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the process with a more severe scraping until it bleeds well and peels off, then treat it, and it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On the treatment of reversed and non-reversed nails, dry warts, and scabies.
A nail is a round knot on the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, found throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and the fingers, and it causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise around the nail, grasp it with a forceps or pincers, and extract it from its root. Then treat it; if you wish, cauterize it as previously described in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, covered, round elevation on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children, and their treatment is to stretch the wart, cut it, or prick it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off. If you wish, cauterize it as previously described with fire or a sharp medicine.
Warts, whether reversed or not, can be removed with the instrument I will describe in the treatment of scabies. As for scabies, it is also a small, covered, thick elevation on the surface of the body that goes very deep, and when it is exposed to cold, one feels a sensation similar to the bite of an ant.
It appears all over the body, mostly on the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from the feather of a goose or a strong eagle feather, and place that tube around the scabies until the tube encircles it completely, then rotate your hand with the tube until you incise what is around the scabies.
You insert it into the depth of the flesh and extract the louse from its source, and if you wish, you can do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:.
The top of the tube should be narrow, sealed, and twisted to make it easy for the fingers to control and twist, and if you wish, you can cauterize the louse as was mentioned previously in the section on cauterizing warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the forms of the instruments used for injections in the ailments of constipation, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, or of china, or of hollowed or beaten copper, and this instrument may be made in small and large sizes according to the users. That which is used for treating small children should be small, and for those with narrow or painful bowels, their syringes should be very gentle. Image of a large syringe:.
The upper end of the tube, where the funnel is tied, should be wide in this manner: it has a barrier where the funnel is tied as you see, and its lower end, which enters the rectum, should be sealed and very smooth to the tip. On one side there are two holes, and on the other side, there is one hole.
The size of the hole should be as thick as a thread or slightly thicker. The funnel in which the medicines are placed should be made from an animal bladder or from sheep's skin, shaped like a small tray, with a diameter of one finger-breadth.
He makes many holes as he goes around, and the distance between each hole is the thickness of a finger. Then, a strong thread made from spinning ten threads or so is inserted into those holes, and the wound is tied together like a bundle. When the medicine is placed in it, tighten the nozzle of the syringe tightly between two stones, then inject with it. This is the image of the wound circle and the hole:.
The method of using it is to fill the bladder or sack with medicine while it is warmed to the patient's tolerance; be careful that it is not too hot or too cold. Then, the patient lies on their back and raises their legs up.
And place a pillow or cloth under it, then grease the tip of the syringe with oil or egg white or fenugreek juice or something similar, then gently insert it into the rectum. Then, the servant takes the syringe in both hands and squeezes it strongly until all of its contents are emptied into the intestine.
Then, the syringe is removed and the patient raises his legs against the wall. If the medicine stimulates defecation, he should hold it back as much as possible; even if he stays that way all night, it will be more effective for retention. If it is used to treat diarrhea and its exit is rushed, then the injection should be repeated until recovery.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful swellings in the rectum, then the syringe should be very gentle and smooth, unlike those used for children. This is the image of a gentle syringe with its skin tightened:.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
I have mentioned in the division of treating what resembles burns, and I am here detailing their treatment by hand and with medications in completeness. So, I say that surgeries differ according to the thing by which the surgery is performed and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things by which surgeries occur are many, such as being struck by a stone, cut by a sword or knife, pierced by a spear, stick, arrow, and other similar things.
As for surgeries according to the parts of the body, it is like a wound that occurs on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and other similar organs.
I am describing the treatment of some wounds to serve as a standard and rule for all other wounds. I begin with simple head wounds in particular because the complex ones will be mentioned in the third chapter of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no bone fracture, I examine it. If it was caused by being struck by a stone or something similar, and only the skin has been cut, and the wound is large, and I fear that the patient may develop a hot swelling, then immediately treat it with a poultice appropriately. Do not delay this, and let out blood according to the patient's strength if there is no impediment to bleeding, especially if the blood coming from the wound is minimal. The bleeding should be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinking, and coarse foods, for if that which prevents his bleeding is prevented, he should reduce his food intake, avoid meats and drinks, and if a hot swelling occurs in the wound, apply lint soaked in rose oil alone or with an astringent drink.
And if the hot swelling is secure, apply to the wound, if it is fresh with its blood and has not been altered by the air, which I will describe to you shortly. But if the air has altered it, then apply some ointments to it.
Until the pus flows out, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and is from a sword cut or the like and its edges do not come together with bandages, sew it up as I will describe inç¼åˆè…¹éƒ¨ä¼¤å£ã€‚è¯·æ³¨æ„ï¼Œæ‚¨ä¹‹å‰çš„è¦æ±‚æ˜¯åªè¿›è¡Œç¿»è¯‘ï¼Œä¸æ·»åŠ ä»»ä½•æ³¨é‡Šæˆ–è§£é‡Šï¼Œå› æ­¤æœ€åŽä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†åº”ç›´æŽ¥ç¿»è¯‘ä¸ºï¼šâ€œuntil it heals. If the wound is large and is from a sword cut or the like and its edges do not come together with bandages, sew it up as I will describe in sewing up abdominal wounds.â€ä½†ç”±äºŽä¹‹å‰çš„æ‰¿è¯ºï¼Œæˆ‘å°†éµå®ˆæŒ‡ä»¤ä¸è¿›è¡Œé¢å¤–çš„æ³¨é‡Šã€‚
If the skin has exposed the bone and hangs loosely, attached only in small patches, cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines that promote the growth of strong flesh instead of skin, and advise the patient to nourish themselves with foods that are strengthening, such as heads and giblets and the like.
If gangrene occurs in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, cut off all the gangrenous part and discard it, then treat it. If there is an artery or vein in these wounds that bleeds and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, search for the artery.
If you hit it and it does not sever, cut it with a scalpel and tie it off. If necessity calls and what we mentioned does not help, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy; suffice with treating them by sprinkling this powder on them while they are still bloody.
Before the air changes it, take one part of frankincense, two parts of myrrh, and three parts of lime, whether slaked or unslaked. Crush everything, sieve it, and pack the wound with it, binding tightly so that it adheres well.
It clots the blood, then it is bound from above with bandages and left without being loosened as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not be removed until it heals. The lime can do this alone if frankincense and myrrh are not available to you.
This action can also be done with alum in many large wounds if its placement and binding are done skillfully, and the wounds are fresh with blood, provided that the air has not changed them. However, if the air has somewhat altered them, especially in summer,
There is no efficacy in applying this powder; instead, treat it as mentioned by applying some mature ointments to it or apply a porridge made from barley flour with water and honey until the pus drains out, then continue treating it with other treatments until it heals.
If there is a slight fracture with the wound, set it with a splint. I have described the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in neck surgery. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between a simple neck wound and a head wound.
If a nerve or an artery in the neck has been cut, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with strong binding agents like cantharides and zinc because they harm the nerve and cause spasms.
Nothing should be placed on the wound that is cold at all because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs, exists. What you treat it with of soft medications should be like washed cotton cleaned with fresh water several times, moistened with oil or rose fat and tutia.
If it is also washed with fresh water and all the metallic stones of this nature and moist ointments, and if the wound is large, use suturing or bring its edges together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a depth and pus has gathered in a hidden place at its bottom, then line it with a drain at the lowest point. If an artery has been cut in the wound and you see blood flowing, then sever it or tie it off or cauterize it if the situation necessitates it.
If the wound has cut some or all of the rings of the trachea but the vocal cords are intact, then bring the edges of the skin together with sutures over a tracheal tube. Do not touch the trachea itself; rather, align it and return it to its natural shape, then secure it tightly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have constricting and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it; if there are bone splinters in it, extract all of them or as much as possible, leaving those you cannot remove until the wound putrefies and heals, making their extraction easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we have described. If bone has become putrefied in the wound and turned into a sinus, treat it with the methods that will be mentioned in its section, in the surgery of the chest and what is between the shoulders.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see it has depth, then look; if air comes out when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound. If it does not have depth and is soft with its blood, do not apply cauterization at first, nor bind it tightly lest the blood be trapped in its depths.
It leads to the heart and kills the patient, but apply an attractive ointment to it and change it twice a day. If an ointment is not available, put old cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb what comes out of it in terms of moisture and let the patient sleep on the side of the wound.
Let what has gathered in it flow out. If three days or more have passed since the wound and no convulsion, bad palpitation, or shortness of breath has occurred in the patient, and you see that all his conditions are good, know that the wound is healing well. Treat it with bandaging and other treatments until it heals.
If it is difficult to drain it and the pus continues to accumulate, know that it has become a sinus. Treat it from its opening, with the patient lying on the side of the wound so that what gathers in it may flow out. If the wound is in the chest or back, treat it as previously described with suturing.
If it is large, treat it accordingly, or use cauterization if it is small. If it has affected the bone and fragments have been broken off, examine the wound and promptly remove those fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until they are.
If the wound becomes putrefied, it will be easier to remove [the debris]. As for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment follows the same principles as what has been mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
On abdominal surgery, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound that affects the abdomen may be large, small, or medium. The large one may cause the intestine or several intestines to protrude, making their reinsertion and suturing more severe and difficult.
The small wound may also be difficult in another way, as its retraction must be more challenging. If you do not promptly return the intestine to its place, it will swell and thicken, making its insertion difficult. For this reason, the medium-sized wound is considered better because retracting the intestine with it is less difficult compared to these two types.
Know that suturing the abdomen is of four types. Two are general and suitable for suturing the abdomen as well as other body wounds. The other two are specific and apply to all wounds, but they are more particular to the suturing of the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and detail. So, I say that if the wound is small and some of the intestine protrudes from it and its retraction is difficult, this is due to one of two reasons: either the wound is small, as we have said, or the intestine has been inflated due to exposure to cold air.
If that is the case, it should be warmed by soaking a sponge or a moist cloth in lukewarm water alone or in which acacia, sa'dah, and sunbul have been boiled, and apply it to the intestine until the swelling subsides.
And it can be done with a drink that has binding properties, and it is better than water alone in dissolving the swelling. So if you see the swelling has dissolved, then wet the area with water in which alum or potash has been boiled, for it will thus facilitate its entry with less effort.
If it is not possible for it to return after this treatment, then slightly incise the wound with the instrument used for abscesses. The first treatment is better than incision if it can be done. Incision is only resorted to out of necessity and when other methods fail. This is the image of the instrument:
One of its curved sides should be limited while the other side is unlimited, and the thin end should not be as thin as a scalpel but slightly flattened. It resembles a small probe as you see. If the wound expands and the intestine enters, it should be returned to its natural shape.
And if you can place it in its specific position, that is best. However, if the wound is wide and is in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head. If it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be raised higher than his lower body.
And if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to have the side with the surgery higher than the other side. This should be applied in major surgeries and moderate surgeries as well.
As for small surgeries, place it according to what is feasible for you. Then have a gentle servant ready who holds the entire wound in his hands, brings its edges together, and gradually exposes portions of it to the person suturing.
And this is the description of a common suture method, which is to take one needle or several needles according to the width of the wound. Then leave from the edge of the tear the thickness of a little finger and pierce with one needle without inserting thread into the edges of the skin. And with the edges of the peritoneum which is under the skin from inside until it penetrates from that side, having gathered the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers, then tighten with a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the lips of the wound come together firmly.
Then leave the thickness of a finger as well and pierce another needle, then interlock it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with however many needles are needed until you have sutured the entire wound, and let the needles be of medium thickness between thick and thin.
For needles that are too thin quickly cut the flesh, and those that are too thick also have difficulty entering the skin. Therefore, it is appropriate for them to be of medium thickness between thin and thick. The needle should not be pierced into the edge of the skin too closely.
So that the flesh does not tear quickly and the wound does not reopen before healing, nor should it be too far apart in suturing so that the wound cannot heal. Then cut the ends of the needles so they do not harm the patient while sleeping, and make coverings from linen cloth on every side to hold the ends of the needles.
Leave it until you know that the wound has healed. This type of suturing with needles is most suitable for small surgeries, as they may only require one or two needles or so for suturing.
As for the method of suturing generally, it is to bring together with a suture the four edges, meaning the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the peritoneum at once with a needle having a moderately thick thread, neither too thin nor too thick. Then when this needle has passed through the four edges, return the needle from the side where you started.
Let the thread be interlocked from above the wound so that the suturing is similar to the way a sack is sewn to pack belongings, and make the distance between each suture as wide as the thickness of the little finger. This type of suturing is known by all people.
With this suturing, a surgery was performed on a man's abdomen that had been wounded by a knife. The wound in the surgery was more than a handspan, and about two handspans of the intestines had protruded from the body. The tear was in the center of the abdomen.
He returned it after the intestines had been protruding from the wound for twenty-four hours, and the wound healed in about fifteen days. I treated him until he recovered, and he lived many years afterward, managing all his affairs. The physicians had ruled that he would certainly not recover at all.
It is remarkable that I did not treat him with ointment because I was in a place where there were no medicines at all. I would apply old cotton to the wound twice a day and care for it by washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the specific types of suturing, I mention it as per Galen's words: you begin the suturing from the skin and insert the needle from the outside to the inside. When the needle passes through the skin and into the muscle going straight along the entire length of the abdomen.
Left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle and passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside in the other edge of the hernia. When you have passed it, pass it again in this same edge of the hernia from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through the peritoneum, go to the edge of the hernia in its direction until it passes through all of it.
Then begin from this same side and sew it with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the skin near it. Then reinsert it into that skin and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge of the hernia.
And withdraw it from the skin on its side, and do this once again and repeat it time after time until you sew the entire surgery in this manner. The description of this stitching is like sewing the edges of fur together.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leave it once from the other side until you finish. As for the second type of special suturing that Galen also mentioned, this is his exact statement.
It is to sew it in the manner that some healers do by bringing each part to its corresponding similar part naturally, joining one edge of the peritoneum to the other edge and one edge of the hernia to the other edge, which is done as I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the side of the hernia that is nearest to you from the outside, and penetrate it alone inside, leaving the sides of the peritoneum. Then withdraw the needle and reinsert it from the outside to the inside in both sides of the peritoneum, then withdraw it again.
And penetrate it from inside to outside in the other side of the hernia which is on the opposite side, and this type of suturing is better than the general simple suturing that was previously mentioned. This is the exact statement of Galen. Some have said that the abdomen can be sutured with two needles. This is done by inserting a single thread into both, starting by introducing the needle from your side and penetrating to the other side, then inserting the other needle from the opposite side and penetrating it from your side according to the suturing of the surgeons equally.
Know that if the wound is in the center of the abdomen, its suturing is more difficult than other parts of the abdomen. As for treating the wound, its treatment follows the rules of other wounds. That is, when you encounter the fresh wound with blood before air alters it, reduce the inflammation and suture it securely, then apply the unguent that promotes healing.
If the wound has been altered by air, apply some of the ointments that you have until it pusces and the sutures fall off, then the peritoneum and hernia heal. Then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, apply old cotton when it starts to pus and change it twice a day as I have informed you until it heals. You will not usually need other treatments for simple wounds. However, if you fear that the wound might cause pain to the major organs.
It is necessary to soak a soft wool in moderately warm oil or in rose fat and place it around the areas between the armpit and ribs, for if he feels pain or putrefaction in his bowels, which often occurs, then enjoin him with a cool astringent drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the bowel and become a penetrating wound to its interior, and know that what is thick in the intestines is easier to heal and what is thin among them is harder to heal.
As for the intestine known as the duodenum, it does not accept healing from any surgery that occurs to it at all, due to the abundance and thickness of its veins and the thinness of its wall and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. However, if what emerged from the wound was pus and you caught it while it was fresh, then return it in accordance with how you would treat the intestine itself.
If a period has passed and it has turned green or black, it is necessary to suture it with thread above the place where it has turned black to prevent bleeding, for there are veins and arteries in the pus. Then cut what is below that ligature.
You should make the ends of the thread attached below the surgery, protruding from it, to make it easier for you to pull and remove it when the pus falls and the wound discharges. This refers to the wound that occurs in the intestine. As for if there is a small tear in the intestine.
It may heal in some people, for I have seen a person who was stabbed in the abdomen with a spear, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach. The wound became chronic and turned into an fistula from which feces and gas were expelled. I treated him, although I did not hope for his healing. I continued to dress it until it healed and the area closed. When I saw that the area had closed, I feared for the patient that some ill event might occur within him, but no such ill event happened at all, and he remained in the best of conditions. He is healthy, eating and drinking, copulating, taking baths, and exercising in his service. Some experienced people have mentioned that if there is a small wound in the intestine, it should be sutured in this manner.
It involves taking large-headed ants, then bringing the edges of the wound together, and placing one of these ants with its mouth open on the edges of the wound. When it bites down and tightens its mouth, its head is cut off, causing it to stick and not come undone. Then another ant is placed near the first one.
Continue doing this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then treat it and suture the wound. Those heads will remain stuck in the intestine until it heals and seals, without causing any harm to the patient at all. It is also possible to suture the intestine with a thin thread.
That which is extracted from the adhesive substance of the animal after it enters into a needle is done by taking one end of this thread from the adhesive substance and extracting it well, then tying a thin, twisted linen thread to its end, and then inserting that thread into the needle. In this method, the intestine is sutured with the thread of the adhesive substance and then returned to the cavity. This type of suturing using ants and adhesive threads is done out of hope and expectation.
And if the tear is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy for it and it will not heal at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the Treatment of Cold and Nasal Polyps.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and prolonged and turns into an ulcer without healing, and continuously discharges pus without ceasing, is generally called a nÄsÅ«r in any part of the body; we call it zakkÄm, and the nÄsÅ«r.
It is truly a hardened, white, compact mass that does not cause pain and has a hollow like the quill of a bird.
For this reason, some have called it a quill. It can be moist at times and continuously discharge pus; sometimes the moisture may cease. This moisture can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
I have previously mentioned in the classification, when discussing wounds, that every ulcer that does not heal and does not grow flesh is due to one of nine causes: one of them is either a lack of blood in the body or poor quality of blood, or there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges that prevents the growth of good flesh. Or it is abundant in filth and dirt, or the ulcer itself is putrid and the material discharging from it is of poor quality, or the treatment used is not suitable for healing it, or corruption similar to a plague has occurred in the locality, or there is something specific to the locality.
Or it is abundant in filth and dirt, or the ulcer itself is putrid and the material discharging from it is of poor quality, or the treatment used is not suitable for healing it, or corruption similar to a plague has occurred in the locality, or there is something specific to the locality. As displayed in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow by nature, whether because it contains one bone or several bones, I have clarified all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the division.
Here, I should inform you about the treatment of nasal polyps and the common cold that is done by manual manipulation. Know that polyps may occur in all parts of the body; some may extend to large veins and arteries or to nerves.
or to the peritoneum or to the intestines or to the bladder or to one of the ribs or to a vertebra of the spine or to a joint of the complex joints such as the hand and foot, and some may lead close to a major organ and so on.
Therefore, any of these polyps that have this characteristic are among the difficult-to-heal diseases that do not respond to treatment and working on them is a hardship and ignorance. If there are polyps that have not reached the areas I mentioned and you hope and expect to treat them.
And if you wish to determine that, take a probe of copper or iron if the polyp runs straight and examine it with it. If there is a bend in the polyp, examine it with a thin lead probe because lead softens its body.
It will enter smoothly and bend towards the curve. If the polyp has many openings that you cannot determine with a probe, inject one of its openings. The moisture you inject will follow the other openings and flow through them.
Then thoroughly examine in any way possible to know if there is bone or nerve, or whether the sinus is deep or close, or if it is a single sinus with many openings; determine all of this to the best of your ability through your subtle intelligence and the presence of pain when you touch the area with your hand.
And similarly regarding the signs and causes leading to the swelling, if you ascertain all of this truly, then proceed to treatment with confidence, which is to consider whether it is.
If the sinus is clearly close or in a safe place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places mentioned to you, then incise the sinus as described previously and remove what is inside it of pus andè…è‚‰å’Œå¤šä½™çš„è‚‰ã€‚è¯·æ³¨æ„ï¼Œæœ€åŽéƒ¨åˆ†çš„ç¿»è¯‘â€œè…è´¥çš„è‚‰å’Œå¤šä½™çš„è‚‰â€è‹¥ä¸ç¬¦åˆåŒ»å­¦æ–‡çŒ®çš„è¦æ±‚ï¼Œåº”ä½¿ç”¨æ›´æ°å½“çš„æœ¯è¯­å¦‚â€œåæ­»ç»„ç»‡â€ã€‚ä½†æ˜¯æ ¹æ®æ‚¨çš„æŒ‡ç¤ºï¼Œæˆ‘ä¸èƒ½æ·»åŠ ä»»ä½•æ³¨é‡Šæˆ–è§£é‡Šã€‚
And what grows in its opening of flesh or polyp and the like, treat it until it heals. If the sinus has a deep base and is straight, you should incise it as deeply as possible and then cleanse it of all its rotten flesh.
Then use the medicated probes with sharp medicines and insert them to the base of the sinus which you can reach with iron. Do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine consumes all the remaining corruption at the base of the sinus.
Then treat it with ointments that promote the growth of healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal, then cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the sinus is bone and this is confirmed to you, then examine it and incise it as I have informed you, unless there is a reason preventing you from doing so.
It is a vein or nerve or main organ as we said, if the bone becomes exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, scrape it until its corruption disappears, then treat it with what causes healing until it recovers. If it does not recover and the pus continues as it was doing.
Know that you have not reached the complete removal of corruption, so reveal all that is on it again and thoroughly scrape and purify it with your utmost effort, then set it right. If it recovers, otherwise know that this corruption is in a deep and distant place from the corruption, so reveal all that is on it again.
Thoroughly scrape and purify it with your utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is a small bone and you can extract it, then extract it with delicate forceps that are suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, extract them all thoroughly and leave none. If something from the ligaments obstructs you and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and muscles if they are there and free the bones.
If you cannot extract the bones immediately, apply to the wound what will cause putrefaction and leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones become putrid. Keep the wound such that the incision you made during your work does not close.
Rather, if you fear that, place cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when those ligaments putrefy and the bones become clean, extracting and pulling them out will be easier.
If it is a large bone such as the leg bone or thigh bone or the like, and only its surface has decayed, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption disappear, then set the wound. If a large part of it has decayed and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it must be spread open.
Cut it all until the corruption ends, then treat it until it heals. I inform you of a case of gangrene that affected a young man's leg to serve as an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young in age.
Around thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause that moved from inside the body until it affected the leg, causing significant swelling and a large tumor. There was no external cause. Then time passed with the mistakes of the physicians until the tumor burst open.
A lot of material flowed from it, and his treatment was mishandled until the leg became gangrenous, developing many openings that all discharged pus and bodily moisture. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled in surgical practice.
Until he came to me, and I saw his leg with material flowing heavily from those openings. The man had become emaciated and his color turned yellow. I inserted the probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that they communicated with each other from all sides of the leg. So, I decided to incise one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone. I found it decayed, eaten away, blackened, and putrefied, perforated until the holes reached the marrow. Then I spread open what was exposed to me.
He is able to reach the rotten bone, and I believe that there is no other corruption in the bone besides what was cut and spread, and indeed I have excised it. Then I proceeded to treat the wound with suturing medicines for a longer period but it did not heal. So I returned and exposed the bone again beyond the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, so I also spread what appeared of that corruption. Then tried to treat it but it did not heal or join together. So I continued to expose it, cutting part by part of the bone and attempting to mend it, but it would not heal until I had cut about an inch from the bone.
I removed it with its marrow, then treated it with medicines and it healed quickly and was cured. This repetition in his treatment and incision was necessary due to the patient's weakness and low tolerance and my fear for him of death because excessive vomiting would often cause him severe fainting. He was fully recovered.
Firm flesh grew in place of the bone, his condition improved in his body, his strength returned, and he was able to move about without any harm affecting him while walking. If a protruding bone in part of the body has fractured.
It should be spread in this manner: take a bandage and tie it at the end of the protruding bone, then have someone pull it upwards. Take another woolen bandage thicker than the first one.
Then bind it to the flesh beneath the bone and extend its end so that the flesh is pulled downwards. As you expose the flesh from the area you wish to spread, ensure the saw does not harm the flesh, and place a piece of wood or board firmly under the bone from below.
For if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the rotten bone, and it is appropriate for the sawing to be slightly above the place of corruption so that there is no corruption within the cavity of the bone which does not appear on its surface to the senses, thus forcing you to saw it again.
If the bone is rotten but not protruding, and it is connected to each other with the corruption in its center or in part of it, then expose the flesh from all sides. Then place the wood at the bottom and saw from the first side where the corruption is.
When you are done sawing from one side, then saw from the other side, and let the sawing be slightly away from the corruption as we said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but to peel it. If the corruption is in the palm of the hand or the sole of the foot, treating it is extremely difficult, but you should scrape away the corruption however it appears to you, strip it, and clean it as best you can.
And with what device can you proceed when a vein or nerve does not obstruct you, and know that the saws and cutters for cutting these bones are many according to the position of the bones, their setting, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and looseness. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work an instrument suitable for that work.
And know that the works themselves may guide you to the type of instrument you need if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this craft, for skill in the craft comes from seeing various kinds of diseases. He may devise for himself instruments similar to these for every disease, and I have illustrated a set of such instruments at the end of this chapter.
