Chapter Eighty-Seven.
On Amputating Limbs and Scraping Bones.
Limb parts may become gangrenous either due to an external cause or an internal cause. If you treat this corruption with medicinal treatments and the treatment is ineffective, and you see that the corruption spreads throughout the organ without anything stopping it.
It is necessary to amputate that organ where the corruption has reached so that the patient may be saved from death or a greater calamity which is worse than the loss of the limb. A sign of this condition is that the limb turns black until it seems as if fire has burned it.
Or it may become gangrenous after turning black until that gangrene spreads to the adjacent parts and affects the entire body; therefore, you should promptly amputate it. Similarly, if the cause of corruption is due to the bite of some creatures such as the sea scorpion, snake, or lizard, or the like.
If the corruption or bite is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger and do not delay lest the corruption spreads to the hand. Similarly, if it has affected the hand, amputate it from the base of the wrist and do not delay lest the corruption spreads until it affects the arm.
And if it is taken in the arm, cut the arm at the elbow joint itself. If corruption continues and you see that it extends towards the shoulder, do not cut the shoulder at all for there is death to the patient in that, and use other treatments as much as possible.
And do the same with the leg if corruption takes hold in the toe, cut the toe at one of the joints. And if it affects the foot, then amputate the entire leg. If it ascends to the knee, cut the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has spread above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it and submit the patient to death. The method of amputating or opening up a limb is to tie a bandage below the place you wish to cut and another bandage above the place.
One servant extends the lower bandage downward, and another servant extends the upper bandage upward. You should strip the flesh between the two bandages with a wide knife until all the flesh is exposed, then proceed to cut or open up. It is advisable to place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the healthy area, causing the patient additional pain and a hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the area quickly or apply some hemostatic agents, then resume your treatment until you finish.
Then bandage the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I inform you of an example that occurred to a man in his leg, this exact condition I am describing to you, which is that there was blackening with a burning sensation similar to fire in his leg.
And that corruption first occurred in his finger until it took the entire leg. The leg came off by itself when he saw the corruption spreading through the limb along with the severe pain and burning he was feeling, so he amputated it at the joint and was healed.
And after a long time, that same corruption appeared in his index finger. He came to me, so I treated that excess with the medicines I applied to his hand after cleaning his body, but the excess did not stop and began to spread into the other fingers.
Until the corruption spread to his hand, so he asked me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to stop that excess and also fearing for him that he might die if his hand were cut off, as the man's strength was failing. When he despaired of me, he returned to his country.
I was informed that he amputated his entire hand, and he was healed. I relate this story to be of help in cases of this kind of disease and to provide a guideline for dealing with it.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject them with medicines.
If a swelling occurs in some of the fleshy organs and the duration of the swelling extends until it collects pus, then it bursts or breaks open and all the pus inside comes out, leaving the area empty like a vessel.
And the skin on it is like a cloth that has become thin, and the corruption was not severe enough to affect the bone, nerve, or ligament. This is why it deserves to be called an abscess and not a sinus, unless its duration extends so that the corruption affects any of these organs.
Then it is called a naá¹£Å«r or zikÄma, and its treatment is to cut all of that skin around entirely, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the area due to its corruption.
If you hope that the skin will adhere and it has not reached such corruption, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, treat it with injection. That is to say, look at the abscess; if it is large and the pus coming out of it has a foul smell, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
It is a mixture of vinegar, oil, honey, and zincugar in equal parts, collected in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine curdles and becomes as thick as honey. Then take what you need from it, dilute it with water and honey, inject it into the abscess, seal its mouth, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then extract it by squeezing. Do this for days until the abscess is clean and the foul smell disappears. If this medicine is not available, it can be injected with lye water, which is made by putting the ashes of vine wood or oak wood in water.
Then filter it and inject until you are certain that the abscess is cleansed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or lye water, inject with a mixture of water and honey in which some powdered zincugar has been mixed.
Or inject it with a mixture of honey and decoction, for honey has the property of cleansing and washing, while the decoction adheres to the abscess, especially if the decoction has astringent and drying qualities. If you do this several times and are certain that the corruption in the abscess is gone, then inject it with something that promotes flesh growth.
Take some of the dough ointment, dissolve it with rose oil and astringent drink or inject it with other drugs and ointments whose properties we have described in the article on ointments. If the mouth of the wound is narrow and does not allow the insertion of the syringe.
Widen it slightly with iron or place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or sericum ointment until it widens. Similarly, if the mouth is also wide, bring its edges together with sutures and leave enough space for the syringe to enter without excess.
And if the mouth from which the pus flows is raised upwards, cut it at the lowest point so that the pus flows downwards, because if the pus congests in the depth of the wound, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the wound downward as desired, then position the limb in a way that allows the pus to drain easily according to what is suitable for you, ensuring that no pus is retained within it at all.
As for the healing ointments that are applied to the wound from the outside and how to apply them, take one of the suitable healing ointments you desire and spread it on a piece of linen cloth. The cloth should be large enough to cover the entire wound. Then, cut with scissors a hole opposite the mouth of the wound slightly wider than the mouth of the wound. Then, spread some soft ointment on another piece of cloth according to the size of the hole and place it over it, keeping it apart from the mouth of the wound to facilitate the discharge of pus. Do not remove the large cloth until afterwards.
Several days, and as for the one at the mouth of the wound, it should be removed at all times so that what has collected in it of pus may come out, and to know what flows from it of sanies whether it is much or little or ripe or unripe.
And also, examine the site of the wound itself whether its owner feels pain there or not, and if there is swelling or no swelling. If your treatment proceeds this way and you see that the material which comes out is slight at the mouth of the wound.
Know that the wound has adhered or is close to adhering. In this case, remove it tomorrow or on the third day. Whenever you remove the dressing, replace the bandage with a new one and renew the ointment. If thin sanies comes out of the wound after a long period, do not despair of its adhesion.
His healing is near, for among people, the growth of flesh in their wounds varies; some take longer while others do not. If after many days pus that is not ripe comes out of the wound, know that the wound has not adhered. If the adhesion of the wound is delayed.
And if the matter persists, increase in drying the medications. Let the medications be such that they are moist in their consistency but dry in their strength, like palm-date ointment if more colocynth has been added to it. Among the most effective treatments is to use the palm-date ointment.
It is dissolved with rose oil, and sprinkle on it a moderate decoction in its consistency, then knead it with it and use it. This can also be done if you do not have palm-date ointment: take honey, boil it until it becomes irritating, and use it. Or take myrrh, aloes, and cubebs, crush them all, and sprinkle the powder on the honey while it is over fire. Then apply it to a cloth and bind it over the wound, or you may coat it with honey that has been cooked until thickened, sprinkle the medicines over it, place the cloths on top, and bind them.
And for such cases, you may use long storax, the root of the asphodel, coriander flour, and hellebore. Use them individually or in combination; crush them, sieve them, and sprinkle them on the honey that was described, then apply it. If the wound persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption deep within it.
And if it affects the bones or any of the solid nerves or ligaments, treat it as described for the treatment of the sinus as previously mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
On the treatment of corns and ingrown nails, and the amputation of excess fingers and the splitting of fused fingers.
A corn is an excess of flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb or toe, and it may also grow on other fingers. If it persists and its treatment is neglected, it swells and becomes a hot sore.
Or it may become infected until it eats away the base of the nail, and may even corrupt it entirely. The corruption might reach the bone, causing a foul smell, with the tip of the finger becoming swollen and its color turning dark. If treating it with the methods mentioned in the division does not succeed.
You should cut off all the excess that grows from the nail with iron, then cauterize the wound. Cauterization is very beneficial in this case. If the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy.
And the angle of the nail that has pressed into the flesh and caused it pain and harm should have a thin piece of leather placed under the nail's corner which is piercing the flesh, then lift it up and cut off that flesh gently.
And apply to what remains of the flesh medicines that are burning and consuming until it all disappears, then treat it with ointments until it heals. And if the corruption has affected the bone, you should cut out that bone and remove it, for it will not heal as long as there is rotten bone present at all.
If you see erosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it at one of the joints as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or crush and severe pain occurs, you should first splint the injured area.
Then, cut the nail with a sharp instrument diagonally from top to bottom, being careful not to reach the flesh beneath the nail, for doing so would cause severe pain to the patient and lead to excessive flesh growth in that area. Then treat the area with what soothes the pains.
As for the extra finger that sometimes grows on people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bones in part of it, or it may even have a nail. Sometimes its growth originates at the base of a joint of one of the fingers.
Or its growth may occur at some joints of the finger. Those that grow at the joints of the finger do not move, while those that grow near the finger joint might move. What is purely flesh can be easily cut off; this is done by cutting it at its base with a broad instrument.
And as for those that grow at the base of the joint, their treatment is difficult so avoid cutting them. As for those that grow in the finger near one of the salamiyat, it is necessary to first cut its flesh in a circular manner to the bone, then spread the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion that occurs between the fingers, it often happens and can be something with which a person is born or can occur when a wound or burn from fire, etc., heals; therefore, it is necessary to split this adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural form, then place between them splints or patches soaked in rose oil so that it does not adhere quickly, keep them apart or place a thin sheet of lead between them until the area heals properly.
And if adhesion occurs between some fingers and the palm, split that adhesion according to what seems appropriate and what restores the form of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On the cutting of warts.
Varicose veins are twisted, thick vessels filled with superfluous black humors that occur in most parts of the body, but they occur most frequently in the legs, especially in the thighs and calves of those who carry heavy loads. It is necessary first to use strong friction on the body with black salt several times.
Then let the patient abstain from sexual intercourse. As for treating it with iron, it is of two types: one is to cut and remove the black blood, and the other is to cauterize the vein and completely remove it. For cutting it, wash the leg first with hot water thoroughly.
Until the thick, turbid blood dissolves. Then bind the patient's leg from above the thigh to below the knee with a turban. Then make one incision or two or three wide incisions in the area.
Then you squeeze the black blood with your hand from below the leg upwards and from above downwards until the amount of blood that you deem sufficient or what the patient's strength can endure is released. Then bind it and instruct him to avoid foods that generate black bile, and repeat vomiting and bloodletting.
When the veins are full and that harms the patient, then this method should be used: if there is a lot of hair on the patient's leg, shave it. Then immerse him in a bath or wash his leg with hot water until it turns red and sweat comes out, or make him exercise vigorously.
If a bath is not available, warm the limb. Then make an incision along the skin opposite the vein, either at its end near the knee or at its lower part near the heel. Then open the skin with probes and peel the vein from all sides until it becomes apparent to touch.
When it appears, you will see it as bright red. Once it is freed from the skin, it will appear like a tendon. Then insert a thread under it until it lifts and comes out of the skin, then secure it with a smooth blind hook. Then make another incision near that incision, about three fingers apart.
Then peel the skin off the vein until it appears, then lift it with thread as you did before and secure it with another hook as you did first. Then make another incision or several incisions if needed.
Then polish it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it out and polish it until it comes out of the second incision, then pull it to the incision above it and do this until you pull it out from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, so that when it is completely out, cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and polishing, then insert a needle with a strong doubled thread and tie it, pull it, and insert the spatula under it and twist it with your hand in all directions until it comes out, be careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to polish it.
And if it causes harm to the patient, when you have polished it all, put wool soaked in a mixture of wine and rose oil or olive oil on the site of the incisions and treat it until it heals. If the tendon is contracted and has twists in various directions.
And if it is not straight as we said, it should be incised at every direction of its twists and places of curling, then hang it with hooks until you polish all of it. Be careful when incising it that you do not cut the vein or injure it, for it will be difficult for you to polish it, so be very cautious.
Image of the instrument with which you incise the tendon:.
Image of the blind hook:.
It should not have a bend like other hooks and its tip should not be sharp so as not to injure the vein; it should be thick in its curve and smooth because if it were thin, it would cut the vein with its thinness; rather, it should have the advantage of thickness as we said.
Chapter Ninety-One.
On the Treatment of Civil Sweat.
This sweat develops in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and the lands of Arabs, and in hot, dry, less fertile countries. It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to corruption that occurs under the skin.
Worms and other parasites can occur inside the bodies, as well as the formation of abscesses and worms that develop between the skin and the flesh. A sign of the beginning of this sweat is a severe inflammation in the leg, then the area discharges fluid, and then the sweat starts to come out from where the discharge occurred.
It is as if it were the root of a plant or an animal, and when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing between one dirham and two dirhams, tied up, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
So, every time something comes out from it externally, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it extends a lot, cut off some of it and wrap the remainder. Do not cut it off at its root before all of it has come out because if you cut it, it will retract into the flesh and cause a tumor, corruption in the area, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be managed and drawn out little by little until it comes out entirely, leaving nothing in the body. Sometimes from this sweat, there may come out something that is five or ten cubits long in some people.
I have been informed that a man had one extending up to twenty cubits. If it should break during your treatment of him, insert a probe into its opening and pack it with a long dressing connected to the body until all its contents are evacuated, and attempt to cleanse the area with medicines for several days.
Then treat it as you would tumors, for this gland may have many branches, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, causing multiple openings to form and each mouth to emit a branch. Treat it as we mentioned in the division and with what has been previously stated.
The Ninety-Second Chapter.
On incising the worm that develops under the skin, which is called the cattle disease.
This disease is called cattle disease in some regions because it often affects cattle. It is a small worm that develops between the skin and the flesh and moves throughout the body, moving up and down.
It can be perceived by touch as it moves from one part to another until it breaks through the skin where it emerges, forming due to the putrefaction of some mixtures, similar to how worms and snakes and melon seeds form in the stomach.
The danger lies in the fact that if it moves through the body and rises to the head, reaching the eye, it may open up within it and emerge, thus destroying the eye, which often happens. If you wish to treat it and remove it, this can only be done when it is moving and can be perceived by touch.
It should be that you tightly bind what is above and below it with a bandage, then cut on it and extract it. If it sinks into the flesh and you do not find it, apply fire cauterization to the area until you burn it off. The greatest fear is its potential to harm the eye as we said.
If you see that it has reached the head near the eye, then tightly bind below it on the forehead and cut on it to extract it. It should be that the patient is cared for by purifying his body with laxative medicines that cleanse the foul mixtures and avoiding foods that generate putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On incising the disease known as nafir.
This disease which is called nafir in our country is a pain that occurs in some organs and then moves from one organ to another. I have seen it as I describe it to you.
I was summoned to a sick woman in one of the villages. She bared her arm and showed me a slight swelling in the vein of the arm. After staying for an hour, I saw that swelling moving along the bone like a worm crawling upwards towards her shoulder as quickly as possible, resembling mercury.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain leaves that spot and settles in the shoulder. After sitting for an hour, it traveled throughout the body until it reached the other arm. She then told me that it moves through her entire body as I witnessed.
I was amazed by its speed in moving from one organ to another. I had not seen this disease with my own eyes in such a manner before, except that I have heard of people who experience pain moving from one organ to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
And I do not estimate that except for the reason that the woman was from the desert people, with a dry body and exposed veins; thus, this wind's transition became apparent to the senses. Hence, it should not appear in this manner among the affluent and those with moist, hidden veins.
If you want to treat it and its owner feels pain, then if it appears before your eyes as mentioned, bind above and below it quickly, incise over it until that congested wind comes out, and clean the area.
If you do not see it with your eye, then treat it with body fumigation and what disperses winds and expels them, such as the herb of the foul smell and the seeds of scamony and similar medicines.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places where they strike in the body. As for their difference according to their types, some are large and some are small, and some have hollow glass tips while others have solid glass tips, and there are those that have three angles and four angles.
Some have barbs, and some have splinters. As for those that differ according to the organs they strike, there are two types: either the organs are major hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. Whenever an arrow strikes one of these organs and signs of death, which I will describe to you later, become apparent, you should avoid extracting that arrow, for death often follows the owner of the wound. And if these poor signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not sunk deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. Among the signs of the brain is if an arrow strikes it, penetrates the bone, and wounds the membrane covering the brain, then severe headache, lethargy, and dizziness will result from that.
And if these poor signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not sunk deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. Among the signs of the brain is if an arrow strikes it, penetrates the bone, and wounds the membrane covering the brain, then severe headache, lethargy, and dizziness will result from that. Redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting once or repeatedly, and sometimes blood comes out from the nostrils or ears, speech may be interrupted, voice lost, and white moisture like porridge flows from the wound site, similar to meat juice.
If these signs appear to you, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow if it has not already come out. As for the signs of the arrow when it strikes the heart and is close to the left breast, one feels as though it has embedded itself in something solid, not in an empty space.
And sometimes the arrow may move in a way that resembles the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness in the extremities, cold sweat, and loss of consciousness; know then that death is inevitable. The sign of the arrow when it wounds the lung is theæµå‡ºçš„è¡€æ¶²å‘ˆæ³¡æ²«çŠ¶ã€‚
And the vessels near the neck swell, the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily, seeking to inhale cool air. If the arrow strikes the membrane in the chest, it is close to the lower ribs, and breathing becomes labored with severe pain.
He sighs, and all the parts of the shoulders move. If the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain follows, and blood similar to the color of the liver comes out of the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may come out of the wound.
And its matter is evident, and if the arrow penetrates the abdomen and becomes embedded in it, and some feces come out of the wound or the ulcer or a part that has been perforated, there is no hope for his treatment nor in extracting the arrow. If the arrow has entered the bladder and urine comes out.
And if something protrudes outward and the pain intensifies for the patient, then know that he is dying. As for other organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are often spared.
When the arrow does not hit an artery or a nerve and is not poisoned, I inform you of some of what I have witnessed regarding these arrows to guide you in treatment. This concerns an arrow that had struck a man at the root of his nose, near his eye socket.
I extracted it from the other side beneath the earlobe, and he recovered without any harm to his eye. I also extracted another arrow for a Jew that had struck him in the eyelid below the lower lash line. The arrow had hidden itself, and I only reached its small end that attaches to the wooden shaft.
It was a large arrow from the composite arrows of the qasi, with a smooth square iron head that had no barbs. The Jew recovered without any adverse incident to his eye. I also extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and this arrow was Arabian.
It had barbs, so I made an incision between the jaws. It had sunk into his throat, so I gently manipulated it until I extracted it. The Christian was saved and recovered. I also extracted another arrow for a man who had been struck in the abdomen, and we feared he would die from it.
And when thirty days had passed or so, and there was no change in his condition, I cut along the arrow and maneuvered around it and extracted it, and he recovered without any mishap befalling him. I saw a man who had an arrow lodged in his back, and the wound had healed around it.
And after seven years, the arrow came out at the base of his thigh. I saw a woman who had an arrow embedded in her abdomen; the wound had healed around it, and the arrow remained without any change in her condition, and she did not feel any harm in any of her natural actions.
And I saw another man who had an arrow in his face, and the wound healed around it, and he did not suffer much pain. There are many similar cases. I extracted an arrow from a commander of the sultan that had embedded in the middle of his nose, slightly leaning to the right side.
And the arrow was completely embedded. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I probed it with a thin probe but could not feel the arrow. He felt stabbing pain and soreness under his ear on the right side.
I suspected that the stabbing sensation might be from the tip of the arrow, so I dressed the area with a bandage containing a drawing agent and awaited suppuration, hoping that the area would swell and reveal the location of the arrow. But when I incised it, nothing happened to indicate that the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued to dress the wound for many days, but no change occurred. The wound eventually sealed over, and the patient despaired of its extraction for a long time until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me. I then applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it, feeling the thin tip of the arrow that adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow became visible to us, and I spent about four months with it.
When the wound widened and I was able to insert the forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to being removed. I continued to treat it and use various tools until one day I grasped it firmly with some forceps, as will be shown in the figure at the end of the chapter.
The physicians had ruled that his nasal cartilage would not heal, but I set it and the wound healed, and the patient fully recovered without any further distress. I inform you of the method of removing some arrows so that you may use this as a standard and guide for what I have not mentioned.
For the parts of this craft and its details cannot be fully grasped by description nor encompassed in a book. Rather, the skilled craftsman measures the little with the much and extrapolates from what is present to what is absent, deriving new work and new tools for unusual occurrences.
If I speak of this craft, I say that arrows are removed from the parts they have penetrated in two ways: either by pulling them out from the place they entered or from the opposite side, and those that are removed are taken out from where they entered.
Either the arrow protrudes in a fleshy area and can be pulled out, or if it does not come out when you attempt to remove it, you should leave it for days until the flesh around it rots, making it easier to pull out. The same applies if it has penetrated bone.
And if it does not come out, leave it for several days and repeat pulling and moving it every day, for it will come out. And if it still does not come out after days, you should pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from all sides with a fine drill until you widen for the arrow, then pull it and take it out.
And if the arrow piercing in the skull has penetrated one of the ventricles of the brain and some of those symptoms that were mentioned to you have appeared in the patient, then refrain from pulling out the arrow and leave it until his condition improves after several days.
Proceed with pulling out the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and does not respond to pulling, use drills around the arrow as described to you, then treat the area until it heals. As for if the arrow has become concealed in a part of the body. And if it has become imperceptible, examine it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with some appropriate tools for pulling. If you cannot do so due to the narrowness of the wound or the depth of the arrow, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in that place.
Make an incision to widen the wound and gain better access to the arrow until you can extract it. If it has two barbs, grasp them and free the flesh pierced by them from all sides with every means at your disposal. Proceed, and if you cannot free the flesh, break the barbs and twist them until you can remove them.
And if you attempt to remove the arrow from any location, use your hands with forceps in all directions until you free it, and be as gentle as possible so that the arrow does not break, making it difficult for you to pull it out. If you cannot manage it at the time.
Leave it for days until the flesh around it putrefies, then return to it as it will be easier at that time. If bleeding obstructs you, use what we mentioned of treatment in its section, and be careful not to cut an artery, nerve, or tendon, and use ingenuity.
In whatever way you can free the arrow, do so gently, patiently, and steadily as described to you. It is best for you when pulling out the arrow to have the patient positioned in the same manner as they were in when the arrow struck, as this is most suitable.
If that is not possible for you, use whatever positions are feasible. As for the arrow emerging from the opposite side, either some part of it will protrude outward or you will find the tip of the arrow by touch above the skin nearby and see it protruding.
Make an incision over it, and let the incision be large enough to accommodate the forceps, then pull it out as it will come out easily. If it gets stuck in the bone, use your hands to twist around until the arrow affects the bone and widens its path, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for days and then return to it until it comes out. If the arrowshaft is still in place, push it out with it. If the shaft has fallen off and you want to use pushing, insert a hollow instrument so that its hollow part enters the tail of the arrow, then push it out with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that can enter that hollow space, thus making the extraction of the arrow easier. If the arrow is poisoned, you should excise all the flesh into which the poison has penetrated if possible, then treat it with what is appropriate for that condition.
If the arrow is located in the chest, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the side, and it is close enough to be probed with a probe, and you can incise around it, then incise, being careful not to cut any vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it requires stitching, then treat it until it heals. Image of the forceps used to extract arrows:.
Its ends are like a bird's beak, designed to grasp the arrow or any other object firmly without releasing it. They can be made in various sizesâ€”large, small, and mediumâ€”depending on the size of the arrow and the width or narrowness of the wound.

This is blunt-ended like a probe to easily enter the hollow arrow and push it out.
Chapter Fifty-Nine.
On Bloodletting.
The veins that are commonly bled in the body are thirty veins, including sixteen veins in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the hesasin, and the two prominent temporal arteries. There are also the two veins at the inner corners of the eyes, known as the nazeerain.
The vein standing in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, the veins on both sides of the neck, the two veins on the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins on the lower lip, and these veins are known as the jugulars, and the two veins under the tongue.
And as for the veins that diverge in the arm and hand, they are five veins. One of them is the cephalic, which is on the ulnar side, and the common people call it the head vein. The basilic is the middle vein, composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the body vein.
And the basilic, which is located on the inner side and is also called the armpit vein, and the common people call it the abdomen vein. The brachial tendon is located above the wrist, to which it adheres, and it clearly appears above the thumb. And the median vein.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, which has two branches. In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one is below the knee bend on the ulnar side, the second is the saphenous, located at the heel on the inner side, and the sciatic vein, located at the ankle on the ulnar side.
And in the other leg, there are three similar veins. As for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit lies in splitting them for chronic catarrhs, migraines, leprosy, and chronic bad sores on the head. The method of splitting them is as I describe.
It involves shaving the patient's head and rubbing its back where the two veins are with a coarse cloth thoroughly. Then, the patient tightens his neck with a turban until the two veins appear and their location behind the ears in the lower areas of the head is visible. You then grasp them with your finger where you feel their pulse under your finger, and there you mark them with ink.
Then take a knife scalpel, which is known as the nashl, then insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone, then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut off the vein along with the skin in a severed piece, and the length of the cut should be equal to two closed fingers.
Or you may release the amount of blood you desire, then tie them with bandages and leave them until they heal. Cutting and cauterizing them has been mentioned earlier in the book. As for the two arteries in the temples, blocking them is beneficial for chronic migraines, difficult headaches, persistent inflammation, and severe, continuous tearing around the eyes.
And the method of blocking them as I describe to you: the patient should tighten his neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly and their pulsation is visible under your finger. At this point, mark them with ink, then lift the skin above the vein slightly with your index finger.
Then insert the nashl scalpel from below, lift the vein upwards, and amputate it as you did with the other veins. Release blood according to your need, then loosen the patient's neck and place your finger on the vein for a moment.
Then place cotton and a bandage on it and tightly secure it from above, leaving it until it heals. Mentioning them, cutting them, and releasing blood from them has been covered earlier in the book. As for blocking the forehead vein, its benefit is after blocking the temple veins for chronic facial ailments such as leprosy, sores, and thick redness.
How to perform it as I describe to you: The patient chokes his neck with a turban until sweat appears, then take the tool which is called the axe, and this is its image:.
Place the sharp spike at the head of the axe on the same sweat spot and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and let the blood flow to the extent you desire, then loosen the patient's neck constriction and treat until healed.
It can be opened with a broad scalpel, but the scalpel should not have a sharp tip like other scalpels; rather, it should have a slightly wide tip. Perform it obliquely because the bone is close, and the scalpel might break if it is thin.
As for the sweat ducts at the inner corners of the eyes, their benefit is for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, trachoma, and facial diseases. As for how to perform their bloodletting, it is that the patient should constrict his neck with a turban, then you perform the bloodletting while standing at his head, and let the incision be slightly oblique lengthwise with a small, slightly broad lancet.
The area has no flesh, so if the lancet has a thin tip, it might break. Then release as much blood as you need, place cotton on them, bind them for one night, and then remove the bindings. As for bleeding the sweat duct of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fever.
And for severe headaches and facial ailments such as chronic red boils that appear on the nose, to perform the bloodletting, the patient should constrict his neck, then hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet.
You insert it in the middle of the nose itself between the nasal septum straight because the vein is not felt there then the blood gushes out immediately, and you should delve your hand with the scalpel a little and let out as much blood as you need then bind it for a night because it heals quickly.
As for the cheeks, their bloodletting is beneficial for breathlessness, the onset of leprosy, and blackish diseases that appear on the skin surface such as black leprosy, smallpox, bad sores, and ulcers. The method of bloodletting is to tie the patient under them in his neck with a bandage and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you perform bloodletting from the vein lengthwise with a somewhat wide incision, then let out a moderate amount of blood or according to what you judge as necessary, then do the same for the other vein, then remove the bandage.
And the veins are tied moderately so that the patient does not suffocate, and leave it until the next day as the wound will heal. As for the veins of the cheeks, their bloodletting after letting blood from the temples is beneficial for mouth ulcers, gum decay, bad sores, lip splits, and bad sores.
Which are in the nose and around it. The method of bloodletting is to have the patient sit in front of you, tie his neck with a turban, then turn his lips and look at the two veins that you see one on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, identified by their darkness.
And that also, around them are thin black veins which should be cut off entirely. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, aim to cut the larger and clearer ones. Do the same with the two veins in the lower lip.
And as for the two veins under the tongue, their benefit is to be slit after slitting the cheeks for sores that occur in the throat due to oral diseases. The method of slitting them is to seat the patient in front of you facing the sun, lift his tongue, and look beneath the tongue where on one side there is a vein and on the other side another vein, both of which are close to black in color.
You should slit them but be careful not to cut too deeply as there are arteries beneath them, and bleeding might occur from those arteries. As for the three veins that are slit at the elbow, they are the ones commonly slit in all people, and their slitting is done in two ways.
Either with a broad needle-like instrument or an olive-shaped one to thinness, or by slicing with a knife-like instrument, and these are the nashl [and this is its image], the image of the broad, needle-like instrument:.
It should be broad as you see, suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, visible veins that contain thick blood like sediment. This is the image of the olive-shaped instrument:.
This instrument is narrower and has a finer tip, suitable for slitting thin veins that contain thin, yellowish blood. This is the image of the nashl instrument:.
This tool is suitable for incision and has various types, broad and thin, according to the width and narrowness of the veins. This can be inferred for other uses as well, and it is famous among craftsmen.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of ligating it is that it draws blood from ailments that occur under the throat and neck, towards the chest and abdomen. The person who performs the ligation should be cautious and steady his hand, as there is an artery beneath it; if he makes a mistake and advances the needle further, he may cut that artery.
Bleeding may occur, therefore it should not be ligated with a needle but rather incised with a scalpel. If the basilic vein is not clearly visible, it should be avoided and another vein should be sought or one of its branches should be found.
Or you can ligate the arm tendon, as it is clear and should be incised with a scalpel as we said. If you want to ligate it specifically, you should feel the area before tightening the arm to identify the pulse location, then mark it with ink.
Then bind the arm and make a oblique incision on the vein with the scalpel as we said, ensuring that the strike is away from the artery's location. When you see swelling in the place you marked with ink while tightening the bandage, know that this swelling indicates the artery; avoid it.
If you see the blood spurt when incised, like a boy's urine, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is from an artery. In this case, quickly place your finger on it for a long moment, then remove your finger; if the bleeding stops, it often stops by tightening the arm.
Leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it. It should be around his neck without moving it for days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and you are overwhelmed and do not have medicine at hand when needed, then cut the artery if it appears to you, as its ends will retract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take the shell of a pistachio, split it, and take one half. Bind it tightly to the sweat spot with ties and bands until the next day. If the bleeding stops, otherwise treat it as previously mentioned by applying substances that stop bleeding. Stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases.
Due to the small size of the wound and the ability to tie tightly on the arm, be aware of this. As for the akhali sweat spot, its usefulness lies in drawing blood from the top of the head to the lower body because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic as we have said.
The person who ties it should do so around the neck where he applies the cautery, as there are nerves beneath. If he goes too deep with the needle and hits the nerve, numbness may occur which is difficult to heal, and sometimes does not heal at all. This nerve is often perceptible to touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not visible, you should make your incision with a probe and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the sweat spot is between two nerves, cut the sweat spot lengthwise. As for the cephalic sweat spot, its usefulness lies in drawing blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this particular sweat spot, if you wish to cauterize it, do so with stitches using an olive needle or a broad needle because it is the safest of all veins as there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
Except that it is necessary for you when incising to avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft area, and it does not harm if it does not hit in the first strike that it be repeated in incision several times except that it might swell in some people if it was not incised in the first strike.
But that swelling does not harm it at all. As for the manner of incision and its effects and what should precede in its correction, the first thing you should know is that incision may be used solely to preserve health.
Or it may be used for diseases. That which is used to preserve health, sustain it, and prevent the occurrence of diseases is to perform the incision in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, basilic, and brachial veins, and the incision should be performed at the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear.
The incision should be performed on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed since daytime began. As for the incision used for diseases, it does not have a limited time but is performed whenever necessity and need call for it, whether at night or day.
And at every hour and every time, it is not advisable to incise boys until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should the elderly who have surpassed sixty years be incised. When anyone intends to perform an incision for any reason.
It is advisable to cleanse his bowels before the incision with a mild enema if there is much retained feces, so that when the incision is made, no putrefactive residue from the intestines may be drawn into the veins and harm the vital organs. One should not incise one who has a fever, or who is drunk or intoxicated until these conditions have passed.
And beware of bloodletting also during menstruation, vomiting, defecation, overindulgence in intercourse, fatigue, exercise, insomnia, fasting, and everything that weakens the body or the mind. Then look to thinning the humors before that if the blood is thick.
By foods, drinks, and medicines if he can do so, then he enters the bath if nothing prevents him or exercises some exercise in order to thin the blood and makes his bloodletting in the middle of the day as we said and aims to clear his mind that day from all bad mental conditions.
Such as worries, anger, fear, and all other mental conditions, and from physical conditions such as excessive fatigue, overexertion, sexual intercourse, and the like. Prepare for his session things that people are accustomed to using, such as varieties of perfumes, fragrances, entertainment, and the like, each person according to what he can manage.
Then the bloodletter sits on a pillow that is higher than the pillow on which the person to be bled sits. Then he extends his arm, and the bloodletter scratches it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the ligature with the probe and twists it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
For the tightening, when it is not moderate, either by excessive tightening it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is too loose it also prevents the flow of blood. Then after the tightening, the person to be bled should scratch both hands with one another.
Until the veins swell and become noticeable to the touch. Then the bloodletter wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then places his left index finger on the vein itself below the spot where he intends to perform bloodletting slightly so that the vein does not move and avoids missing the mark. For there are veins that you will find like tendons, adhering when incised, and some are filled with air; so whenever the scalpel is applied to them, they fall below the scalpel and deceive the cutter, who does not open the vein with the scalpel, and even if he opens it, it would only be narrowly.
Therefore, the cutter should be careful and take his time with all these matters, then apply the scalpel; if he opens the vein in that attempt, otherwise he should repeat it slightly below or above that spot without haste if the area does not swell.
If it swells or the patient is distressed, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage as it might cause a hot swelling and he should not enter the bathhouse. Then, if desired, incision can be repeated; if the scalpel penetrates and the opening is small and the blood flow is thin.
And if you fear that the amount of blood you desire will not come out, then reuse the scalpel in the same hole gently and straight, slightly increasing the opening, and do this quickly before the area swells, for in many people, the area may swell even with a small incision.
If you see that it has swollen, do not repeat the incision at all as it will not help; instead, apply something with oil that facilitates blood flow, which is better for this area than oil itself and other ointments.
Use oil mixed with other substances in all vein incisions when blood flow is difficult. The Farouk antidote and schizonea can also be used; if either is applied to the area, the blood will become thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of bloodletting, it often happens, especially for those who have only been bled that one time or if the vein was cut small. So hurry and place on the swelling a sponge soaked in warm salt water and bind it for an hour, as it will dissolve.
It should be done after the blood has completely come out from that vein or another. If there remains some blackness or greenness in the place after a few days, it does not harm. If you wish, apply to it some dissolved aloes and vinegar.
Or something from the juice of fuddanj and the like. A swelling and ntu often occur when bloodletting the basilic vein, so place your hand on it. If you find it softens when you press it, then that is bad ntu, so be careful not to apply anything to it from what we mentioned.
For it may happen that artery blood flows from it, but dress it with something constricting to harden the area, then treat it with other treatments until it heals. It should be that you let out blood from every person according to their strength and what appears dominant in the color of the blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red. Similarly, if you see it thick, let it flow until it becomes thin. And if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides. It is appropriate for one who is full and strong and needs to have blood drawn all at once.
that he should widen the bloodletting incision and the lancet should be wide. For one who is weak, it is the opposite of that. It is appropriate to draw blood in multiple sessions and for the hole to be narrow. The best method for bloodletting is to use a deflected, tempered blade that makes a cut, not a stitch.
This type of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and severing nerves, and it is better and more reliable than bloodletting lengthwise or crosswise. For whoever faints during bloodletting, it is advisable to feed him some bread soaked in pomegranate juice or wine before the bloodletting.
If he is feverish, let the blood be drawn in three or four times. If his temperament is cold, let him take before bloodletting some bread soaked in hyacinth drink or in fragrant honey syrup with afawaih or in a good-smelling herbaceous drink.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and the cause is excessive loss of blood, it is advisable to give him meat broth and a light aromatic drink, use fragrant camphor to rub his chest, and employ all that we mentioned in the section on fainting.
That which pertains to emesis, and as for one who wishes to relieve his arm and release blood again, it is advisable for him whose bloodletting was for abundant emesis and whose strength is weak that the blood be released little by little according to his strength over consecutive days.
As for him who wishes to relieve his arm and release blood again and his body is strong, he should do so after seven or nine hours from the first bloodletting. As for him who wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite side it has inclined towards, it is advisable to relieve him on the second or third day.
As for him in whose body the blood is abundant, having become heated and intense and causing fever, it is advisable to draw out the blood all at once and extract a large quantity, widening the incision until fainting occurs, after being attentive to all the conditions of bloodletting. And that you place your hand on his pulse when the blood is flowing to prevent death from occurring instead of fainting, for that often happens if the operator is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it is not appropriate when you want to cut the arm and let the blood flow again.
And if the vein's mouth has closed and it is difficult for the blood to come out, do not poke it forcefully or twist strongly, as that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until you can treat it again or remove with a scalpel what of the blood has congealed in the vein's mouth.
Or you may apply to it some salt that has been dissolved in water, or apply some of the Farouk antidote or schyzandra, and gently poke it until the blood comes out. If the vein has become swollen, then leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If there is a necessity to let the blood flow again and it is unavoidable, then either treat above that spot or treat in the other arm or another vein.
As for bloodletting from the arm vein, it can be done instead of using al-akhil and basil if they are not available or hidden, because it is composed of both. The method of bloodletting is to have the patient immerse their hand in hot water until the wrist turns red and the vein becomes clearly visible, then bind above it slightly with a moderate tightness.
Then bloodlet from the vein slightly at an angle, neither crosswise nor lengthwise, and let the incision be wide. Your incision should be slightly above the wrist joint. If the blood does not flow easily, immerse the hand again in hot water and allow the blood to flow into the water.
Until you reach your need, if you are in the days of summer, you might do without immersing the hand in hot water; and mostly running blood in hot water is done in winter, and letting this vein is safer than all veins. Because there is no vital vein or nerve beneath it, and as for letting blood from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method of bleeding is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no vital vein or nerve beneath it, and as for letting blood from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method of bleeding is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells. It becomes very apparent to the sense, then bleed it slightly obliquely, and if you completely cut it off, it will not harm it at all. Be careful not to plunge the scalpel deeply as there is a nerve beneath it, and the area is bare of flesh. Then immerse the hand back into hot water.
Leave the blood to flow through it, for if you do not immerse it in hot water again, the blood will coagulate at the mouth of the vein and prevent further flow. Once you have extracted the necessary amount of blood, apply fat and salt to the vein so that it does not close quickly.
You should do the same with every narrow branch. As for letting blood from the left hand, it is beneficial for spleen ailments. Proceed in the same manner as you did with the previous one. As for letting blood from the superficial veins, it is beneficial for diseases in the lower body, such as uterus ailments.
And for the retention of menstruation, kidney diseases, chronic sores on the thighs and legs, and similar ailments. The method of bleeding is to immerse his foot in hot water and massage until the veins dilate, then bind above the ankle joint with a tourniquet, placing the vein at the instep, outward toward the big toe.
It branches out into many branches on the face of the man, so open it in its widest branch or at the ankle where it converges, for that is better and safer. If you open it on the face of the man, be careful of the nerves beneath it on the face of the man.
Make your opening for him oblique as if you want to amputate it, and the incision should be slanted. If the blood does not come out easily, let him put his leg in hot water and allow the blood to flow out until it is emptied. If the bleeder misses the vein with the first attempt, try again.
Let him return with the incision slightly upwards, for the place is safe and there is no fear from it if you are careful of the nerve as we said. And likewise, do the same with the other leg's ankle. As for the sciatic vein, its location is as we said, at the heel on the outer side.
The benefit of its incision for hip pain, if that is due to hot blood, and the method of its incision is that the patient should enter the bath and then quickly tie his leg from the hip up to four fingers above the ankle with a thin long scarf, for it will not be visible otherwise.
When it appears, incise it in whichever state you find it, either obliquely, which is better, or amputate it entirely or split it. Its location is safe, and it is very hidden in most people, so if you do not find it.
If it does not appear at all to the touch, then incise some of its branches, which are visible on the back of the foot toward the little toe and the ring toe, and be careful of the nerves. Let as much blood as you desire.
Then relieve the tension and place cotton on the area of bloodletting and bind the area tightly, for it will heal quickly.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On bloodletting and how to use it.
Leeches can be made of horns, wood, copper, and glass. Bloodletting is done in two ways: one by incision and drawing blood, and the other without incision.
And this bloodletting without incision is done in two ways.
And the bloodletting points of the cheeks, which are both sides of the neck, and the bloodletting points of the chin, which is below the lower jaw of the mouth, and the bloodletting points of the shoulders, and the bloodletting points of the ankles at the end of the heel, and the bloodletting points of the hips, which are in the middle of the arms, and the bloodletting points of the legs, and the bloodletting points of the calves.
Bloodletting draws blood from the thin veins spread in the flesh, and for this reason, it does not weaken as much as bloodletting by incision. It is not appropriate to use bloodletting, whether with fire or without fire, in any of the diseases that result from excess until the entire body has been purged.
If there is a need for bloodletting due to illness or as a customary practice, we use it at any time, whether at the beginning of the month, at the end, in the middle, or at any other time.
And it is that among people, when blood increases in them to the point where it is necessary to expel it through cupping, some feel heaviness and headache in their head, and some experience fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck, and some feel itching on their face and side and darkness and opacity in their eyes, and some scratch the area of their cupping.
And some laugh excessively, and some find the taste of blood in their mouth, their gums bleed, and they spit out blood. Some sleep a lot, and some see blood, redness, killings, surgeries, and things like that in their dreams. Whenever we see any of this.
And especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed since noon. As for the benefit of the nisfah cupping, it is beneficial for heaviness in the head and water running into the eyes.
But it should be done after the entire body has been purged. This cupping can be a substitute for splitting the temple. It is cautioned that those who have a cold brain or have had a stroke should not use it, as it will greatly harm them.
For that reason, it should not be used by the elderly and those who have cold diseases in their heads, and those who are addicted to it may develop forgetfulness.
For that reason, it is advisable to instruct the cupper to lower the cup slightly downward to avoid causing forgetfulness. As for the cupping of the shoulder, it is a substitute for splitting the canthus and splitting the basilic vein.
It is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, respiratory organ fractures, coughing, and fullness. The shoulder cupping should be raised slightly because if it is placed too low, it may cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
And as for the cupping of the axillae, it is beneficial for pains occurring in the head, trachoma, migraine, choking, and pain at the roots of the teeth, and it is a substitute for extracting the molars. It is appropriate to order the cupper not to deepen his hand with the lancet lest he cut an artery causing bleeding.
And as for the cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for mouth sores and gum decay and similar diseases in the mouth, and it can serve as a substitute for extracting the molars that are in the lips. As for cupping on the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
And as for the cupping of the two flanks, it is beneficial for what extracting the three veins, basiliq, akhoul, and qayful, can heal because it attracts blood from all those thin veins in the flesh, and these thin veins attract from other thicker veins until the attraction reaches the three thick veins.
It is appropriate to order the cupper not to deepen in cutting because the area lacks flesh and has nerves and arteries underneath. As for a single cupping applied to the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
It is appropriate to order the cupper that the cupping vessel should be large and made of copper because the area requires strong suction, and a glass cup might break and require frequent cutting. As for cupping on the legs, it noticeably reduces fullness.
Because it attracts blood from the entire body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, it induces menstruation and is helpful for blisters and boils, and can serve as a substitute for extracting the saffan and arquub veins. However, it significantly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people. The benefit of cupping on the arquub veins is similar to that of cupping on the legs.
The method of applying leeches is to first place the leech empty and let it suck moderately; do not prolong the application of leeches but rather place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the mixtures are evenly accepted at the site. Continue repeating this until you see the area has reddened and opened up.
When the redness of the blood appears, then you should incise and gradually resume suction. Then examine the condition of the bodies; for those whose flesh is delicate with loose pores, it is appropriate to incise only once so that the area does not become ulcerated.
You instruct the cupper to widen the incision, deepen it slightly, and adjust the suction gently with a delicate movement. If the blood is thick, it should be incised twice. In the first instance, open a path for the thin blood and its moisture, and in the second, to thoroughly remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, the incision should be repeated a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to extract a small amount of blood, one incision suffices; if we wish to extract a large amount of blood, we make more incisions, considering that the blood is thick.
We should make a deep incision, and the moderate limit for the incision is only the depth of the skin. What ointments should be used when applying leeches, what waters are appropriate, and what precautions the person undergoing cupping should takeâ€”these considerations apply especially to those whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and has tight pores.
You should anoint the places where leeches will be applied with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments. In summer, for example, use ointments like that of styrax, violet, sweet almond, or pumpkin seed.
And if it is in winter, then like narcissus oil or lily oil or chamomile oil or hyacinth oil and the like. If the residue is thick and cold, let the oil be mustard oil or linseed oil or banana oil or spitha oil and the like.
If the cupped individual has wide pores and tender flesh, it is advisable to abstain from oil. These individuals should wash their cupping marks after cupping with rose water or cold water or foxglove water or pumpkin water or fennel water and the like. As for those whose blood is very moist.
He should wash the cupping marks with vinegar or with great burnet and smock water and the like. As for those whose residues are thick, they should wash the cupping marks with old wine or mustard oil or cooked spith or chamomile and the like. It is advisable to avoid cupping in the bathhouse and immediately after bathing.
Rather, it should be used one or two hours after leaving the bathhouse. It is not advisable for anyone to sleep after cupping. One should take care of the cupped and bloodlet individual before and after the procedure.
You must first examine if the cupped or bloodlet individual is bilious and if his blood tends to be sharp and inflamed, it is advisable for him to take cooling substances such as pomegranate, chicory with vinegar, lettuce, sorrel, syrups, and the like. His meals should consist of chickens, lamb stews, alkanet, and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, it is advisable to give him honey syrup or sorrel with seeds or burdock. He should consume moderately aged wine that lies between old and new wine. He should be advised to eat less and his food should consist of chickens, pigeons, sparrows, and squab stews. The drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting should be more than the food.
And it may be necessary to give at certain times to some people theriac, or musk medicine, or sylphium before cupping or bloodletting, or after, to strengthen the vital organs and thin the blood. It should not be given to those who are delirious.
And as for the cupping that is done without scarification, it is the cupping that is placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and hip joint because these organs cannot tolerate scarification; rather, the intention is to draw blood from one organ to another.
Placing the cupping vessels on the breasts in the case of coryza, or using them to expel a cold wind that has lodged in an organ, such as placing the cupping vessels on the abdomen and navel, for they loosen the organ, warm it, and relieve pain by expelling that wind.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stone, for its strong suction might open the obstruction or dislodge the stone from its place. It does the same when placed on the liver and spleen when a wind is congested in them. These cups may be used empty with suction only.
And they may be used with fire, or they may be filled with warm water for ailments like scabies. This is done by filling the cup, which should be large, with hot water alone or with water in which some herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Then it is placed, filled, on the area, held in place, removed, and repeated as needed. This is a depiction of the cup that is used with fire:
Its mouth width is two spread fingers as depicted, and its depth measures half a finger-breadth. There is a small hole on its side, about halfway, through which a needle can pass; it is made of Chinese brass or yellow brass with a thick rim.
It should be smooth, flat, and polished so that it does not harm the organ when placed on it. There is a cross slot in its center made of brass or iron where the candle with fire is placed. This cupping vessel can also be made larger than described or smaller according to the disease.
And regarding its use, the cupping vessels for children and thin individuals are different from those for men and obese bodies. As for how to place this cupping vessel with fire on the organ, ignite a wick made of tightly woven cotton or a small wax candle and place it in the center of the hollow that is in the middle of the cupping vessel.
The fire should rise upwards towards the bottom of the cupping vessel to prevent the patient's body from burning. Then place it on the organ with a finger over the hole mentioned until, when we hold the cupping vessel firmly, we can remove the finger.
The steam will escape through that hole, and the cupping vessel will loosen in place. Then re-ignite the wick as described and repeat if necessary. As for the cupping vessel used for treating hemorrhoids with water, it has no solid stem nor a hole.
It is used by filling it with water and placing it on the organ only, and this is its shape:.
And this cupping vessel is better when it is large to hold a lot of water.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the treatment of phlegm.
Phlebotomy is mostly used in organs where cupping cannot be applied, either because they are too small, like the lip and gum and the like, or because the organ is bare of flesh, like the finger and nose and the like.
And the manner of using them is to seek leeches that are in pure fresh water, free from infections, then leave them in fresh water for a day and night until they hunger and nothing remains in their gut. Then the body is first purged by venesection or cupping, then the affected part is wiped.
Until it turns red, then place the leeches on it; when they are full, they will fall off. If possible, cupping the area is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water, rub and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off and this is a flux, soak a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it on top until the flux stops. If bleeding is profuse, sprinkle powdered zager or alum or similar astringents until the bleeding stops, or apply peeled split fennel seeds to the area.
Leave it until the fennel sticks to the area, for the bleeding will stop. If leeches need to be reapplied, do not use the same leech if possible; use others instead. If the leeches refuse to attach, wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the place until some blood comes out then it is placed for if it senses some blood it sticks to its position so when you want to remove it sprinkle on it some storax or salt or ashes so it falls off in place.
The Third Chapter.
On Fractures.
This chapter also deals with what is necessary in the art of medicine, which is setting fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter may be dismissed by ignorant physicians and laymen, and by those who have never browsed through the books of our predecessors on this subject nor read a single letter from them.
Because of this reason, this branch of knowledge has become non-existent in our country, and indeed, I have never come across anyone who is proficient in it at all. And what I have gained from it is due to my extensive reading of the books of the early scholars and my eagerness to understand them until I extracted that knowledge from them, then I devoted myself to practice and experience throughout my life.
And I have outlined for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience has validated it after I have presented it to you, purified it from the complexities of lengthiness, condensed it as much as possible, clarified it to the utmost, and depicted for you numerous illustrations of the instruments used in it.
For it is an addition to the clarification, as I have done in the previous two chapters, and there is no strength except by Allah the Most High, the Greatest.
The first chapter.
In this chapter and in the summary of the matter of bone fractures, it is necessary to present them. Before we begin to mention the broken and dislocated limbs one by one, it should be mentioned at the beginning of this chapter some sayings and sections. It first necessitates you to understand it and grasp its true nature, you and whoever is eager to learn this noble craft besides you.
I say that when any individual suffers a fracture, dislocation, bruise, or fall, you should first attend to his bloodletting or purging, or both. Unless there is a hindrance, such as weakness of strength, or if the one who experienced any of these events is a child or an elderly person, or if the time is extremely hot or cold.
Then limit his diet to cold legumes, poultry, and goat meat, and forbid drinks, heavy meats, overeating, and any food that fills the veins with blood until you are certain there will be no swelling and do not expect any substance to flow to the affected area.
Then the patient should return to his usual regimen. When the fractured bone begins to heal, the patient should be nourished with nutritious, substantial, strong foods that are binding, such as dates, rice, heads, joints, and cattle stomachs. And eggs, soft fish, thick drinks, and the like, for with this regimen, the healing of the fracture will be faster and better. Know that fractured bones in strong men and elderly people cannot reconnect and heal to their original nature ever.
Their bones are dry and strong, but what was extremely soft, like the bones of small children, can connect and heal. However, nature grows on all sides of the broken bone something resembling glue that is thick, adheres to it, and strengthens until they firmly join together.
It binds them together until it reaches the highest strength and solidity as it was at first, so that nothing hinders its actions.
For this reason, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are strong, sticky, and thick, as we have mentioned. Know that fractures vary in type according to the differences in body parts because a leg bone fracture is different from a skull bone fracture, and a chest bone fracture is different from a spine bone fracture.
And similarly, all other body parts differ from one another. We will discuss each type of fracture in detail in its own section, separately. The type of bone fracture may also vary on its own.

If there is no apparent bending, no crackling sound, and you do not feel any disturbance when touching the bone, and the patient does not experience much pain, then there is no fracture. But apply to it the medicines that will be mentioned later what suits the place, then bind it gently. Know that if the bone is broken and severed into two withoutç¢Žç‰‡ä¹‹é—´çš„ä»»ä½•éª¨æŠ˜ï¼Œå½¼æ­¤çš„æ‰€æœ‰éƒ¨åˆ†éƒ½å·²ç§»å¼€ã€‚
You should immediately set it and align it before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it as gently and skillfully as you can.
Know that setting and aligning it is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred. Then bind it as will be mentioned later. If the bone has fragments, it is necessary to stretch the broken limb from both sides, whether an arm or a leg.
Do so with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both rope and hand. Place the limb on a flat surface in its natural form until the broken bone extends. At that point, gently and skillfully return those protrusions to their places.
Strive to ensure that your actions do not cause pain or swelling to the patient. Aim to join one part of the bone with its counterpart in the best possible form. During this time, touch and feel both parts with your hand; if you find anything amiss, correct and align it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and strong manipulation, as many unskilled individuals do. Often, their actions cause hot swelling or dislocation in the limb, which you may have observed from their practices on multiple occasions.
Then, after theå¹³æ•´ã€ç²¾ç¡®å’Œæ‹‰ç´§ï¼Œå¿…é¡»è®©è¯¥è‚¢ä½“ä¿æŒé™æ­¢å’Œå®‰å®ï¼Œå¹¶å‘Šè¯«ç—…äººåœ¨æ¸…é†’æ—¶ã€ç¡çœ ä¸­ã€ç¿»èº«ä¸å®‰æ—¶ã€æŽ’ä¾¿æ—¶ä»¥åŠæ‰€æœ‰åŠ¨ä½œä¸­å°½æœ€å¤§å¯èƒ½ä¸è¦ç§»åŠ¨å®ƒï¼Œå¹¶ç¡®ä¿è‚¢ä½“çš„ä½ç½®æ˜¯å®‰å…¨æ— ç—›çš„ã€‚
For if he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, it should be moved to another position where he does not feel pain, while ensuring that this new position is level and straight.
So that no deformity occurs in the limb when it is set. As for how to bind the broken limb, I will describe it to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their size and shape; those that are small, such as the arm, finger, ankle, and the like.
The bandages should be soft and gentle for smaller limbs. For larger, tougher areas like the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be wide and strong. The broad binding is necessary for large limbs to hold them tightly from all sides with equal tension, without any imbalance.
Then, after you have finished leveling it, you should apply the appropriate coating. Place a gentle padding over the site of the fracture, then begin wrapping the bandage around the fracture itself three or four times, depending on what the limb requires. Tighten the bandage slightly with your hand and then extend it to the upper part of the fractured area.
Apply less tension than you did on the broken area, then move the wrapping slightly away from the fracture and gradually loosen the tension until you achieve a suitable position. Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
Wrap it, then take the wrapping to the lower side of the fracture. Let your action in tightening and loosening the wrap be as we mentioned in the first upper wrap. Then place between the bandages soft plaster or cloths that straighten the deformity of the fracture.
If there is a deformity, otherwise put nothing in it, then wrap another bandage over it. Then immediately apply tight plaster casts on these wraps if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply something that reduces the swelling and eliminates the inflammation. Leave it for a few days, then apply the plaster casts afterward. The plaster casts should be made from split, broad, wisely carved reeds or made from colander wood.
Which are made from pine, palm fronds, willow, linen, or similar materials that are available. The width of each plaster cast should be in this form and exactly this shape.
