Except that the plaster cast which is placed directly on the fracture should be thicker and slightly wider than the other casts. As for the length of the cast, it should be made according to what is appropriate for the size of the broken limb, whether large or small. The form of the cast:
Then tighten another bandage over the casts in the same way as your first tightening. Then secure them from above with tight threads as we mentioned for tightening, which is that your tightening should be tighter at the site of the fracture. The tightening should be less as it moves further away from the fracture.
The threads should be of moderate thickness and fineness, and they should be made of wet linen especially because if the threads are thick, like what I have seen ignorant people do when they make their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a great error.
Because the tension with them falls outside of moderation, and very thin threads are also not suitable because you will not achieve the desired tension with them. There should not be less than a finger's width between one bandage and another.
If the patient is bothered by the edges of the bandages after they have been properly tightened, place soft padding or loose wool under them so that nothing bothers him. As for when the fracture is accompanied by a wound and there is a tear in the skin, we will discuss this separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be bandaged on the first day. If the limb is large, bandages should not be applied until after five, seven days, or even more, depending on your assurance that a hot swelling will not occur.
This is the description of the bandages that were used by the early practitioners for fractures, dislocations, and swellings. It is a general and concise bandaging method that can be applied to fractures and suits most temperaments, especially children and women, as it does not tend to become too hot or cold.
Take the flour dust from the mill, which adheres to the walls of the mill when the mill is in motion. Knead it as it is without sieving, using egg whites, and make its consistency neither too thick nor too thin, then apply it.
The description of another bandage that sets fractures, dislocations, and swellings. Take from wool, ladanum, aqiqia, rasn, malath, and sugar ten dirhams each; and vinegar and saffron five dirhams each; and manna twenty dirhams; and Armenian or Roman clay twenty dirhams. Grind all of it and mix with manna water or egg white if the patient's temperament is irascible. Then use this bandage, for it is well-compounded, quickly sets broken bones, and is suitable for most people due to its balance.
The description of another bandage that is also beneficial for fractures and swellings. Take malath, wool, and white khartoum ten dirhams each; vinegar and saffron five dirhams each; aqiqia six dirhams; Armenian clay twenty dirhams. Grind all finely, sieve, knead with water or egg white, and use.
The description of a bandage for joints and bones that have moved from their places, to relieve the accompanying pain, bone cracking, and fractures. Take white wool, soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area. Note that this bandage does not have the strength to set bones.
However, it is excellent for reducing hot swelling and alleviating pain particularly. The description of another bandage that sets broken bones: take the leaves of the black fig tree and wild poppy, grind them together, and apply them moistly. The description of another concise bandage used when setting a bone fracture.
When you want to reduce the remaining swelling, take equal parts of khartoum root, chamomile, purple woad, and ground cranesbill. Grind all of them finely, knead with ointment if the limb is not inflamed; if it is inflamed, then knead with coriander water or plain water, and apply it.
Description of another bandage that is stronger in dissolution than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs at the fracture of the bone: take the root of hemlock, flaxseed, fenugreek, marigold, mastic, purplebine, and chamomile, one part of each.
It is pounded and kneaded with alkaline water or fresh water or vinegar, all of this according to the heat of the limb and its inflammation cooling. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb when setting it.
Except for the cerotici made from wax and oil alone, and it is described as being moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he opined that when setting a broken limb, dry things with some heat should be applied, such as salt, gum arabic, frankincense, and the like of what we have mentioned.
Description of a bandage that is beneficial for weakness and pain: take slaked lime, chickpeas, human hair that is cut or bird feathers, hemlock, and salt, all in equal parts. Pound them, sieve, knead, and use it as a bandage. As for the duration the bandage should remain.
Then comes the removal, which you should assess by observing if there is no pain or irritation and the broken bone does not move from its place; do not remove it for many days. If there is severe irritation or disturbing pain or swelling in the area, however.
Hasten to remove it at that time and do not delay at all, then remove the bandage. Take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, soak it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the irritation and pain subside, then let the limb rest for an hour.
Then apply onto it the cleaned wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose fat and bind it on for a night until you are safe from the hot swelling and the inflation of the organ subsides and its pain disappears then return it to gentle binding and light dressing and do not bind it as tightly as before.
Nurse it until it heals, if you see that the swelling, redness, pain, inflation and all symptoms have completely disappeared and you need dressing and binding then redo it as you did first equally, if nothing of what we mentioned happens in the organ.
Do not remove it until three or four or five or seven days have passed and sometimes it may be left for twenty days, all of this depending on what appears to you regarding the condition of the limb as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh around it begins to heal.
Increase the binding more than your first binding entirely, and also increase the thickness of the patientâ€™s diet as previously mentioned. If you see that the fracture site has dried and become thin more than it should, know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Wash it with lukewarm water each time you remove the binding, and let this be done every three days, and slightly loosen the binding. With this action, nourishment will flow to the limb and it will heal quickly. As for what the ignorant among those who set fractures do, which is breaking the limb again.
If it does not set properly the first time and sets crookedly, this is an error in their practice and a great deception. Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and followed it, but I have seen no trace of such a thing from any of them at all. And it is correct that it should not be acted upon.
Chapter Two.
On Transverse Fractures in the Head.
Transverse fractures in the head are diverse, their forms are different, and their causes are varied. Some fractures result from a sword blow, where the bone may be split entirely until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. Just as an axe does to wood.
This type of fracture is called an axe fracture. Alternatively, the sword may cut part of the bone and split its surface without penetrating completely, which is called a chipping fracture. The wounds from these fractures can be either large or small.
There are also fractures that result from crushing or contusion, caused by a blow with a stone or falling onto a stone or similar objects. This type of fracture may either penetrate close to the membrane beneath the bone or affect only the surface of the bone.
The wounds from these fractures can also be either large or small. There are also hidden fractures in the bone, at the thinness of hair, which is a slightè£‚çº¹ã€‚
This type of fracture is called a hairline fracture. There are also fractures resulting from falls or impacts with stones, where the surface of the bone indents inward, causing a depression similar to what happens to a copper pot when struck, causing part of it to indent inward.
This is most common in the soft bones of heads, such as those of children. All these types of fractures may have dislodged fragments or not. We will discuss the treatment of all these in their respective places, and we identify all these types of fractures through examination.
And examine it with lancets and extract the rotten flesh from it. As for the hairy type, it is recognized by uncovering the bone, wiping it, and smearing ink on it; then the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of the fracture, first look at the symptoms of the patient.
If you see symptoms that clearly indicate fear, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of mind, cessation of voice, fainting, acute fever, protrusion and redness of the eyes, and other similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him, for death will inevitably occur with these symptoms in most cases.
And if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his safety, then at that time proceed with his treatment. This is by taking the wounded person when he first gets injured, especially if this happens in the winter months.
It is advisable to strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case. If it is during the summer months, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day to prevent what is under the bone, the membrane, fromè…çƒ‚ï¼Œä»Žè€Œé¿å…å‡ºçŽ°æˆ‘ä»¬æè¿°çš„é‚£äº›ç—‡çŠ¶ã€‚è¯·æ³¨æ„ï¼Œæœ€åŽä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†çš„ç¿»è¯‘â€œfè…çƒ‚â€æ˜¯é”™è¯¯çš„å»¶ç»­ï¼Œæ­£ç¡®çš„è¯‘æ–‡åº”ä¸ºâ€œto prevent the membrane under the bone fromè…çƒ‚â€ï¼Œä½†æ ¹æ®æ‚¨çš„è¦æ±‚ï¼Œæˆ‘ä¸èƒ½æ·»åŠ ä»»ä½•è§£é‡Šæˆ–ä¿®æ­£ã€‚å®Œæ•´çš„æ­£ç¡®ç¿»è¯‘åº”è¯¥æ˜¯ï¼šâ€œIt is advisable to strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case. If it is during the summer months, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day to prevent what is under the bone, the membrane, from rotting and thus causing the symptoms we described.â€ä½†ç”±äºŽè§„åˆ™é™åˆ¶ï¼Œæˆ‘ä¸èƒ½æä¾›ä¿®æ­£ã€‚
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is fragmentation and crushing, you should cut off the crushed and fragmented part as I describe to you, which is to shave the head of the injured patient.
And expose the bone in whatever manner is feasible for you, according to the shape of the wound and what is easier for the patient. If bleeding or a hot swelling appears when exposing the bone, address it as appropriate.
It is to fill the place with cloth soaked in a mixture of wine and rose oil, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding is controlled, then proceed with setting the bone and extracting it, which can be done in one of two ways.
As for the one method, it is to cut the bone with a sharp narrow-bladed cutter, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter after this one, slightly wider, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter, even wider than the second. It is necessary to have a set of different cutters, some wider than others and some shorter than others, with extremely sharp edges. They should be made of Indian iron or good steel.
Use gentleness when hitting the cutter so as not to shake the head and harm it. If the bone is strong and hard, you should drill around it before using the cutters with drills that are called non-penetrating drills. They are named non-penetrating drills.
Because they do not penetrate beyond the skull bone to what is behind it. The drill has a rounded edge below its sharp point, similar to a ring or small circle, which prevents it from sinking in and going beyond the thickness of the bone.
You should have a variety of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have a drill whose sharp point is the appropriate length and width for the thickness of each skull. This is the form of three types. Of the large, medium, and small perforators:.
As for the manner of piercing around the fractured bone, it is to place the perforator on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then move the perforator to another location, making sure there is a distance between each hole equal to the thickness of a needle or so.
Then, you cut between each two holes with the cutters and do this with utmost gentleness as we said until you remove the bone, either by hand or with another tool from those prepared for that purpose.
Such as glue and delicate clamps, and you should be extremely careful that the perforator or cutter does not touch any part of the intestines. If you have scraped the bone clean and it has healed from any attached intestines, and you have removed them, then you should dress the wound.
Smooth out any roughness remaining in the bone with another tool that resembles the cutter, except it should be thinner and more delicate than other cutters. If any small bones or shards remain, remove them gently with the tools you have.
Then treat the wound with bandages and ointments which will be mentioned. As for the other method of procedure, it is quite simple and free from danger, as Galen mentioned and greatly praised. This is what he says. You should first begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent. Once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this lens-shaped cutter underneath it, and here is its shape:.
The lens part of it should be smooth, not cutting anything, and the sharp part of it should be on its lengthwise sides as you see so that the lens part rests against the peritoneum and the sharp face of the cut is in the bone. Then strike on one side of the cut.
With a small mallet until all the bone is cut gently as it turns, and you are safe from any accident in the membrane that could be frightening at all, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and timid of people. Indeed, even if he were drowsy.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the edge of the lens-shaped cutter itself and remove it gently, for it can be removed without harm or fear.
And if the bone fracture has not penetrated to the membrane, and what is broken off from the bone is its surface, with roughness and fine splinters remaining, you should remove that roughness and extract those splinters with fine chisels that have been prepared in various sizes so that you can use the most suitable and appropriate one for each place according to what the work guides you to.
And the nature of the broken bone and its shape, you should use the widest of those chisels first for removing the bone, then use a finer one after it. Continue doing this sequentially until you reach using the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for other small, hairline cracks and delicate fractures, you should treat each one according to what leads to its healing. This is something not hidden from anyone who has even a little skill in this craft and has understood what we have written and explained about larger fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, you should take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And soak it in a mixture of rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do this as gently as you can so as not to weigh down the peritoneum. Then apply a wide bandage on top and tighten it only enough to hold the wound closed.
You leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are certain that the heat has subsided, then remove it and use medicines that have a drying effect such as the roots of lilies, crushed cress, powdered barley, and zinc oxide and similar ones. You make a powder from these medicines.
And sprinkle the wound with them when they are dry. In general, use a medicine that cleanses without irritating. Strive to keep the treatment of the wound clean, free from any impurities or dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate at all.
For if pus accumulates on the membrane of the brain, it will corrupt and putrefy it, causing great harm to the patient. Pus may also appear in the pericranium when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If there is blackness on its surface, and the patient exhibits the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is surely doomed. If the blackness occurred due to a medicine applied and the strength of that medicine caused such blackness, note that.
It is necessary to take a part of honey and three parts of rose oil, mix them well together, apply them on a cloth, then place it on the hernia, and treat it with various treatments as needed until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of the nose if it is broken.
Know that only the upper sides of the nose break entirely or one of them because they are bones, whereas the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break; rather, it may suffer from swelling, distortion, and indentation.
If one of its sides is broken, you should insert the little finger into the nostril and align the fracture from inside with your index finger and thumb from outside until you restore the nose to its natural shape. Do this gently and ensure that your actions do not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, it should be aligned with a slightly thickened blunt end. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same. Promptly set it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise wait until the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides. Then insert a wick of cotton cloth into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or insert two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some of the early practitioners have mentioned that the wick should be soaked in fat and changed daily, but I do not agree with this. Instead, the wick should be soaked in egg white mixed with mill powder.
Then leave the splint until the bone is stable and the cartilage solidifies, and you can place tubes of duck feathers in the nose where the splint is after wrapping them with soft cloths so that it holds the nasal fracture more firmly and to prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
And this is not something necessary; if you wish, you can make it, and if you wish, you can make the wicks. If during your work a hot swelling appears on the nose, dress the nose with kerotiy or with cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or some of the diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, it is advisable to dress it externally with semolina flour and ground millet that has been kneaded with egg white, then place a soft bandage on it without binding the nose at all. If the nasal bones are fractured in small fragments.
Or if they are shattered, it is advisable to incise over them and remove them with the appropriate tool, then suture the incision and treat it with ointments that promote healing and suppuration. If a wound occurs inside the nose, it should be treated with splints and lead tubes should be used. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of a fractured lower jaw.
If the lower jaw is fractured and there is no accompanying wound, examine it. If the fracture is only from the outside and has not broken into two pieces nor indented inward, then its treatment is easier.
It is necessary that if the fracture is on the right side, then insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth. And similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, then insert the index finger of the right hand and gently lift the prominence of the fracture from inside to outside.
And with your other hand from outside the bone, you control its alignment. If the jaw fracture has two breaks, it is necessary to use traction from both sides in a straight line until you can align it properly. If there has been misalignment of the teeth.
Or if it spreads, sew what you hope will remain with a gold or silver thread or silk. Then place kerotie on the broken cheekbone, then put a folded cloth over it, and place a large tight bandage or a piece of sole leather equal to the length of the cheek over the cloth. Then bind it from above according to what seems appropriate for binding and ensuring it is compressed so that it does not come undone. Instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and provide them with soft, easily digestible food. If you suspect that any part of the form has changed in any way, act promptly to loosen it on the third day.
Then bind it from above according to what seems appropriate for binding and ensuring it is compressed so that it does not come undone. Instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and provide them with soft, easily digestible food. If you suspect that any part of the form has changed in any way, act promptly to loosen it on the third day. Then prepare what you bandage with flour from the mill and egg white or with semolina flour after removing the kerotie, and place a soft cloth over the bandage. As long as that bandage sticks to it and no change occurs in the bone, leave it as is.
Do not loosen it until it heals and the fracture strengthens. This fracture often strengthens in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during that time, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to reduce it until the swelling disappears.
And if the fracture is with a wound, look to see if any bone splinters have become detached; extract those splinters delicately using whatever method you find suitable for their removal from the instrument. If the opening of the wound is narrow, widen it with the scalpel as needed.
Then, after you have extracted those splinters and none remain, suture the opening of the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that. Suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of a fractured collarbone.
The collarbone most often fractures in front of the shoulder end, and its fracture can occur in one of three ways: either it breaks and curves into two without splintering, which is easier to treat.
Or splinters may occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to treat. Or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, prepare two assistants; one to hold the arm adjacent to the broken collarbone and the other to extend the neck toward the opposite side.
Then, align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, without any protrusions or indentations. If more extension is needed, you should place a ball of cloth or wool under the armpit of the patient.
The bone should be adjusted to your need, extend and lift the collarbone, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is aligned as it should be. If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outward because it has moved inward, the patient should lie on their back.
And place a moderately thick pillow under his shoulder, and the servant presses his shoulder downwards until the clavicle which is in depth rises upwards; then adjust and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, you should incise over it and gently remove that fragment. If the fragment is lodged in the bone, you must devise a way to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for this purpose. After placing the device under the clavicle that protects the peritoneum, which is an instrument made of wood or iron, and this is its shape:
It resembles a spoon without a handle, and its width is according to the size of the bone, whether large or small. As for its length, it is according to what is convenient for the work. Let it have two ends, as you see, one end being wider and the other narrower.
If the incision you made to remove the bone fragment is wide and you are not concerned about a hot swelling, then suture the edges of the wound together. If the incision is small or if you fear a hot swelling, then pack the wound with lint and bandages according to the extent of the cut.
If a hot swelling occurs, then soak the lint in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting the bone without cutting or incision, you should apply a bandage made from mill powder mixed with egg white to the bone. Place a soft pad on it, then place a ball under his armpit if needed. Then take a long scarf, about a finger-width wide or so, and apply a folded bandage over the pad and dressing. Then take a splint from a thin board. Its width is three fingers and in length the same, then insert it into a cloth, then insert that cloth with the splint into the part of the turban which falls on the site of the fracture, then tighten the turban around the fracture as it circles. And place it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under his injured armpit, wrap it several times on each side, however you see fit for the tension to securely hold the fracture in place, which is not unknown to you.
The aim of all this is that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day. Whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on their back and place a small pillow under their armpit when they sleep at night.
Let it raise his arm from his side so that the clavicle fracture rises with the shoulder, or bind his arm to his neck. Do not remove the bandage unless an incident occurs in the area such as itching or swelling for twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a need for it. And maintain the tension, leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and sets, and it strengthens more, which takes twenty-eight days. And it may be less than that for some people.
Chapter Six.
On the setting of a fractured shoulder. The shoulder rarely breaks at its broad part; rather, it fractures at its narrow parts. Whenever it breaks at any point or in its middle, this is known by touch. Therefore, the treatment should correspond to the form of the fracture.
Straighten and return it to its natural shape with all the skill you can muster, then apply mill powder mixed with egg white and soft adhesive. Place a pad of folded cloth over this, then secure it with a wide board splint.
The splint should be wide enough to cover the entire shoulder or slightly wider. If there is an indentation under the splint in any part of the shoulder, fill that indentation with a soft adhesive until the splint lies flat. Then secure it with a long headband above.
Tie it firmly and secure the splint so that it does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day, and whenever it loosens, tighten it. If the splint moves from its place, adjust it. The patient should lie on his healthy side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty days.
For twenty or twenty-five days. Once this period is over, remove the bandage with confidence, for it is one of those bones that are not feared to crumble or collapse. If a fragment protrudes from the bone and pierces under the skin, make an incision over it and extract it. And do as mentioned for the fracture of the clavicle to alleviate any heat swelling that may occur.
Chapter Seven.
On setting a rib fracture.
The chest may fracture in its middle, though this is rare. More often, the sides are fractured and shattered. Its symptoms when its middle breaks include tilting downwards, severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
And sometimes, the fractured bone may become sunken, which is evident and not hidden to the sense. To set it, the patient should lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulders, then press on their shoulder joints, bring the ribs together from both sides with hands, and carefully align them as much as possible.
Continue until the bone returns to its proper shape, then apply bandages and pressure, and place a splint made from a light board of willow or sycamore or similar lightweight material over it after wrapping it in cloth. Then carefully tie it to the fractured bone so that it does not move, wrap the bandage around in a circular manner towards the back several times and secure it tightly, then check the bandage frequently, and tighten it whenever it loosens.
And if necessity calls for removing it due to infection, pain, or swelling in the area, promptly remove it, take off the bandage, and treat whatever occurred accordingly, then reapply the bandage if you see fit. And bind it tightly until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
On setting broken ribs, know that the ribs are fractured in the thick areas near the back and at their front ends, which are subject to contusion because they are cartilaginous. Knowledge of this is not hidden to the touch when examined with the fingers.
To set it, the fracture should be aligned by hand in a way that the shape becomes as it should be, then bandage and immobilize the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If the rib fracture is inclined inward, then the patient will experience severe pain.
And it perforates like the perforation that occurs to one with a spike because the bone perforates the membrane, and shortness of breath, coughing, and spitting up much blood is also presented, which is difficult to treat. The ancients devised many strategies for this condition.
Some say that the patient's diet should include foods that produce gas to inflate the abdomen and expand it, pushing the fracture outward. However, we dislike this as it may lead to the occurrence of a hot swelling if it has not already happened; if it has occurred, it will exacerbate and confirm it.
Some say that a leech should be placed on the area and then suctioned strongly, which is similar to the method used, except there is concern that the leech may draw too much from the weakened area. Some suggest that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
It becomes a pad between the ribs until it fills, making the bandage even when wrapped around. Then treat the patient with the treatment for spikes, whether through diet or medication. If the patient is severely exhausted and cannot endure it. If the bone perforates the membrane in an injurious manner and we are concerned for the patient, then we should incise the area and expose the broken rib. Then place beneath it the device that protects the peritoneum, which has been previously described, cut the bone gently, and remove it.
Then we sew the edges of the wound together if it is large and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears during this time, act quickly, soak lint in rose oil and apply to the area, and also treat the patient internally with what soothes the swelling.
He should lie on the side that makes him sleep better until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On the Treatment of Backbone and Neck Fractures.
As for the neck bones, if they are fractured, it rarely happens. What usually occurs is contusion, and this is also the case with the vertebrae of the back. If this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not. Then look, if you see that his hands have become weak, numb, and lifeless, and he is unable to move them, stretch them, or clench them, and if you prick them with a needle he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that he will not recover in most cases, and he is doomed.
And if he can move them and feels the pricking and poking, then know that the spinal cord is intact and the patient will recover with treatment. If the backbone suffers a similar fracture and you want to determine if he will also recover.
Then look at his legs. If you see that they have become weak and the same thing that happened to the hands has occurred, and when he lies on his back, gas and feces are released involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine is released involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and wishes to urinate but cannot do so, then know that he is doomed, so do not bother with his treatment. And if none of these symptoms occur, the matter is lighter; treating what happens from this is to aim to soothe the hot swelling.
That you place on the affected vertebra rose oil alone or with roasted egg yolks. Apply that three times a day until the hot swelling subsides, then apply one of the strengthening, drying poultices to the area and bind it tightly with a bandage and order him rest and stillness.
He should only sleep on the side that does not cause him pain until he heals. If a fragment of bone or something that has become dislodged occurs due to the blow, it is necessary to cut the skin and extract that bone. Then, if the wound is large, sew together the edges of the wound, then treat it with unifying ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
Then, with the other hand, align the broken bone as much as possible and to the extent that alignment can be achieved. Then apply the dressing and splint if necessary, and bind it tightly.
If you feel a broken fragment in it, make an incision over it and extract it, then treat the wound as described until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of hip fractures.
Rarely are the sacral bones fractured, but if they are fractured, it is only that they crumble at their edges and split lengthwise and lean inward, and the patient suffers pain in the area and swelling and numbness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Set it by passing your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at its edges, then adjust that fracture according to what seems right to you for leveling until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has leaned inward, then lay the patient on their stomach so that you can set the fracture. Once you have leveled it, apply a dressing, then place a splint made of wood or leather over it and bind it tightly.
Do not fear the displacement of the fracture or the shifting of the splint. Fill in the concavities of the sides to ensure the binding is even. Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side. If swelling with heat occurs, address it accordingly.
Stop extending and setting it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply what is necessary to alleviate it as previously mentioned, then return to setting and binding it properly. If fragments or crumbling at the edges of the bone are present, they should not be removed.
It should not be touched but leveled from the outside as we said, and its binding should be left until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting the fracture of the arm.
The upper arm is what is between the elbow and the shoulder top. If it fractures, set it in one of two ways: One way is to take a smooth, moderately thick curved stick in this form:.
And tie two straps at its ends, then hang it from a high place. The patient sits on a chair and then throw his broken arm over the stick until his armpit is attached to the middle of the curvature of the stick. Then hang something heavy above it.
Or a servant extends it downward, then the doctor aligns the fracture with his hands until he sets it properly. The other method is for the patient to lie on his back and have his hand suspended from his neck with a strap.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, each pulling in their direction. If you want the extension to be stronger, tie a strap under the fracture and another above it, and have each servant pull them in their respective directions.
If the fracture is close to the shoulder tip, it should be that the middle of the strap is under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, the straps should be in that position and on the elbow itself.
Then align the fracture gently without force until the fracture is set properly and unites well. Then bind it if there is no hot swelling. If there is hot swelling, leave binding it until the seventh day and apply a clean woolen cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then you should bind it. The method of binding is to place a bandage on the fracture, then put a wrap of new cloth over the bandage, then gather the arm to the body itself and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And place the cloth and binding on the arm and shoulder so that the arm serves as a substitute for splints if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing changes in the broken bone, but if you fear that something might change because of that, then use splints.
It involves placing on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and there should be the width of a finger between each splint. The length of the splints should correspond to the fracture, with an additional three fingers' width on each side.
Then bind the splints with the binding I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
It is that your binding at the site of the fracture should be tighter.
The binding should be less tight as you move away from the fracture. If you see that placing the splints and binding as we have described at the time of setting the limb is appropriate, then do so.
And if you fear a hot swelling, then leave the binding and splints until the seventh day as we have said. Then examine the bandage every three days to ensure that there is no itching or puffiness in the area, or that nutrition does not fail to reach the limb due to excessive tightness. Adjust everything accordingly as mentioned.
If you are confident that none of this will happen, do not loosen the bandage for many days. The patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a padded, moderate-sized cushion filled with wool should be placed under the armpit.
And examine at all times, whether night or day, to prevent the shape of the broken limb from collapsing or the ligament from loosening; correct all of this with your effort, and let the patient's diet be as we mentioned before, that the food should be gentle initially until the bone begins to strengthen.
It is advisable to make his diet more substantial, for it is usual for the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days. At that point, the cast should be removed, he may use the bath, and be treated with ointments suitable for this purpose. If the fracture is severe and inflamed.
Do not remove the bandage until fifty days or two months have passed.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of an arm fracture.
The arm is composed of two bones, known as the ulna. One is smaller and is next to the thumb, and the other is larger and is positioned below the smaller one from beneath. The larger ulna may break alone, or the smaller one alone, or both may break together.
When the upper smaller ulna is fractured, its setting is easier and healing is faster. When the lower ulna is fractured, the fracture is worse and healing is more difficult. The worst case is when both bones are broken together. If the bone that is fractured is the upper smaller ulna.
It is advisable for the doctor, when setting it, to make the extension slight and gently until it is aligned. If the larger ulna is the one fractured, it is advisable to make the extension stronger. If both ulnas are broken, it is advisable to make the extension much stronger.
It should be that the shape of the hand, when setting and stretching it, is extended on a pillow, and the thumb of the hand is upward higher than all the fingers, and the little finger is lower than the other fingers, and the patient is sitting cross-legged.
And let the pillow be at his height so that the patient does not undergo difficulty.
Then, a servant stretches the arm from below, either by hand or with a band, and another servant stretches it from above in the same manner. Then, the doctor aligns the bone until he restores it to its best possible form. If there are shards in the broken bone, they should be removed.
Place each shard in its position to the best of your ability. If a shard appears detached and is piercing the skin, and you have no hope of setting it, make an incision over it and extract it as described previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have dedicated a separate section for it.
Take the treatment for that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of setting, apply a cloth dipped in kerotiy, which is made with rose oil and white wax. Let it be of moderate thickness between thick and thin, and bind the cloth gently until the swelling subsides.
Remove the kerotiy and apply a dressing prepared from mill powder mixed with egg white. Then, apply the bandages, and let the bandage placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger. Know that the number of arm bandages is usually six.
If the fracture is in one molar or in both molars, then make your binding stronger and tighter at the site of the fracture. The more you extend the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding slightly looser as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
Let the bandages that are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not very hard. Let the thread with which it is tied be of a special kind of cotton, medium between thinness and thickness as we have described, and examine the limb and ligament after days.
If something that needs to be corrected occurs, such as an itch appearing in the limb, it should be soaked in warm water until the itch subsides and the limb is left unbound for a night to rest, then the binding is resumed.
If the binding has loosened or the bone has moved, correct all of that to the best of your ability. Also, if the nourishment is unable to reach the limb due to excessive binding, you should loosen it slightly and leave it for days until the nourishment can flow, then bind it again.
If nothing mentioned has occurred to the patient, it should not be loosened until after twenty days or so. Then, suspend the patient's arm to his neck and let his arm be in a balanced position, and guard against agitated movements, and have him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will heal in thirty days or thirty-two, and it may even heal in twenty-eight days, all of which depends on the conditions of the humors and the strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of the fracture of the hand and the fingers.
The palm's crease and the smooth parts of the fingers rarely fracture, but they often suffer contusions. Whenever a fracture or contusion occurs in the palm.
The patient should sit cross-legged with a chair in front of him levelled.
Then he places his hand on it stretched out, then the servant extends the broken bone and the doctor straightens it until it unites well. At that point, the bandage and pressure should be applied if no hot swelling occurs, then a splint should be placed over it according to the area.
I have wrapped it in a soft cloth. If the fracture is downward toward the palm, make a ball-like shape from the cloth and ask the patient to grasp it with their fractured hand, then secure it with a long piece of cloth.
Let the splint be made of leather with flexibility so that it conforms to all the bends of the palm and secure it as necessary. If the fracture is outward, a splint should be placed from above and another from below in the palm so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then secure the binding as the hand is rotated and interlace between the fingers with a strap. If the fracture affects one of the finger joints, if it is the thumb, straighten it as necessary then bind it with the palm. If you wish, make a small upright splint for it to support the fracture.
And it should not be moved. If the fracture is for other fingers such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, they should be straightened and bound to the adjacent healthy finger or all can be bound together in alignment, which is better, or a small upright splint can be placed on them.
As we said about the thumb, examine during and after setting for any hot swelling and address it as necessary if anything of this sort occurs, as has been described repeatedly.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the setting of a thigh fracture.
The thigh bone often breaks and can be detected by sensation because it shifts forward and backward. Setting it involves placing a bandage above the fracture and another below it, with the patient lying face down.
Then, each servant should stretch the bandage to his side evenly. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. However, if it is close to the base of the thigh, it is advisable to use soft bandages, either of wool or similar material.
At the base of the thigh towards the groin, the stretching should be upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee so that the stretching is downwards. Then, the doctor should align the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural form.
The bone will heal well. At this point, it is advisable to maintain the bandaging and binding unless the limb develops a hot swelling. If such swelling occurs, leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to treating it.
As for binding it, you should wrap a broad and sturdy turban around the fracture two or three times, leaving some excess. Then, bend the leg so that the heel is near the base of the shin, and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, with the ends of the thread above on both sides.
Then, wrap the remaining part of the turban around the leg and thigh. Place bandages on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and use one bandage for the shin bone. Fill the gap between the thigh and the leg with soft cloths to even out the binding.
Then begin with strong binding from the center at the site of the fracture, three or four turns, and the further you go from the site of the fracture with the bandage, let your binding be less tight, softer, and looser.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg, tie it with the plasters above, then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel of the foot, also tighten them with the ends of the plasters on the other side.
So that the binding does not move from its place, then leave it tightened as long as there is no sign of pain, swelling, or inflammation in the member, if any of these occur, immediately loosen it and correct whatever has happened as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter in the bone that protrudes, it should be leveled if possible; otherwise, make an incision over it and extract it, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals. This fracture of the thigh can sometimes be treated without involving the leg with plasters.
As we mentioned regarding the forearm and arm, except that bracing it this way does not cause the patient to limp if you brace it alone without adding the leg. However, the owner will inevitably limp.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more, or a little less, all of which depends on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the patella.
The patella rarely fractures but is often subjected to contusions. If it does fracture, it either splits or shatters into pieces, and this can occur with or without a laceration.
All of this is determined by touch, and it is set by aligning the fragmented parts with the fingers until they come together and unite as much as possible with care, precision, and gentleness. Then, dress the area with a bandage and apply a circular splint if necessary.
Tighten above appropriately according to that, then attend to all the conditions we described in other fractures such as hot swelling and the like by addressing each symptom with what is suitable for it until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the setting of a leg fracture.
The leg has two bones, one thick called the tibia and the other thin called the fibula. Both are subject to various types of fractures similar to those of the arm bones. Therefore, setting it is the same as setting an arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg will turn in all directions. If the thinner bone is fractured, the leg will turn forward. If the thicker bone is fractured, especially below, this will not be unknown to you.
Use traction and alignment, and bind the splints accordingly. However, if the leg fracture is severe with many fragments, the traction should be less forceful, and you should be more gentle in setting it.
And in the leg there is an additional task compared to the arm, and that is when you have aligned the splints and completed all your work, take two fasiqiyat of pine wood which are used for flattening the gaps between the boards or they can be made from palm fronds or similar.
Choose those that are slightly thick and not thin, and let their length be the length of the leg from the knee downwards, then wrap a cloth around each one twice along its length and place one on the leg and the other on the other side.
And let it extend from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the two fasiqiyat at three points, the ends and the middle, for with this restraint, the leg will not tilt to the right or left and will be set properly. Sometimes a wooden trough of the length of the leg can also be used.
It is placed there to protect it from movement, and you should do this more especially in cases of fractures accompanied by a specific wound. Then, examine the leg every day and pay close attention to any swelling or inflammation or anything else. Whenever any of that occurs, address it appropriately until it heals. The bone of the leg heals in thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the fractures of the leg bones and fingers.
As for the heel, it is rarely fractured at all. As for the bones of the foot, they can be fractured, and fingers are also seldom fractured; rather, they are more often bruised.
If a man's bones are broken and you see those bones nearly touching each other, let the patient stand with his foot on the ground like one walking. Then place your foot on what has risen of those bones and press them down and level them.
Until they return to their places, then take the bandage and pressure from above and place under the sole of the foot a small board that has two flat ends, then tightly bind the foot after wrapping it with cloths and all else it needs.
If three or four days have passed, release the binding as you will find the bones leveled whether they were broken or dislocated. The procedure is as seen. As for if some of the toes are broken, set and align them according to what has been described to you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken toe along its length, and let it be slightly wider than the toe. Then place under the foot the board I described to you and bind it tightly. If two or three or more of the bones are broken.
Place a splint on each toe wrapped in a soft cloth, and bind the board at the bottom of the foot. Let each end of the board extend slightly beyond the sole of the foot to secure it properly.
You should not forget to attend to all that we have mentioned regarding the various fractures and their symptoms, and address each symptom as appropriate.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubic bone and the man's penis.
When the woman's vagina is torn, sit her down and make her slightly bend towards her back little by little and let her hold on from behind. Then the midwife should stuff her vagina with cotton until it fills it and becomes like a ball inside her vagina.
Then the woman is shaken and her body is lifted slightly, little by little. The cotton will come out until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vagina, and the bone fracture returns to its place. Then she should have padding on her back. Whenever she wants to urinate, she gently removes the cotton to urinate.
She should put it back in the way it was first inserted, then return to her initial padding. She does this for seven days or so until it heals. If you want, you can take a sheep's bladder, attach a reed tube to its mouth, and insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then blow into the tube forcefully so that the bladder inflates inside the vagina, and the fracture returns to its place. Then stuff it with cotton and keep it for several days as described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, it should be treated similarly for healing.
And its alignment is as we described for the hip bone. It should not be hidden from you the correctness in these rare fractures that seldom occur, especially for those who have some experience and understand this book well, for the fracture itself will guide you in most cases.
Follow the right method for setting and binding it, understand this. As for a fractured penis, take the neck of a goose, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage wrap over it and bind it tightly. Leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the setting of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
He who has suffered a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the femur or the humerus or the like, should immediately proceed to set it at once if the conditions for setting assist him, as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding, you should immediately cauterize it by applying powdered zinc if nothing else is available. Then proceed to set the fracture on the same day and do not delay it.
If a hot swelling does not occur, you may proceed with setting it. If a hot swelling does occur, postpone setting the fracture until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides. Do not touch it at all on the third and fourth days as it may result in adverse effects.
If the broken bone protrudes and is exposed on the skin, you should attempt to gently replace and align it with your hands using slight traction. If you are unable to replace and align it with your hands, use this instrument, which is made of iron and measures about seven or eight finger-widths in length. And its width should be according to the size of the wound.
For this reason, the physician should prepare three or four of them, according to what is needed for treatment in each type of fracture. They should be rounded and have a slight thickness so that they do not bend when pressed upon during use.
They should have sharp ends with a slight curvature at one end. The top should be thicker, and from the middle downwards, it should become very thin. This is its shape:.
It is called in Greek "pirm" which means a small lever, and it should be such that its curved sharp end reaches the protruding bone and you push with it once until when the bone returns and becomes somewhat aligned then stop to align the ends of the fracture with each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, you should cut that bone's end until the instrument can grip it. If you are unable to realign the bone as described at all, then cut it using one of the cutting methods we have mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the saws however you can, then scrape off what remains of the roughness and thin scales on the bone. If after realigning the bone, the patient feels severe painful discomfort, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to realign it to its natural position, do so, for you greatly benefit the patient. Once you have completed setting the bone, soak a cloth in a black constricting potion, especially if you are in summer, and do not apply any fat or ointment to the wound.
To prevent rot and corruption from occurring, then use bandages after you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole with scissors in the bandage that fits the size of the wound.
Beware with utmost caution not to bind the wound tightly along with the fracture, for ignorant physicians have often done this and caused either death or gangrene or infection in their patients. Your binding should be gentle and loose, different from how you would bind other fractures.
If the wound is poor or it is a large wound and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described and he feels painful in the area disturbingly, then you should not put on him bandages, but make rolls from hard cloths in place of the bandages and bind him with them.
If it is after a day or two and you see that the wound has begun to produce pus, then remove the cloth you placed on it with the potion. Then use bandaging and ointments which are customary for us to treat wounds with, such as the four-part ointment and the like, and you should loosen the binding.
You should examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it heals. It is advisable to elevate the limb so that the pus drains downward easily. If many days pass and the wound does not close nor does the pus cease flowing from it, then know that there are small splinters of bone present.
You should probe the wound with aæŽ¢é’ˆã€‚é‚£äº›å¯ä»¥è½»æ˜“ç§»é™¤çš„ç¢Žç‰‡å°±å°†å…¶å–å‡ºï¼Œè€Œå¯¹äºŽé‚£äº›éš¾ä»¥ç§»é™¤ä¸”å¼•èµ·ç–¼ç—›å¹¶åˆºæ¿€è‚¢ä½“çš„ç¢Žç‰‡ï¼Œåˆ™åº”è®¾æ³•åˆ‡å‰²å’Œç§»é™¤å®ƒä»¬ã€‚
If a wound is exposed to colds or bites or any other kind of corruption and putrefaction, it should be treated with a treatment similar to that previously described in its section.
And it is important that you pay attention to what I say and keep it in mind: if a large bone, such as the thigh or the arm bone, or other similar large limbs are fractured, do not attempt to pull it or extract it. For death often results from that. So leave it until it putrefies; perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see an opportunity for treatment; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the swelling that occurs after some fractures.
This swelling often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially near the joints, and it can distort the appearance of the limb and may prevent the limb from functioning normally.
If the swelling is soft, use medicines that constrict, such as frankincense, mastic, aloes, colocynth, and similar substances. Take some or all of these, and mix them with a constricting drink or with egg white or vinegar.
Apply it to the swelling and bind it tightly. Leave the binding for many days, then remove it and reapply another until the swelling goes away, if God wills, or bind a sheet of lead securely over it.
For lead has the property of removing all that is corrupted in the organs, if the knot has hardened and become severe and necessity has driven to its removal then cut into it from above and cut off the excess that arises or strip it with some knives until it is removed and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the treatment of fractures when they have healed and the limb remains weak, not as it was originally.
If a bone fracture has healed and the limb remains weak and thin, this is due to many reasons. One reason is either due to the frequent loosening and improper tightening of bandages, or due to overly tight bandages that prevent nutrients from reaching the limb, or due to excessive and inappropriate bleeding, or due to performing excessive movements at inopportune times, or due to a lack of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body until the blood increases, to use baths, and to bring joy and happiness to him, and so on. Then apply pitch to the limb so that the pitch attracts abundant nutrition or continue to bleed it with lukewarm water. Until the nutrients flow through it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of fractured bones that have healed crookedly and prevented proper function.
And when a deformity or protrusion occurs in a bone that has been set and healed, or becomes complicated, the appearance of the limb is marred. Unless the limb does not prevent its natural function, it is not appropriate to accept the claim of one who asserts that the limb should be broken from the start. Many ignorant physicians and bone setters in our country have often done this.
This action is highly condemnable and leads to great danger, the least of which is disability. However, if the deformity and complexity are soft, it should be treated with water in which relaxing herbs have been boiled, such as willow leaves and roots, marjoram, and the like.
It should be bandaged with relaxing dressings such as well-made diachylon, or the sap of the willow root can be taken and mixed with chicken fat and cerate, then bandage it with that. Alternatively, take the juicy figs and grind them with pigeon droppings and similar medications known as dispersers of adhesion.
And the complexity may be resolved by constant, gentle massage with the hands and using movement of the limb in all directions at all times. If the deformity has persisted, become severe, and hardened, and there is a necessity for its treatment with iron.
It should be cut above, and the connection of the bone should be released, and any remaining complexity or bone should be carefully removed with delicate cuts. This should be done with great care and attention. Then, treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
Dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its position, which hinders movement, spoils the appearance of the organ, and causes severe pain to the patient. Whenever someone suffers a dislocation, it should be immediately reduced.
It should not be delayed at all, for if it is postponed, the area will swell and make reducing the dislocation difficult. Therefore, it should not be delayed or moved or stretched while it is swollen, as this often causes spasms and painful discomfort to the patient.
However, if this occurs, one should immediately begin treating the patient. Wait until the swelling subsides somewhat, then foment the area with hot water and oil, then gently reduce it. Each part should be treated as described in its respective section.
I have also arranged the sections on dislocation in accordance with what was previously outlined for fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The jaws rarely dislocate except in rare cases, and their dislocation can occur in two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their positions and somewhat relaxed, or they are completely and fully dislocated until they become relaxed. Until they move towards the chest, causing the patient's saliva to drool and unable to hold it back, and unable to close their jaw, with their tongue stumbling while speaking. If the dislocation is slight, it often returns on its own with minimal intervention.
And if the dislocation is complete, it should be immediately reduced without delay. This is done by having a servant hold the patient's head while the doctor inserts his thumb into the base of the jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side.
Or he inserts both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and with the rest of his fingers outside, he aligns them and instructs the patient to relax his jaw and let it go in every direction, and the doctor aligns the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to reduce it, especially if both jaws are dislocated, use a compress with hot water and oil until their reduction becomes easy; do not delay their reduction at all as we have said. Once they return to their proper place and the patient's mouth closes without loosening, then apply bandages treated with wax and oil on them.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on his back with his head restrained between two pillows so that he does not move it to the right or left, and he should avoid chewing anything but instead consume liquid, soft food.
Once the pain subsides and the jaw is set, he may eat what he pleases. This should be done gently without straining to open his mouth while eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is fully set and healed.
If it is difficult to reduce the dislocated jaws when they have been out of place for some time and do not return to their proper positions, fevers and constant headaches often occur, and the patient may suffer from diarrhea or vomit bile. If you observe these symptoms, know that it has become corrupted.
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the Treatment of Dislocations of the Lower Jaw and the Shoulder Joint.
As for the lower jaw, it does not dislocate on the inner side due to its connection to the chest, but it can dislocate outward, which can be detected by touch. To set it, the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the area with your palm firmly, and it will return to its place. Then apply bandages and bindings and tighten them. As for the part that is next to the shoulder and connected to it, it rarely dislocates. If it ever does dislocate, it should be reduced and aligned as we have described, and do whatever seems appropriate to you.
Then apply bandages, bindings, and tighteners. Instruct the patient to remain still and at rest until healing occurs. With this same treatment, you can also reduce a dislocated shoulder that has moved from its place.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the Reduction of Shoulder Dislocation.
Know that the shoulder can dislocate in three ways: one is when it dislocates towards the armpit downward, the second is when it dislocates toward the chest, and sometimes it may dislocate upward from the shoulder, though this is rare.
It does not dislocate backward due to the position of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate forward due to the presence of the nerves. It most often dislocates and moves downward toward the armpit, especially in those who have less flesh, as it can exit and enter quickly in such individuals.
And as for those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, meaning that it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty. Sometimes some people suffer a blow or fall, and their shoulder swells with hot swelling, and they presume that it has become dislocated, so you should examine this to ascertain its truth.
Then proceed with its treatment. The jaw will be identified if it moves downward toward the armpit. If you align the dislocated shoulder with the healthy one, you will find a noticeable difference between them, and you will find the head of the shoulder socket out of place. When touched under the armpit, the head of the shoulder will feel like an egg.
The patient is unable to raise his hand to his ear or move it in all movements. Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find that obvious upon touch, not hidden.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if it is fresh or if the patient is a child. To reduce it, have an assistant raise the patient's arm above their head. Then place your thumbs under the armpit and forcefully lift the joint back to its original position while the assistant raises and extends the arm upwards.
Then lower it down, and it will return quickly. If it does not return as mentioned and the dislocation has been ongoing for many days, the patient should bathe in hot water and use laxatives that relax and soften, such as boiling the root of fenugreek, sesame, and camomile in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool, which is moderately balanced between softness and firmness, is placed under their armpit. The doctor then places his foot on the ball and holds the patient's hand in his hand, extending it downward.
He moves the ball with his leg, then he returns to his stance. If you wish, return him this way: have a taller person present and stop him on one side of the flank, and let his shoulder enter under the armpit of the patient and raise his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. Another servant pulls the patient's hand to his lower abdomen. If the patient is light, it is advisable to hang something else on him to increase his weight, for then the joint will return immediately, and it can also be done in another way.
It involves fixing a long stick in the ground, with its head round like a pestle, neither too thick nor too thin. Then it is placed under the armpit of the patient after placing a soft cloth on top of the stick, and the patient stands along the length of the stick.
Then extend his hand downward from the other side and also pull his body strongly from the other side, then the joint will return to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we mentioned, use this treatment: take a stick about two cubits long. Its width should be about four fingers and its thickness about two fingers, with a round head to facilitate its entry deeply into the armpit in this manner:.
Then tie soft cloths on the round head to prevent the stick from harming the patient. Then place it under the armpit, extend the entire hand and arm along the stick downward, and secure the stick to the upper arm, forearm, and end of the hand.
Then place the arm on a bar, hold it firmly and extend the hand downward, leaving the rest of the body suspended from the other side, then the joint will return immediately. Once it has been returned by any possible means, it is advisable to place a moderately sized ball of wool under the armpit.
Then the dressing prepared from mill dust with frankincense and egg white is applied to the entire shoulder as it extends from above, then the ball is tightly secured under the armpit and bound with a bandage over the dressing, and his hand is suspended to his neck.
He should not move his hand for seven days, and it is best to make the patient's diet light so that the limb strengthens, which will facilitate its recovery; after seven or five days, the bandage is removed and he is trained through movement, if it remains stable and does not loosen, then it has healed.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture exposure or another condition, it is advisable to use cauterization with three saffron applications as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If you have done all of this and removed the bandage after seven days but the joint has not stabilized.
And if you have reapplied the dressing and binding multiple times but it has not stabilized, and it has fallen and loosened, and you cannot raise it upwards, know that the nerve at the top of the shoulder has been severed, stretched, or loosened. Know then that the joint will never stabilize in its place.
As for the jaw that is displaced towards the chest and breast and to the back, its reduction is done by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns. All binding and treatment are applied until it heals. If stiffness occurs in the joint and movement slows down after healing.
The patient should use the bath frequently until the stiffness is softened and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of dislocated elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns to its place with difficulty. It can dislocate in all directions, particularly forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is evident both to the sight and touch, regardless of the form of dislocation.
When you compare the dislocated elbow to the correct one, it will be clearly evident by the deformation of the joint. The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder be touched with the hand. You should immediately reduce the dislocation at once before swelling occurs.
If swelling does occur, reducing the dislocation will be difficult and it may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backward. This is the worst kind of dislocation, causing the most pain, and often leads to severe complications including death.
To reduce it if it can be returned, a servant should extend his arm fully and grasp it with both hands while the doctor places his hands above and below the elbow. The doctor should push the joint with the thumbs of both hands or the base of his palms until it returns to its proper place.
If the dislocation is forward, it can be reduced by bending the arm suddenly until the base of the hand strikes the adjacent shoulder. If the dislocation does not respond to this, then a very strong extension should be used, which requires two servants to pull the arm.
Two servants should also hold the patient to prevent movement during the extension. Then, the arm should be rotated in all directions after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide bandage around it. When the doctor handles the joint with his hands, he should smear them with oil to facilitate theæ»‘åŠ¨å…³èŠ‚ã€‚
Then push the joint strongly until it returns, and after its return, it is necessary to apply a bandage that includes contraction and drying with egg white, and bind it tightly, and suspend the arm from the patient's neck for days.
Then release it, if the joint is stable in its place, remove the bandage and leave it. If you see that the joint has not become strong enough, reapply the bandage and binding and leave it for more days until it strengthens, then release it. If stiffness occurs after returning the joint and releasing it.
If there is difficulty in movement, then use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and pulling until it becomes flexible, or place a thick sheepskin-like device on the joint, then bind it and leave it for a day and night, then remove it and enter him into the bath. When he sweats, rub the joint moderately.
Then apply the device to him again, a second and third time, along with entering the bath until it becomes flexible. If you want to put fresh, light cow's udder with butter on it and bind it tightly, do so several times, for it will become flexible and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Dislocating the Wrist.
The wrist of the hand often gets dislocated and its reduction is easier compared to other joints. However, it should be reduced immediately when it gets dislocated, before the area swells or a hot swelling occurs.
You should place the patient's wrist on a board, and the servant extends his hand while the doctor places his palm on the protrusion of the joint and pushes it until it returns, but he must check if the dislocation has moved to the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the outer side of his hand on the board during extension and flexion. If the dislocation is protruding to the outer side of the hand, let him place the inner side of his hand on the board so that the doctor's hand falls on the exact protrusion of the joint; if it returns immediately, otherwise secure it with a bandage that reduces swelling.
Leave it without repeating the procedure, for it cannot tolerate it and you will not be able to realign it after a few days, unless the joint remains misaligned, which does not harm the patient except that the hand may become lax and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been cut or damaged, and there is no remedy except to cauterize it, which may be helpful or not. Once the wrist is realigned, apply the bandage we described, then secure it and leave it for five days.
Then it is unbound and the hand is exercised. If its movement is difficult or some stiffness appears, soften it with warm water and rubbing repeatedly until it becomes supple.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Treatment of Finger Dislocations.
Fingers can dislocate in any direction. When a finger dislocates to the outer or inner side of the palm, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then bandage the tip of the finger and suspend it toward the direction it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and stretch it until it straightens on its own on that day. When night falls, bind it as described; continue to untie it during the day, exercise it with movement, and tie it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
And do the same if it turns inward toward the hand, binding it towards the same direction and perform the initial treatment until it heals. Do the same if it turns in any other directions.
Chapter Thirty.
On the treatment of dislocated vertebrae.
When a vertebra from the vertebrae of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or when many vertebrae have moved from their places, there is no treatment because death quickly comes to the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excrement comes out without intention; he cannot hold it back, and often some of his limbs become weak, either his legs or his arms or one of them. As for if a single vertebra moves from its place, it often returns to its position.
Its displacement can occur in four directions. If it moves backward, it is called a hump. As for its treatment, if the hump occurred during adolescence, there is no treatment and it will not heal at all. However, if it resulted from a fall or blow or something similar,
The earlier authorities have described various treatments in great detail, much of which is not very beneficial. I have condensed what they said so that a little of my explanation suffices instead of the lengthy descriptions they provided. I have also illustrated the instrument differently from how they explained and depicted it.
I say that the hump which occurs in front of the chest cannot be treated or cured. The same applies to those on both sides. Only the one that occurs on the back can be treated as I will describe, and that is by laying the patient face down.
On a flat bench near a wall, spread a moist cloth underneath to avoid harming his chest. Then place a vertical wooden beam embedded in a hole in the ground at one end of the bench towards his head and another wooden beam towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the beam, which should not be firmly fixed in the hole, and another servant holds the other one in that manner. Then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, tight cloth, and extend the end of the cloth to the beam at his head and tie it there.
Then bind another cloth over his hips, above his knees, and at his ankles. Gather all the bindings together and tie them to the other beam at his feet. Then each servant pulls the beam in their direction with a rope, and the beams do not move from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not firmly fixed, as we said. The doctor places his hands on the hump with force until it returns, or he puts a board on it and leans on the board with his legs until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board that is about three arms' length long.
And dig into the wall, which we said should be near the patient, a place where one end of the board can fit. Then place the middle of the board on the hump and have the doctor put his feet on the other end and pull tightly until it presses on the hump.
