And return it to its place, and if you wish to make it with the hand-spun screw, which is that you plant in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop two pieces of wood whose length is an arm's length, and at his feet at the other end of the shop also two pieces of wood.
And let there be a cubit between each piece of wood, and make a hole in each piece of wood through which the screw runs, and secure all the wood firmly in the ground so that it does not move at all, and insert a round rod, which is the screw where the rope is wound into the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end, there is a hole in which a rod of a cubit length is firmly tied where it is wound, and the same for the other two pieces of wood. Then tie the rope that you have tightened on the patient's chest to the screw at his head and the ropes that you have tightened on his legs to the screw at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, turning it by hand with the wrench that turns the screw, and the doctor adjusts the hump as previously described. This is the image of the screw, the operating table, and the patient:
Then after the vertebra returns and the area is even, it is advisable to apply a bandage dried with egg white, then padding. Then place on top of the bandage a splint made of a board that is three fingers wide or so, and its length should be enough to cover the area of the hump.
And on some of the correct beads, then tie it with the appropriate rope, and the patient should use a mild diet until he recovers. If there remains some protrusion in the area at the end of recovery, it is advisable to use medicines that relax and soften, along with using the board we described for a long time.
And for that, a sheet of lead may be used. Sometimes a protrusion appears at the end of the spinal vertebrae and it might be mistaken for dislocation; in fact, this is an extra bone that has grown, so do not treat it with this method as death may occur.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the Treatment of Dislocated Hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint only experience dislocation, and do not suffer from the slight shifting and indentation that other joints may endure. The hip joint can be dislocated in four ways.
That is, it can dislocate inward, outward, forward, and backward. It most often dislocates inward, rarely forward or backward. The sign of its dislocation inward is that if you align the healthy leg with the affected one, the healthy leg will appear longer.
The knee will protrude more than normal, and the afflicted person will not be able to bend their leg at the arvil. The area adjacent to the arvil will show aæ˜Žæ˜¾å‡¹é™·ï¼Œå› ä¸ºè‚¡éª¨å¤´å·²ç»ç§»è‡³é‚£é‡Œã€‚
The signs of outward dislocation are the opposite of these symptoms. For forward dislocation, he will fully extend his leg, but he cannot bend it without pain in the knee. If he attempts to walk, he will not be able to move forward.
He may retain urine, and his ankle will droop. When walking, he will step on his heel. For backward dislocation, he cannot extend the knee and cannot bend it before bending the ankle. His leg will also be shorter than the other one.
And the thigh is relaxed, and the head of the femur at the site of the hip is evident. As for reducing types of this dislocation, you should look; if the dislocation is old, it has become chronic with its owner and he did not attempt to reduce it and remained in his condition, then there is absolutely no treatment.
You should not attempt to treat it. As for the one whose dislocation is recent, and whatever type it may be of the four aspects of dislocation, you should immediately twist the joint, extend it inward and outward, and move it right and left, perhaps.
If it returns without needing other treatments, then if it does not return this way, you should prepare a strong assistant who extends his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the knee, and another assistant extending it from above.
By inserting his hands under the armpits, then tightening a soft bandage at the base of the thigh, and another third assistant holding the end of the bandage, extending it either from the front, from the side of the ribs to the side of the clavicle, or from behind towards the back.
Their extension should be done simultaneously so that the patient is lifted off the ground and remains suspended. This type of extension is common to all four types. If the dislocation returns as we said, otherwise each type requires the specific treatment I mentioned.
As for its specific reduction if the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on his healthy side, then a bandage should be placed at the base of the thigh between the head of the femur and the area below the ribs.
Then extend the bandage from the armpit area upwards to the upper body towards the clavicle. Then another strong servant should support with his arms and hug the thick part of the affected thigh and pull out strongly, for it will return to its place.
This type is easier than other types of treatment used to return this organ. If it is difficult for you and does not respond at all to this type of treatment, it is advisable to bind both legs of the patient with a strong flexible bandage around the ankles and knees.
There should be a distance of four fingers between each one, and the affected leg should be extended two fingers more than the other. Then suspend the patient by the head from a beam in the house, with a distance of two cubits from the ground.
Then order a strong servant to hug the thigh and another servant to hold the patient. The other servant who is hugging the thigh should push with force, for the joint will return to its place quickly. As for its specific reduction if the jaw is displaced outward.
The patient should lie on the bench as described for the hunchback case, and the bandage should be tied specifically to his affected leg and chest. Then place two beams, one at his feet and the other at his head, then add another beam in the middle of the bench, secured very tightly.
It should be wrapped with wet cloths to avoid harming the patient, so that the board is between his thighs to prevent it from being pulled down during extension. Then each servant extends in his direction, and the doctor aligns the hip with his hands, if it responds to being returned.
Otherwise, place the board on it and press it as we mentioned in hump treatment alike, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side, and if the dislocation is forward, you should extend the patient's leg once while he is described exactly on the bench.
The doctor places the palm of his right hand on the diseased joint, then presses it with the other hand; thus, he extends the pressure downward towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be extended downwards.
It should be raised from the ground; it is preferable to place it on something solid. Similarly, if his hip has dislocated outward as we mentioned, he should lie on the bench face down with the bandages tightly secured.
As we said before, pressing with the board should also be used on the place to which the joint has moved. Once the hip joint is fully returned as it should be, its return will not be hidden from you, and you will be able to extend the patient's legs.
When you see them level and the patient can bend and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the joint has returned as it should. At this point, bind the thighs together, secure the dressing, and tightly wrap with a turban so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then untie the bandage and remove the dressing. Compare the leg to the other one; if you find them equal in measure, know that the joint has set, and allow the patient to walk. If you notice any laxity, however, take further action.
Return and dress it, and bind it according to your first binding. Leave it for three days as well, then loosen it. Walk slowly on it for some days until it strengthens well, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the treatment of dislocating the knee.
The knee can dislocate in three directions: inward, outward, and downward, which means backward. It never dislocates forward at all. The sign of its dislocation is when you ask the patient to bring his leg close to his thigh.
If it does not attach to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. To correct all forms of its dislocation, have the patient sit with his legs extended if he is able to do so. Have a servant sit behind him to hold his waist and bend it slightly backward. Then you sit on his thighs.
Lean your back against his front, place his leg between your legs, then hold his knee with both of your hands and interlock your fingers around it. Then grasp the sides of his knee firmly with both of your hands, and have another servant pull his leg until the knee returns to its proper place.
The sign that it has returned is when the leg attaches to the thigh smoothly and without force. Then dress it, attach the leg firmly to the thigh, and bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. Only light walking should be done for some days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it as described, then use strong traction with bandages, which I previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of ankle dislocation.
The ankle may be slightly dislocated or completely dislocated, and its dislocation can either be inward or outward. The sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen and protruding towards the side to which it has dislocated.
As for the treatment of slight dislocation, it is easily reduced by gently stretching with the hands and aligning until it returns. As for treating complete dislocation, the patient should sit down and be held firmly by a strong servant who grasps him from behind in the middle of his back.
Then grasp his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand below the foot at the ankle. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then do it twice more with the left hand. Then stretch it with the left hand and push the front of the foot with the right hand towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as described, then on the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling at the ankle. If it returns in one or two tries and you see the foot is aligned, otherwise repeat the procedure, for it will return to place.
If it does not return as described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and fix a pole in the ground very securely between his thighs, wrapped with cloths so as not to hurt the patient. Then have an assistant hold his thigh firmly.
Then another assistant stretches the leg either with his hands or with a strap tied around the ankle. Each assistant then pulls in opposite directions, while the stake between the patient's thighs prevents his body from being pulled downward during the stretch. The doctor then aligns the dislocation with his hands.
Another servant holds the healthy leg downward, then the dislocation returns quickly. Once the dislocation has returned and you are certain of its proper return, apply the bandage and splint and secure it with tight bindings, and immobilize the foot by binding it downward. You should be cautious of the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
So that the binding is not too tight and injures him, then leave it for two or three days. If the binding becomes loose, tighten it again, then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, do not be assured that the joint will not dislocate again and become ruined, and it will no longer accept treatment. If a hot swelling occurs, you should use what we have previously described in another section of treatment and prolonging until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes.
You should align what has become dislocated with a gentle pull without force, which is not difficult but rather easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints on the top of the foot, the patient should place his foot on a flat surface of the ground.
or on a board while standing as if walking. Then you place your foot on top of the dislocated joint and press it firmly with your foot until it returns and you see that it is aligned and no bump appears in the area.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that covers the entire foot, which has two ends, and bind it tightly for three days. Then release it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are assured it will not recur, if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the types of jaw that may be accompanied by a wound or a fracture or both together.
When any of that occurs and swelling follows its treatment and setting, it is often followed by death.
Therefore, one should not undertake the treatment of such conditions except someone who is skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, patient, not reckless or bold. At the beginning, only use medicines that alleviate hot swellings and leave the patient alone.
Except, O Lord, for what I hope will be safe from damage with the lightness of the disease and where there is some hope in it, then do not delay its treatment at the first moment before a hot swelling occurs.
If the part returns as desired, then use the treatment that alleviates hot swellings and treat the wound with appropriate drying ointments. If the jaw is fractured and there are detached bone splinters, do not delay in extracting them. Follow in this what we have mentioned regarding simple diseases as detailed in their respective sections.
Exert your utmost effort and refrain from taking risky paths as my previous advice to you has indicated. That will preserve your reputation and keep you safe, if God wills.
